
\part{Energy Mechanics}\label{part: energy mech}
\pagenumbering{arabic}
\pagestyle{fancy}

\begin{chapquote}{Leonhard Euler}
    ``Nothing arises in the universe in which one cannot see the sense of some maximum or minimum.''
\end{chapquote}


\chapter*{Energy Mechanics Overview}

We may motivate our study of energy mechanics by asking a simple, yet profound question:
What is the fundamental nature of the universe?

If you lived in the time of Isaac Newton, the answer might be fairly simple - if we know the positions, velocities, and accelerations of objects, you can determine the future of that object with exacting certainty.\endnote{This idea of knowing the future and past given the set of all initial conditions is known as \textit{Laplace's Demon}.} Forces, it might have been said, are the fundamental actors in the universe.

Later, physicists would derive a different way of looking at mechanics -- using the methods of energy. This is what we explore in our study of variational calculus and energy mechanics. At the time, many physicists believed that this reframing was simply another expression of Newtonian physics. In some way, this is true. However, the energy method allows us to peer more deeply into the beating heart of physics to examine the fundamental qualities of nature. 

Examining the proceedings of physics in the 20th century, we see this become increasingly apparent. Mass -- Einstein had shown -- was fundamentally tied to the energy of particles in perhaps the most famous equation of all time, $E^2=m^2c^4+p^2c^2$. Later, as physicists began to develop quantum mechanics, energy, not forces, became the fundamental quantity to measure. The famous Schr\"odinger equation, in fact, uses this energy relation:
$$\hat{\mathcal{H}}\psi=E\psi$$
The fruitfulness of this approach in physics is very apparent. Everything from classical physics to high energy particle physics seems to be governed by energy principles. It is not a great exaggeration to say this framework is instrumental and foundational in our best currently known framework of physics.

The application of energy mechanics has more recently become useful for engineers, who need to understand the dynamics of complex systems. It is in this context that they have found prevalent use in engineering applications as well. To understand the dynamics of complicated control systems or spacecraft, we rely on energy mechanics, rather than Newtonian mechanics, to guide us.

It is with this background that we motivate the study of energy mechanics. This subject gives us the key understanding needed to examine subjects from the smallest to the largest scales of the universe.

\chapter{Historical Background }\label{sec: historical background}

In this chapter, I cover a brief history of the early years of energy mechanics. I will mostly cover the main figures and ideas that are instrumental to our study of energy mechanics. These main figures include Isaac Newton, Pierre de Fermat, and Johann Bernoulli.

The story of these figures is closely intertwined. It is impossible to discuss one figure without discussing the others as well. This section will thus take on a more chronological approach, but background information about some of the important characters is also included. Notably, I include some information about Isaac Newton for his far-reaching impacts in many aspects of mechanics. The story of these figures will culminate in energy mechanics, possibly the most important implication of the studies of these historic figures. The foundations set by these scientists persist in the landscape of modern engineering and physics. 

Euler, Lagrange, Hamilton, and others' later contributions to the subjects will not be discussed here, but rather in \autoref{chap:calculus of variations}, \autoref{chap:lagrangian}, and \autoref{chap:hamiltonian}. While the contributions of Weierstrass, Hilbert, and others are important to the mathematical solidification of the calculus of variations, I have chosen not to cover them in this text because I feel that it does not greatly aid readers in understanding the core of the subject. The book by Goldstine \cite{goldstine_history_1980} has been especially useful in the development of this chapter. I highly recommend reading chapters 1-3 for readers who are interested in learning more about the history of this subject.

The goal of this chapter is not necessarily to derive many mathematical expressions, as most of the methods used were archaic and difficult to interpret for the modern reader. Goldstine has great historical analysis of these mathematics that is beyond the scope of the historical background presented here. Instead, I aim to present the core ideas of energy mechanics, show how it emerged, and why it is useful for solving some types of problems.

\section{Isaac Newton (1643-1727)} 

\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.5\linewidth]{Images/Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton,_1689_(brightened).jpg}
    \caption{Portrait of Sir Isaac Newton}
    \label{fig:Newton}
\end{figure}

The history of any kind of mechanics must include a study of Isaac Newton. Newton is very famous for his description of mechanics based on forces and vectors. However, Newton is also instrumental character in our study of energy mechanics.  In fact, Newton was a founder of Calculus of Variations, solving two of the first problems in the subject, the \textit{minimal resistance problem} and the \gls{brachistochrone} problem, which we will further explore in our study of the calculus of variations. It is on these subjects that we will spend most of our time.

Before we explore his contributions in this field, though, an understanding of his work in force-based mechanics and calculus is needed. This background section draws mostly from \cite{history_isaac_2017}. Newton's work on variational calculus draws from several different sources, including the original \textit{Principia} and \cite{goldstine_fermat_1980}.

\subsection{Newton's Beginnings (1643-1661)}
In a story likely familiar to many people who find their interests in the realm of physics and engineering, Newton was born as an unwanted child to both his mother and father. On Christmas Day in 1642 (Early January, 1643 in the modern calendar), Newton was born into an England in the midst of a civil war and full of strife. His father had died 3 months prior to his birth, and Newton was a premature baby. As a result of these many factors, he was raised by his maternal grandmother, Margery Ayscough.

Needless to say, Isaac Newton's early years were dominated by a lack of control and great turmoil. Thus, Newton took great solace in the isolation and fundamental truth of mathematics. This background in Newton's early life is likely to have had profound influences on his growth into adulthood and his later exploration of math.

Newton, in his years of study before college, had a great fascination for mechanical devices and an understanding of the world around him. It was in 1661 that he gained admittance to the Trinity College in Cambridge, right as the English Civil War was coming to a close. It was here that his true exploration of math began to blossom.

\subsection{The Newtonian Calculus (1661-1679)}

During his time in college, Newton began his real exploration of mathematics. Newton's first great contribution to mathematics was in his studies of the Binomial theorem, which can give an expansion for a binomial raised to an arbitrary degree. Before Newton, there existed a theorem for the binomial theorem for the positive integers, $\mathbb{Z}^+$, which is presented below in \autoref{thm: binomial}.

\begin{theorem}[Binomial theorem]\label{thm: binomial}
    A binomial of form $x+y$ to a power n, where $n\in\mathbb{Z}^+$, the expansion can be written as:
    $$(x+y)^n=\sum_{k=0}^{n}\binom{n} {k}x^ky^{n-k}$$
    where $\binom{r}{k}$ is defined as:
    $$\binom{n}{k}=\frac{n!}{k!(n-k)!}$$
\end{theorem}

The original scope of this theorem, as I have just stated, only applies to the positive integers, $\mathbb{Z}^+$. Newton started by applying this formula on negative integers, which turned out to work well! He then extended the application of this theorem to the whole of the real numbers, $\mathbb{R}$ (Note: this is a \textit{great} accomplishment, but there is not time to dive into this interesting idea in this book). Newton's generalized theorem is then:
\begin{theorem}[Generalized Binomial theorem]
    A binomial of form $x+y$ to a power r, where $r\in\mathbb{R}$, the expansion can be written as:
    $$(x+y)^r=\sum_{k-0}^{\infty}\binom{r} {k}x^ry^{r-k}$$
    where $\binom{r}{k}$ is defined as:
    $$\binom{r}{k}=\frac{r(r-1)(r-2)\cdots(r-k+1)}{k!}$$
\end{theorem}

We note here that Newton has introduced an infinite sum! This is one of the first steps towards the methods of calculus. In order to calculate such an infinite sum requires the prerequisite tools for calculus. In fact, this equation has applicability towards the definition of the constant $e$, which is very fundamental in our studies of calculus.\endnote{This equation also allowed mathematicians to calculate the value of $\pi$ to better approximation than ever before. See \cite{veritasium_discovery_2021} for more info on this.} We can see this by inspection the definition of $e$ as:
$$ \lim_{x\to\infty} \left(1+\frac{1}{n}\right)^n$$
Using Newton's generalized binomial theorem, this infinite limit can be approximated and the value of $e$ can be found to high precision.

Newton continued his work on this subject, graduating in 1665. When Trinity University closed due to the Black Plague in the summer of that year, Newton took his work back home and began the most prolific part of his mathematical career, where he famously had the thought experiment of the apple falling towards the earth. It was during this time that Newton made his famous discoveries in optics and universal gravitation.

It was also during this period of great mathematical bounty that Newton began his studies of calculus. As early as 1665, Newton had methods to compute the derivative and find integral areas of curves. Using the generalized binomial theorem, Newton began to develop the concepts of integration and their applications to physics.

While the Newtonian methods of calculus are largely forgotten today in favor of the notation of Leibniz, it is without a doubt that Newton's contributions to the subject were extraordinary significant. Newton's contributions during this time will play a direct part in our later studies of energy mechanics.

\section{Fermat's Least Time Principle (1662)}
\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.75\linewidth]{Images/Fermat.jpg}
    \caption{A portrait of Pierre de Fermat}
    \label{fig:fermat}
\end{figure}
It is in the midst of Newton's most fruitful years that we must talk about Fermat's\endnote{Fermat is famous in mathematics for \textit{Fermat's Last Theorem}, which remained unsolved for 358 years. A solution involving elliptic curves exists, on which you can find an in-depth discussion in \cite{hellegouarch_invitation_2001}.} contributions to the subject of energy mechanics, which predate Newton's earliest contributions by nearly a score. Fermat, in the view of many, was the first to consider how nature may perform actions in `optimal' ways. 

His contributions here relate to the so-called \textit{Fermat's principle of least time}. He was interested in understanding how light interacts with mirrors and lenses. More importantly, he wanted to know \textit{why} it reflected and refracted the way it did. Up to that point, many experimentalists and others had figured out the relationship for light reflection and refraction (Snell's Law), but no strong explanations for the phenomenon had yet emerged. Fermat's big insight was the understanding that `nature operates by means and ways that are easiest and fastest'. By this, Fermat says that light will always take a path that minimizes the total time. Although this may seem a simple statement, it will underlie the physical principle at the root of energy mechanics.\endnote{During Fermat's time, it was thought that religious figures such as the Christian God dictated that such a principle was true. It was popular at the time to combine religious beliefs with physics and even use such beliefs as a kind of `proof' for why such principles are preferred to other principles. Newton, in fact, was a devout Christian. Today, most physicists view Fermat's Theorem as an embodiment of the fact that we can describe things locally (Newtonian-like) or globally (Fermat/Lagrangian-like) and still arrive at the same result either way. The fact that we can do so is astounding, but isn't thought to require divine intervention as things stand today.

The concept of `God' still enters the vernacular of physics sometimes (notably, Einstein was quite famous for this), but it is often used as a kind of personification of nature rather than a statement about the role of religion in physics.}

For any system, Fermat's principle of least time involves finding a light path such that the total time is minimized.\endnote{It can be shown that this is not exactly true, as the only condition required for sufficiency in this problem is that the changes to the variation are of the second-order. Fermat's condition is simply one such case of this. See \cite{feynman_feynman_1963} for more info.} Consider the refraction of light, which is easily observed in experiment. The setup is as shown in \autoref{fig:least time ray}. Fermat saw that the light path refracted and changed angle when moving through a medium where the speed of light is slower.\endnote{There are two things to note here. Fermat's assumption that the speed of light was finite was quite a divergence from the common thought of other philosophers, such as Descartes. As we know now, Fermat turned out to be correct! This assumption is the primary reason he was able to construct such an argument for the bending of light.

I should also note that the speed of light is constant. However, we may closely approximate the speed of light as being slower in more optically dense mediums. What really happens is that the light reflects and changes directions between a greater number of particles in an optically dense medium, which gives the appearance of a reduced speed on the macroscopic scale.} Notice that when entering the more optically dense medium, the angle of the light will become more steep ($\theta_r<\theta_i)$ because light travels slower through this dense medium. This is Fermat's principle in action.

    \centering

    \label{fig:least time ray}
\end{figure}

Why exactly does this happen? If we are to believe Fermat's idea that the light ray wants to minimize time, we can see why this must be true. Consider a similar scenario where you are running along a beach and trying to reach a buoy in the water at point $B$, as shown in \autoref{fig:beach}. You can run faster on the sand than you can swim in the water, so it seems obvious that you want to run along the sand for as long as possible before you enter the water. There is some point where it becomes optimal to transition into the water to swim the rest of the way.
\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering

\tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

\begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-1,xscale=1]
%uncomment if require: \path (0,300); %set diagram left start at 0, and has height of 300

%Shape: Rectangle [id:dp336479312158828] 
\draw  [color={rgb, 255:red, 126; green, 219; blue, 222 }  ,draw opacity=0.51 ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 126; green, 219; blue, 222 }  ,fill opacity=0.51 ] (130,140) -- (500,140) -- (500,255) -- (130,255) -- cycle ;
%Shape: Rectangle [id:dp763265624867049] 
\draw  [draw opacity=0][fill={rgb, 255:red, 248; green, 231; blue, 28 }  ,fill opacity=0.18 ] (130,64.7) -- (500,64.7) -- (500,140) -- (130,140) -- cycle ;
%Straight Lines [id:da0037110008614668066] 
\draw    (160,70) -- (380,140) ;

%Straight Lines [id:da6218104451616018] 
\draw    (380,140) -- (426,215) ;
\draw [shift={(426,215)}, rotate = 58.48] [color={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.75]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 3.35, y radius= 3.35]   ;

% Text Node
\draw (156,57.6) node [anchor=south east] [inner sep=0.75pt]    {$A$};
% Text Node

\end{tikzpicture}
    \caption{Analogy of Fermat's least time problem}
    \label{fig:beach}
\end{figure}
The idea is exactly the same for Fermat's problem with light. Of course, we don't attach sentience to the light. It just so happens that the way the glass refracts the light will match this optimal path. We will see why light \textit{must} behave this way in \autoref{sec: connection to quantum}.

Fermat's calculations to figure out the relation between the entrance and exit angles is quite archaic, so I won't proceed with it here. Instead, I will take a more modern approach which follows roughly along with Fermat. Following the standard procedures of calculus, we will find a minimization of the function by considering the point where the first derivative is zero. Starting from the set-up shown in \autoref{fig:least time ray}, we can write equations for the time for each of the sections of the light path. To do so, we use the fundamental relationship for velocity:
$$t=\frac{d}{v}$$
Using the definition that the velocity in an optical medium is $v=\frac{c}{n}$, we can write the time for each section. Starting with the section in the lower optical density:
$$t_{upper}=\frac{n_i\sqrt{{h_1}^2+x^2}}{c}$$
And for the more dense optical surface, we do the same:
$$t_{lower}=\frac{n_r\sqrt{{h_2}^2+(l-x)^2}}{c}$$
Altogether, we have a the following relationship:
$$t=\frac{n_i\sqrt{{h_1}^2+x^2}}{c}+\frac{n_r\sqrt{{h_2}^2+(l-x)^2}}{c}$$
We can achieve the desired goal of finding the minimum time by taking the derivative of this time with respect to the variable $x$ and setting it equal to zero as follows:
$$\frac{dt}{dx}=\frac{n_ix}{c\sqrt{{h_1}^2+x^2}}-\frac{n_r(l-x)}{c\sqrt{{h_2}^2+(l-x)^2}}=0$$
A rearrangement leads to the following:
$$\frac{n_ix}{c\sqrt{{h_1}^2+x^2}}=\frac{n_r(l-x)}{c\sqrt{{h_2}^2+(l-x)^2}}$$
Using the established angles $\theta_1$ and $\theta_2$, we can express this relation more simply as:
\begin{gather}
    \color{boxBlue}\boxed{\color{black}n_i\sin\theta_i=n_r\sin\theta_r}\\
    \color{boxBlue}\text{Snell's Law}
\end{gather}

Interestingly, this theoretical result derived from the least time principle matches the experimental results exactly! Fermat's guess that the time is minimized turns out to be the correct one.\endnote{I suppose we wouldn't be talking about it if he were wrong!} We will keep Fermat's result in mind, as we will see it again when we solve the problem of the \gls{brachistochrone}. For now, let's go back to Newton to understand his developments in his masterwork, \textit{Principia Mathematica}.

\section{Principia (1687)}
It is in \textit{Principia} that we find the first evidence of Newton's contributions to the field of energy mechanics. It is Newton's works in the \textit{Principia} that most scholars agree are the first real works of the calculus of variations.

Newton's first attempt at a variational problem in 1685 was to understand the shape that minimizes drag, which Newton called the \textit{minimal resistance problem}. In Newton's time, no solidified theories of fluid dynamics had been formulated, so Newton suggested a fluid that is `a rare medium of very small quiescent particles of equal magnitude and freely disposed at equal distances from one other'. Although this fluid is not at all reminiscent of a real fluid\endnote{Unless, curiously, the fluid is travelling at hypersonic speeds, in which case this model is quite good.}, it still remains a useful exercise in the study of the calculus of variations.

The major advancement in this problem came from Newton's formulation to solve it, rather than the problem setup, which we will not derive\endnote{See \cite{goldstine_history_1980} for information on the derivation.}. Newton came up with a simple integral to represent the drag of an object:$$\int_{t_1}^{t_2}{\frac{y\dot{y}^3dt}{\dot{x}^2+\dot{y}^2}}$$In this equation, Newton has parameterized the minimal drag surface curve in terms of the variable $t$ and expressed the derivatives of $x$ and $y$ with respect to $t$ as $\dot{x}$ and $\dot{y}$. As we will see later, this integral is something that we will call a \gls{functional}. When we evaluate this integral, we will get a single number, and that number is the quantity which we want to minimize in order to get the lowest drag. The $x$ and $y$ which led to this minimization will tell us the curve that minimizes drag.

Newton solved this problem in \textit{Principia}, but his exact methodology was not known. It was not until 1691, when Huygens\footnote{17th century Dutch physicist and polymath.} began independent study of the problem, that anyone had shed any light on the methods of solving the problem. The original text, however, still inspired the idea of the \gls{functional} and began the further exploration of the subject of calculus of variations. With the idea of the functional created and a methodology to solve problems in the calculus of variations, the next major step in the subject came with the \gls{brachistochrone} problem.

\section{Brachistochrone (1696)}

In June of 1696, nearly a decade after Newton's initial foray into the calculus of variations, John Bernoulli challenged the mathematical world to solve the problem of the \gls{brachistochrone} -- a word coming from Greek, meaning `shortest time'. In its simplest description, the problem involves finding a curve for which a frictionless bead will descend the fastest. A simple diagram of the system is reproduced in \autoref{fig:Brachistochrone}. 

\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering



\tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

\begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-1,xscale=1]
%uncomment if require: \path (0,300); %set diagram left start at 0, and has height of 300

%Curve Lines [id:da5626290173929862] 
\draw [line width=2.25]    (226,80) .. controls (224.23,164.47) and (303.23,269.47) .. (426,230) ;
%Shape: Circle [id:dp16005060334035148] 
\draw  [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,fill opacity=1 ] (228,88) .. controls (228,85.24) and (230.24,83) .. (233,83) .. controls (235.76,83) and (238,85.24) .. (238,88) .. controls (238,90.76) and (235.76,93) .. (233,93) .. controls (230.24,93) and (228,90.76) .. (228,88) -- cycle ;
%Straight Lines [id:da9179973387326911]

% Text Node
\draw (207,72.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$A$};
% Text Node
\draw (437,222.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$B$};
% Text Node
\draw (130,122.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$\hat{x}$};
% Text Node
\draw (179,70.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$\hat{y}$};


\end{tikzpicture}


    \caption{Brachistochrone Problem Set-Up}
    \label{fig:Brachistochrone}
\end{figure}

John Bernoulli had come up with a quite an ingenious solution to the problem. However, he challenged the rest of the mathematical world to solve the problem as well, with a deadline of Easter of 1697.

At the time, there were 4 notable solutions, those coming from Leibniz, John Bernoulli, James Bernoulli, and Isaac Newton (who submitted anonymously). Newton, supposedly, spent one night on his anonymous solution, and submitted it to Bernoulli. Despite being an anonymous submission, Bernoulli is said to have recognized Newton's solution by the nature of the writing. Bernoulli said, ``tanquam ex ungue leonem'' (I recognize the lion by its claw), of Newton's solution.

An exploration of the problem in the modern context (using the Euler-Lagrange approach) will be studied in \autoref{chap:calculus of variations}. The approach of Leibniz and Newton is similar to the modern approach, so I will not touch on those here. However, I will explore the solution of John Bernoulli, mostly for the interesting approach. This will also help prime the reader for the derivation that is to come later. This derivation is by no means necessary to the understanding of the calculus of variations, but readers may find it to be quite elegant and enjoyable.

\subsection{The Bernoulli Brachistochrone Solution}
The set-up of the \gls{brachistochrone} problem is fairly simple. From energy relations, we know that the total energy of the system must be conserved:
$$E=T+U=\text{const.}$$
Defining the reference at point $A$ as shown in \autoref{fig:Brachistochrone}, it is apparent that the velocity at any given point is:
$$v(x)^2=2g(x-x_0)+v_0^2$$
Where $v_0$ and $x_0$ represent initial conditions. Typically, we take these to be zero for the problem and can simplify this relation to:
$$v(x)=\sqrt{2gx}$$
Importantly, we can see that the velocity is independent of the horizontal range or the path of the particle. In other words, we have a \textit{conservative} system, which gives us great flexibility. From here, we can proceed in a number of ways. The standard method to do this will be explored later. Here, however, Bernoulli makes an insight relating the problem to Fermat's Least Time principle.

Bernoulli's insight is as follows - a ray of light moves through a medium in a way that reaches the boundary in the shortest amount of time (as shown by Fermat). Bernoulli's idea was to create an optical medium where the refraction index changes as a function of the height. So, he set the index of refraction at each height inversely proportional to the velocity (as shown in \autoref{fig:Brachistochrone Optical}). Since the velocity is only dependent on the height, this optical picture is exactly analogous. By finding the path that light would take through this medium, Bernoulli could find the curve of fastest descent and the solution to the brachistochrone problem.
\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering
    
% Gradient Info
  
\tikzset {_3o1kvs6si/.code = {\pgfsetadditionalshadetransform{ \pgftransformshift{\pgfpoint{0 bp} { 0 bp }  }  \pgftransformrotate{-270 }  \pgftransformscale{2 }  }}}

With Bernoulli's idea in mind, let us formulate a mathematical description. We will start by showing a modern depiction of Bernoulli's drawing, which I will discuss as we work through the problem. This is shown in \autoref{fig:Brachistochrone Bernoulli}.  The \gls{brachistochrone} curve is denoted with the curve $ABK$. The velocity of the particle as a function of height is denoted by the blue curve $AH$, with the magnitude at that height denoted by the horizontal distance from the line $\overline{AC}$. $A$ and $B$, like before, denote the start and end points of the \gls{brachistochrone}.

    \label{fig:Brachistochrone Bernoulli}
\end{figure}



Next, we find the differential element, $dz$. This is given by the vertical component ($\hat{x}$-direction in our established coordinates), which is given by the length $\overline{Mn}$, and the horizontal component ($\hat{y}$-direction), $\overline{nm}$. Denoting these distances $dx$ and $dy$, we have the fundamental relation:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:pathlength}
    dz^2=dx^2+dy^2
\end{equation}
Next, he uses the relationship from Snell's Law:
$$n\sin\theta=const.$$
The quantity $\sin\theta$ is thus $\frac{dy}{dz}$ and $n$ is some arbitrary value depending on the exact curve.

Remembering that the optical density is inversely proportional to the velocity at a point, Bernoulli relates the horizontal and path length distance to the velocity as well, using the previously established relationship that $\sin\theta=\frac{dy}{dz}$:
$$\frac{dy}{dz}=\frac{v}{n}$$
Using the fact relation from \eqautoref{eq:pathlength}, this can be related to $\frac{dy}{dx}$:
$$\frac{dy}{dx}=\frac{v}{\sqrt{n^2-v^2}}$$
There is one final step in this derivation. Using the fact from before that the velocity of the particle is $v=\sqrt{nx}$, the problem can be solved for a particle acted upon by gravity. Rearrangement yields:
\begin{equation}
\frac{dy}{dx}=\frac{\sqrt{nx}}{\sqrt{n^2-nx}}
\end{equation}
Which can be further simplified by dividing out a $\sqrt{n}$ term:
\begin{equation}
        \frac{dy}{dx}=\sqrt{\frac{x}{n-x}}
\end{equation}
From here, it is simply a matter of solving the differential equation to yield the resulting curve. Performing separation of variables and integrating, we arrive at the final solution:
$$a\tan^{-1}\left(\sqrt{\frac{x}{n-x}}\right)-(n-x)\sqrt{\frac{x}{n-x}}$$
This curve is the curve of a cycloid, the curve generated by tracking a point on a circle which rolls without slipping.

The cycloid, Bernoulli noted, also has some other interesting properties which are strongly connected with the \gls{brachistochrone}. Huygens had found earlier that a pendulum with a cycloidal path is \textit{isochronous}, meaning that the time for a swing of the pendulum is independent of the amplitude. Similarly, for the \gls{brachistochrone}, a particle released at any point along the curve will reach point $B$ at exactly the same time.

It is at this point that we have sufficient background to understand the work that led to the creation of the calculus of variations. From these foundations, mathematicians such as Euler and Lagrange built up a rigorous basis for the subject, which we will now explore in the next chapter.

\subsection{Historical Addendum: Proving Fermat's Principle}\label{sec: connection to quantum}

Now\footnote{This section may be skipped without loss of continuity.} that we've examined the history of variational calculus, let's return to Fermat's principle to understand why it is true. Why does light take a path that minimizes time? Is there any physical reason for this?

To fully understand this question, we must understand that during Fermat's time there was a rift in the world of physics concerning the behavior of light. Some, such as Newton, believed light to be a particle. Others, led by the aforementioned Christiaan Huygens, believed that light was a wave.

When we consider light to be a wave, the problem of least time seems to have a simple explanation. If the light takes a suboptimal trajectory (that is, a trajectory that takes more time), the wave will destructively interfere with itself. It is only light which takes the shortest path which will constructively interfere and produce a visible spot. So the riddle is solved. Newton is wrong, and light is in fact a wave! The problem is this: We can measure individual packets of light, photons, arriving at photomultipliers or sensors. When a light source gets dimmer, we simply measure fewer numbers of photons, but the energies associated with each one are exactly the same. There is another great piece of evidence that light \textit{must} come in discrete packets which comes from studying blackbody radiation. If light were not discretized, we would expect high frequency light to contain infinite amounts of energy!\endnote{This is known as the UV catastrophe in physics. It was solved by Max Planck, who introduced the notion of quantized light in order to solve this issue.}

So we must again reconsider our viewpoint. Light must showcase both properties to match all of the evidence! Of course, it is now popularly understood that light - and in fact everything else - exhibits both properties in a wave-particle duality.\endnote{Of course, the wave-particle duality is not precisely true. At the most fundamental level, we now believe that the universe is described purely by quantum fields. Particles, however, can appear to arise out of fluctuations in these quantum fields. Because the fluctuations of the field are quantized, it gives the appearance of individual particles. The description of light as particles suffices for our purposes.}

The existence of this duality alone does not necessarily answer our question, but it gets us on the path to doing so. The true answer to this question remained unknown for nearly 300 years -- until the advent of \gls{qed} in the 1940's, the invention of which would lead Richard Feynman, Julian Schwinger, and Sin-Itiro Tomonaga to win the Nobel Prize in physics.

Of course, explaining a Nobel Prize worthy achievement in physics is no easy task! Luckily, Feynman himself has offered quite a simple explanation of the phenomena of light in his book \cite{richard_phillips_feynman_qed_1988}. I highly suggest the interested reader to read this book in full (it is quite short and easy to read) to better comprehend this section. Here, I will present only a portion of Feynman's argument to convince you that the least time principle naturally arises from \gls{qed}. The principle indeed quite simple for the case of light rays, but it will demonstrate something fundamental that will come up again in \autoref{chap:lagrange continued}.

\subsubsection{Mirrors}
Although we earlier made a computation for Snell's Law using Fermat's principle, we will instead consider the case of the reflecting surface here. The derivation of the equivalent least time principle for the mirror is left as an exercise, but follows from a similar procedure.

\begin{exercise}
    Using the same methods as were used to derive Snell's Law, find the criteria that minimizes time for the case of a mirror. The set up for such a mirror system is shown in \autoref{fig:mirror}.

(442.62,100.34) ; \draw [shift={(443.61,103.09)}, rotate = 250.98] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,fill opacity=1 ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.04,-3.86) -- (0,0) -- (8.04,3.86) -- cycle    ; 

% Text Node
\draw (231,32.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$\theta _{i}$};
% Text Node
\draw (330,273.4) node [anchor=north] [inner sep=0.75pt]    {$L$};
% Text Node
\draw (108,105) node [anchor=east] [inner sep=0.75pt]    {$h_{1}$};
% Text Node
\draw (106,64.6) node [anchor=south east] [inner sep=0.75pt]    {$A$};
% Text Node
\draw (557,52.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$B$};
% Text Node
\draw (220,136.6) node [anchor=south] [inner sep=0.75pt]    {$x$};
% Text Node
\draw (406,31.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$\theta _{f}$};


\end{tikzpicture}

    \caption{Mirror Set-Up}
    \label{fig:mirror}
\end{figure}
\end{exercise}

Readers who carefully perform the derivation above using the methods shown before will find that in the case of the mirror, $\theta_i=\theta_f$. That is, the incident angle is equal to the outgoing angle. In our previous derivations, we showed this to be a consequence of the principle of least time.

If we think of the problem more `democratically', we shouldn't give any preference to any one path. Let's consider all of the straight line paths that light can take. A few of these paths are shown in \autoref{fig:light traj}.

    \label{fig:light traj}

Of course, we know from experience that mirrors do not show reflections like this. If they did, we could look from point $A$ at any angle and we would see $B$ centered in the view. There must be something wrong with our model. And there is: we are missing a key component called the phase of the light. Essentially, the phase measures the `direction' of the light (this description will suffice for our purposes)\endnote{More technically, we can encode this phase with a complex number. The vector addition of these complex numbers causes constructive and destructive interference.}. We can think of the phase of the light as a little clock that moves with the light. The clock hand starts pointing straight up at point $A$, and it winds around at a constant rate until the photon reaches point $B$, at that point, we measure the direction the clock hand is pointing. This will be a vector with a length of 1, but the direction will change based on the time duration!

So, if we take phase into consideration, we get a new picture of the phenomenon, which is shown in \autoref{fig:polarization paths}. What is shown is the time that it takes each ray of light to reach point $B$ based on the point where it hits the mirror. These are marked with a red marker below the point of intersection with the mirror.

% Text Node
\draw (95,212) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [font=\small] [align=left] {Time};

    \caption{Phase of light for different paths}
    \label{fig:polarization paths}
\end{figure}

The final phase of the light upon reaching point $B$ is shown below this image. The phase of the light rotates very rapidly, so a difference in time will cause large changes in the phase of the light. For paths that are far away from the center, these time differences create arrows that point in seemingly random directions! The result of this is that, on average, these arrows tend to cancel each other out. In physics and math lingo, we can apply the stationary phase approximation, and all the waves tend to cancel each other out.

However, for paths that are close to the path of least time, we can move the intersection point left and right and the amount of time taken doesn't change much at all! This occurs, of course, because we are at a local minimum of the function, where the derivative is zero. When we look at the vectors for these portions, we find that the phase for each of these is almost identical! So we find that these paths near the path of least time have vectors that are all very similar to each other.

The last step in this process is to add all of the vectors together to generate a final vector. The square of this final vector is representative of the probability that light reaches point $B$. By adding together all of the vectors, we can see that the largest contribution to the final vector comes from the portion in the middle. In fact, the portions near the outside can cancel each other out so that they have almost no effect on the final outcome. We can see that the path of minimal time is the one which dominates our probability -- which is why this is the path we see in the classical world and the one that Fermat derived. Only the path which minimizes the time will have this local minimum and exhibit this property.

Although the methodology described above is perfectly sound, the angular and time differences described here are quite exaggerated. In reality, even paths deviating by less than a degree from the ideal path tend to cancel each other out for visible light. This is a result of the frequency of light being so high that phase shifts occur on the order of picoseconds. Another simplification is that we only considered straight paths. In reality, we should consider all paths, straight or curved. We would find that the same result holds.

If you want to gain a better understanding of this relationship, an interactive MATLAB script "Mirror.m" is in the GitHub. The script will run a simulation of this scenario. In this toy model it can be seen how the vectors add in a similar way to our diagrammatic depiction. For a true physical system, these effects occur on a much smaller scale. However, we will still see the same effects arise and the formation of spiral patterns near the ends, where the vectors tend to cancel each other out. Together, they form a very beautiful spiral that resembles the sound holes of a string instrument, which we can see in \autoref{fig:mirror vec 2}. If you are somehow unconvinced by the greatness of Euler at this point in the book series, here is an interesting fact: the path of this spiral is called an Euler spiral, and was first described by Euler in 1744. The connection to optics was made nearly a century later by Alfred Cornu.


To recap, only the components near the path of least time add together! So, on a large scale, we say that light does indeed take the path of least time, even though we can show on the quantum scale that it is free to do anything! We thus recover the classical picture from a purely quantum line of reasoning.

\begin{figure}
    \centering
\includesvg[width=.625\linewidth]{Scripts/MirrorVectors.svg}
    \caption{Vector Representation of Mirror. Each blue component is an individual reflection, and the orange arrow is the sum total, which points along the general direction of the minimum time photons.}
    \label{fig:mirror vec 2}
\end{figure}

Another simplification you may have noticed is that the magnitude of the final vector arrow squared is the probability. However, we have given each component a length of 1! So if we add infinitely many of these together, the total probability will go to $\infty$, which is obviously incorrect. This is a problem in \gls{qed} called renormalization. It took some very smart physicists and a certain disregard for mathematical rigor to resolve this issue. Despite some of these challenges, \gls{qed} still provides a very nice framework that allows us to understand why the principle of least time holds. Having gained this understanding of Fermat's least time principle, we may return to our journey on understanding variational calculus.

\newpage
\printendnotes

\chapter{Calculus of Variations}\label{chap:calculus of variations}

% \color{red} This chapter needs to be expanded quite a bit, it is definitely lacking in the current state. Maybe include Lagrangian multipliers and problems of the 1st and 2nd variation. Finish the brachistochrone derivation. 
% \color{black}

\begin{chapquote}{Richard Feynman}
 ``Nature uses only the longest threads to weave her patterns, so that each small piece of her fabric reveals the organization of the entire tapestry.''
\end{chapquote}

The calculus of variations underpins the ideas of \gls{Lagrangian} and \gls{Hamiltonian} mechanics, and also serves as the foundation of optimal control. It is important to understand the principles behind it before moving on to more physically relevant ideas. In analogy to how single variable calculus is fundamental to Newtonian mechanics, the calculus of variations is fundamental to energy mechanics.

The fundamental idea of the calculus of variations is simple. We want to find the maximums and minimums over a whole \textit{path} in our problem. We can draw an analogy between single variable calculus to understand more deeply what the calculus of variations is. In the standard picture of calculus, we are concerned with the change of a function when subjected to some infinitesimal change in a single direction, a small $dx$. In variational calculus, we are concerned with varying the whole path by some $\eta(x)$\endnote{Actually, there are two distinct ways of performing a path variation. For our introduction, we'll discuss the first type, but the second is introduced when we do optimal control.} and how that changes the value of an integral over the function -- analogous to our local vs. global view.
\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering

\tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

\begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-1,xscale=1]
%uncomment if require: \path (0,300); %set diagram left start at 0, and has height of 300

%Curve Lines [id:da7609637284959898] 
\draw [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,draw opacity=1 ]   (290,200) .. controls (338.73,126.96) and (416.62,206.96) .. (459.77,110) ;

\draw [shift={(290,200)}, rotate = 303.71] [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,fill opacity=1 ][line width=0.75]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 1.34, y radius= 1.34]   ;
%Curve Lines [id:da5393827702174848]

\draw [shift={(290,200)}, rotate = 308.05] [color={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.75]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 1.34, y radius= 1.34]   ;

\draw [shift={(90,210)}, rotate = 346.1] [color={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.75]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 1.34, y radius= 1.34]   ;
%Straight Lines [id:da808657671697389] 
\draw    (181,171) -- (203.75,171) ;
%Straight Lines [id:da21364308945871324] 
\draw    (203.75,171) -- (203.75,151.4) ;

\draw (469,92.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$y( x)$};
% Text Node
\draw (386.46,167.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,opacity=1 ]  {$y( x) +\varepsilon \eta ( x)$};
% Text Node
\draw (183,174.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$dx$};
% Text Node
\draw (210.64,160) node [anchor=west] [inner sep=0.75pt]    {$dy$};
% Text Node
\draw (114,39) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]   [align=left] {Single Variable:\\Variation in $\displaystyle x$};
% Text Node
\draw (306,41) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]   [align=left] {Variational Calculus:\\Variation of whole path};


\end{tikzpicture}
    
    \caption{Demonstration of the key difference between standard single variable calculus and variational calculus.}
    \label{fig:variational calc}
\end{figure}
Let's extend the analogy between single variable calculus a little bit further. In standard calculus, when we do a small perturbation by $dx$, we get some change $dy$. At a maximum or minimum, the change $dy$ will be zero when we do a shift by $dx$. By the same reasoning, when using a whole function, we know we must have reached a local minimum or maximum whenever we add some $\varepsilon\eta(x)$ and the value of our whole function doesn't change.\endnote{You may wonder at this point what it means to have the `value' of a function. We will return to this point later.} We call such a point a \textit{stationary point}.

We could justify wanting such optimal curves easily in physical problems. In physics, we often see that quantities are minimized or maximized for some reason. For example, a soap surface will minimize its surface area, or a beam of photons will follow a \gls{geodesic} of spacetime. Light will also reflect and refract across surfaces to minimize time, as we have seen with Fermat's Principle. As we will soon see, all of mechanics can be understood by the fact that a quantity called the \textit{action} can also be minimized.

There is an interesting idea contained in the statement that we have just made. In the Newtonian paradigm, we consider forces acting on an object, and these local changes lead us to a final trajectory as we advance time forward -- what we might call an initial value problem. In the paradigm we have just described, we instead consider the whole path taken from point $A$ to point $B$ -- a boundary value problem. Both, however, give the same answer. Instead of taking a local view of the physics, we take a global view. Think about Fermat's principle -- we could either consider how light interacts locally at the boundary between air and glass, or the total minimization of time. Unless we understand how the wavefront of light interacts with such a boundary, the global approach may prove easier.

Because so many physical phenomena are governed by this behavior to minimize certain quantities, the calculus of variations became a prominent field of study from the 17th to 20th century. Of course, the calculus of variations also has many applications outside of the confines of physics and control theory, some of which will be briefly explored as exercises and examples at the end of this chapter.

\section{Functionals}

Optimization of a function occurs when we've found a minima or maxima of some quantity. In single variable calculus, we only concern ourselves with finding the minimum value of a function, say finding the minimum value of $y=x^2$. In variational calculus, we now have a new challenge. That challenge is to find a whole function which is a minimum.

Whenever we try to perform optimization, we need to know \textit{what} we are optimizing for. So, we should state our goal a bit more precisely: we are attempting to find a function that is the minimum for some set of conditions. A common case is that we consider a function and its derivative: $y(x)$ and $y'(x)$. Because we also have $x$, there are 3 inputs which define such a function.\endnote{It is implicit here that the function $y(x)$ is at least $\mathcal{C}^1$ continuous such that the first derivative $y'(x)$ is well behaved. Most physical systems which we are concerned with in physics and control theory will naturally satisfy this condition (most are $\mathcal{C}^\infty$ continuous).}

Because the paths $y$ and $y'$ are themselves a function of $x$, we incur additional complexity as compared to single variable calculus. We must optimize the paths for all of the values of $x$!\endnote{In fact, we must minimize our function over a range that has an infinite number of values. For this reason, the calculus of variations is sometimes cited as a type of infinite dimensional calculus.} We call the function that we are trying to minimize $f$. Strictly, we define the function $f$ as\endnote{For those unfamiliar with this notation, it tells us that the function $f$ takes in inputs $y$ and $y'$ (which generally exist in some space $\mathbb{R}^n$). It also takes in $x$ which is an element of $\mathbb{R}$. Together, these are combined (through a cartesian product). The arrow points towards the codomain, which tells us what space the output of the function lies in. In this case, the output is a real number in $\mathbb{R}$.}

\begin{equation}
f:\mathbb{R}^n\times \mathbb{R}^n\times \mathbb{R}\rightarrow\mathbb{R}
\end{equation}

To reiterate, the conditions take the form of a function, as they are dependent on some variable $x$. We define a function, $f$, which abides by these constraint equations. Since $f$ has a dependence on these constraints, we may write $f$ as:
$$f[y(x),y'(x);x]$$
This statement means that $f$ is a function which is defined by $y(x)$ and its derivative $y'(x)$. The semicolon notes that the variable of interest is $x$. We should note that there is no intrinsic reason that $f$ is a function of $y(x)$ and $y'(x)$. It turns out that for the \gls{Lagrangian} this is the appropriate constraint on the system.\endnote{In fact, the \gls{Lagrangian} is so influential in the calculus of variations that we call this function the \gls{Lagrangian} even when it does not have this specific form. For this reason, some authors will denote this function $L$ instead of $f$.} However, we can imagine in the general case that $f$ is dependent on some other criteria, such as $y''(x)$. We will not tackle that situation here, but the proceeding steps may be conducted similarly for such a function. For now, let's return to a function $f$ which is defined in terms of conditions on $y(x)$ and $y'(x)$.

We may write a function $f$ which abides by these conditions, say $f = y(x)+2y'(x)$. That is fine, but how can we find where this function is at a minimum? What does that even mean?

\subsection{Norms of Functions}

To define the minimum of the function $f$, there are two things we need to do:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Find a way to convert the function $f$ into a single variable problem so we can optimize it.
    \item Define a notion of what it means for one $f$ to be larger or smaller than the other.
\end{enumerate}

For the first point, since our function exists over a whole range of values of $x$, the sensible thing to do is to integrate the function $f$ so that we may reduce the dependence on $x$ to a single function integrated over all $x$. We can evaluate this at two set endpoints, $x_1$ and $x_2$, and this will return a single number value that represents the function. We call this scalar output a \gls{functional}. This \gls{functional} is essentially a function of $f$, so it is a function of a function. We typically describe a functional with the letter $J$, and we write the form of the integral as:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:functional}
    J=\int_{x_1}^{x_2}f\left[y(x),y'(x);x\right]{dx}
\end{equation}
Remember that the most important aspect of $J$ is that we have taken an entire function, over a fixed range, and collapsed all of that function's properties into a single number, $J$. We may say this more precisely as: The \gls{functional}, $J$, is a quantity which maps a function onto the real number line. This means that it takes the function as its input and outputs a single real number (that is, a function of a function). 

This idea of a functional is one of the key tools in the calculus of variations. For our purposes, we only want to use this property to solve for an optimal function $f$. More precisely, we are attempting to find conditions on the original function, $y(x)$ and $y'(x)$, which yield an extrema of $J$. 

This leads us to the second point, which is how we compare two inputs, $y(x)$. We should have some notion of how two paths, some arbitrary $y^*(x)$ and $y(x)$, may differ from each other. To do so, it is typical to define two different norms. The first is the $0$-norm:
$$||y||_0=\max_{x_1\le x\le x_2}|y(x)|$$
Here, $||\cdot||_0$ is the zero norm, and $|\cdot|$ is the standard Euclidean $2$-norm on $\mathbb{R}^n$. Thus, the 0-norm of a function $y$ is the maximum value of the $2$-norm over the interval of interest. 

Because we often also include the first derivative, we sometimes also want to ensure that this derivative remains small. In that case, we define the 1-norm:
$$||y||_1=\max_{x_1\le x\le x_2}|y(x)|+\max_{x_1\le x\le x_2}|y'(x)|$$

With the norms defined, we can formally define what we mean by the local minimum of our functional.

\begin{definition}[Local Minimum of Functional]

The curve $y^*$ is a local minimum of a functional $J$ if there exists $\varepsilon>0$ such that for all $y$ satisfying $||y-y^*||<\varepsilon$, we have $J(y^*)\leq J(y)$.
\end{definition}

We can apply either the 0 or the 1-norm to the definition above. It is only with the definition of these norms that we can compare the distance between two functions $y$ and determine if the points are local minima. While the specification of this norm may seem arbitrary, the choice between the 0-norm and 1-norm turns out to have important consequences in optimal control! In particular, these correspond to strong and weak extrema, respectively. For our study of energy mechanics in the upcoming chapters, the distinction has little practical importance. However, it will soon become highly important in optimal control!

\subsection{Variations}
In the previous section, we defined a variation as some optimal path $y(x)$ with a variation by $\varepsilon\eta(x)$. Let's explore that idea further to create the equivalent of the derivative for functionals.

\subsubsection{Extrema conditions}
The approach to finding the extrema of the function is quite similar to the approach we would take in single variable calculus, which we are all familiar with. To find the minima or maxima of a function $f(x)$, we find where $f'(x)=0$ (\autoref{fig:single var func}).
\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering

\tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

\begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-1,xscale=1]
%uncomment if require: \path (0,300); %set diagram left start at 0, and has height of 300

%Shape: Polynomial [id:dp31740373171286806] 
\draw  [line width=1.5]  (137,222) .. controls (207,32) and (277,317) .. (347,127) ;
%Straight Lines [id:da15243145724705576] 
\draw [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][line width=1.5]    (248.6,196.2) -- (328.6,196.2) ;
\draw [shift={(288.6,196.2)}, rotate = 0] [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,fill opacity=1 ][line width=1.5]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 1.74, y radius= 1.74]   ;
%Straight Lines [id:da29067177346887385]

\draw [shift={(194.4,153.6)}, rotate = 0] [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,fill opacity=1 ][line width=1.5]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 1.74, y radius= 1.74]   ;
%Straight Lines [id:da1070293344802159]

\draw [shift={(191.59,166.21)}, rotate = 78.83] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 218; green, 44; blue, 0 }  ,fill opacity=1 ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;

% Text Node
\draw (204.2,232.8) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [color={rgb, 255:red, 218; green, 44; blue, 0 }  ,opacity=1 ]  {$f'=0$};
% Text Node
\draw (263.4,89) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [color={rgb, 255:red, 218; green, 44; blue, 0 }  ,opacity=1 ]  {$f( x)$};


\end{tikzpicture}

    \caption{Extrema of a single variable function}
    \label{fig:single var func}
\end{figure}
What is important to note is what happens right around these extremal points. If we are right at the point where $f'(0)$, we are at a local minimum or maximum of the function\endnote{There is another case where we are at neither, if the function does not change sign and just goes perfectly flat. This is not illustrated here for simplicity.}. However, if we move left or right by some very small amount -- which we call $\varepsilon$ -- the value of the function stays the same! This should come as no surprise, because $f'(x)$ gives a local value of the tangent of the function. In other words, it gives a linear approximation of the function at that location. As long as $\varepsilon$ remains small, $f(x)$ is perfectly represented by this linear approximation.

Now, we may extend this definition to the more difficult case of the extrema in variational calculus. Instead of adding some small value, we are instead adding some small function. This function is typically denoted $\eta(x)$, and by convention we still multiply $\eta(x)$ by $\varepsilon$ to denote the fact that we are considering very small functions. In analogy to the single variable case of a minimum, if we have a minimized functional, we can add $\varepsilon\eta(x)$ to $y(x)$ and the value of the functional remains unchanged! We can see this in \autoref{fig:neigh func}.

\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering


\tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

\begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-1,xscale=1]
%uncomment if require: \path (0,300); %set diagram left start at 0, and has height of 300

%Curve Lines [id:da3860827164688039] 
\draw [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,draw opacity=1 ]   (120,225) .. controls (167.17,151.96) and (242.56,231.96) .. (284.33,135) ;
\draw [shift={(284.33,135)}, rotate = 293.3] [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,fill opacity=1 ][line width=0.75]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 1.34, y radius= 1.34]   ;
\draw [shift={(120,225)}, rotate = 302.85] [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,fill opacity=1 ][line width=0.75]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 1.34, y radius= 1.34]   ;
%Curve Lines [id:da7977961164869156] 
\draw    (120,225) .. controls (160.3,171.8) and (249.23,208.6) .. (284.33,135) ;
\draw [shift={(284.33,135)}, rotate = 295.49] [color={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.75]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 1.34, y radius= 1.34]   ;
\draw [shift={(120,225)}, rotate = 307.15] [color={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.75]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 1.34, y radius= 1.34]   ;
