\draw    (337.13,221) -- (504.61,221) ;
\draw [shift={(507.61,221)}, rotate = 180] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (3.57,-1.72) -- (0,0) -- (3.57,1.72) -- cycle    ;
\draw [shift={(334.13,221)}, rotate = 0] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (3.57,-1.72) -- (0,0) -- (3.57,1.72) -- cycle    ;
%Straight Lines [id:da4291618420656924] 
\draw    (420.87,104) -- (420.87,221) ;
\draw [shift={(420.87,101)}, rotate = 90] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (3.57,-1.72) -- (0,0) -- (3.57,1.72) -- cycle    ;
%Shape: Parabola [id:dp32441741010727465] 
\draw   (342.39,111) .. controls (394.71,177.67) and (447.03,177.67) .. (499.35,111) ;

% Text Node
\draw (286.93,123.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$y( x)$};
% Text Node
\draw (194,193.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,opacity=1 ]  {$y( x) +\varepsilon \eta ( x)$};
% Text Node
\draw (329.78,100.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$J$};
% Text Node
\draw (508.22,210.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$\varepsilon $};
% Text Node
\draw (420.87,224.4) node [anchor=north] [inner sep=0.75pt]  [font=\small]  {$0$};


\end{tikzpicture}
    \caption{Graphical representation of the neighborhood function}
    \label{fig:neigh func}
\end{figure}

We can see this playing the same role as it did in the single variable case. As $\varepsilon$ tends to zero, the functional approaches the minimum/maximum value (shown in the RHS of \autoref{fig:neigh func})! The calculus of variations has some special terminology to define these properties. The special values where $J$ is minimized are called \textit{stationary points}. We call them this because we can add some $\varepsilon\eta(x)$ and they are unchanged! For values that are not the stationary points of $J$, this condition does not hold.\endnote{When we later consider Lagrangian and Hamiltonian mechanics, it actually does not matter if the point we are considering is a minimum, maximum, or just a point where the derivative goes flat. So long as the point is stationary, we can still guarantee the solution is correct.}

Typically we call $\eta(x)$ the \gls{neigh func}. The function $\eta(x)$ must meet the condition that it has zero value at the endpoints of the integral, $x_1$ and $x_2$. That is, $\eta$ must vanish at the endpoints. Otherwise, there is no guarantee that $\varepsilon\eta(x)$ will be correct, since the endpoints of the functional integral may take on some finite value. 

We often compactly write $y(x)+\varepsilon\eta(x)$ as $y(\varepsilon,x)$. We define the condition $y(0,x)\equiv y(x)$, where $y(x)$ is the function that gives the extrema of $J$. So, we can write $y(\varepsilon,x)$ as:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:y epsilon}
    y(\varepsilon,x)=y(0,x)+\varepsilon\eta(x)
\end{equation}
The other important condition is that both $y(x)$ and $\eta(x)$ must have a continuous first derivative so that $y'(\varepsilon,x)$ can be defined:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:y prime epsilon}
    y'(\varepsilon,x)=\frac{dy(0,x)}{dx}+\varepsilon\frac{d\eta}{dx}
\end{equation}
Now, we have all of the components that we need to evaluate our original equation for $J$ in terms of $y(\varepsilon,x)$.

\subsubsection{The First Variation}\label{sec: first variation}
The goal of defining the first variation is to have a concept of a first derivative for functionals. We can set such a derivative equal to zero in order to find stationary points of the system. For the first variation, we'll consider a fixed value of $\eta$ and change $\varepsilon$ to derive something analogous to the derivative. We'll remember that we can write the function $y(\varepsilon,x)=y(0,x)+\varepsilon\eta(x)$. Let's consider the function of this:
$$J(y+\varepsilon\eta)=J(y)+\delta J(y,\eta)\varepsilon+\mathcal{O}(\varepsilon)$$
This is, in essence, a Taylor series expansion. We define the variation nearly the same as the standard derivative:
$$\delta J(y,\eta)=\lim_{\varepsilon\rightarrow0}\frac{J(y+\varepsilon\eta)-J(y)}{\varepsilon}$$
We call this derivative of a functional a Gateaux derivative, and it does not depend on the choice of the norm. If we do choose a norm, we will get a slightly different first variation, but we will not need that until we study optimal control.\endnote{That derivative, dependent on the choice of the norm, is the Fr\'echet derivative.} We can represent the Gateaux derivative using the chain rule:
$$\delta J=\frac{\partial J}{\partial \varepsilon}d\varepsilon$$
The Gateaux derivative looks very much like a directional derivative and in fact is one.

Finally, we know that our problem will have a solution when the variation is zero. That is the point where the Gateaux derivative is zero, and therefore we have a stationary point of the functional.
$$\delta J(y,\eta)=0$$
We now have all the necessary background to develop one of the most important results of the calculus of variations, the Euler-Lagrange equation.

\section{Derivation of Euler-Lagrange Equation} 

We have all of the key ingredients to derive the Euler-Lagrange equation already. We'll consider a functional of the form \eqautoref{eq:functional}. To remove equation clutter, I have made the dependence of $J$ on $y$ implicit.
\begin{equation}\label{eq:neighborhood functional}
    J(\varepsilon)=\int_{x_1}^{x_2}f\left[y(\varepsilon,x),y'(\varepsilon,x);x\right]{dx}
\end{equation}
We can now find the extrema using the familiar calculus rule, setting the derivative of $J$ equal to zero. We also evaluate this condition $\varepsilon=0$ since we defined the extrema to exist at $y(0,x)$:
\begin{equation}
    \left.\frac{\partial J(\varepsilon)}{\partial\varepsilon}\right\vert_{\varepsilon=0}=0
\end{equation}
Applying this to \eqautoref{eq:functional}, we arrive at
\begin{equation}\label{eq:neighborhood functional diff}
    \frac{\partial J(\varepsilon)}{\partial\varepsilon}= \frac{\partial}{\partial\varepsilon}\int_{x_1}^{x_2}f\left[y(0,x),y'(0,x);x\right]{dx}
\end{equation}
The $\varepsilon$ terms drop out from the RHS from the evaluation of $\varepsilon=0$. Applying the Leibniz integral rule, the partial derivative can be moved inside the RHS integral:
\begin{equation}
    \frac{\partial J(\varepsilon)}{\partial\varepsilon}= \int_{x_1}^{x_2}\frac{\partial}{\partial\varepsilon}f\left[y(x),y'(x);x\right]{dx}
\end{equation}
Applying the rules of implicit integration on the integrand, we get:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:diff functional}
    \frac{\partial J(\varepsilon)}{\partial\varepsilon}= \int_{x_1}^{x_2}f\left(\frac{\partial f}{\partial y}\frac{\partial y}{\partial\varepsilon}+\frac{\partial f}{\partial y'}\frac{\partial y'}{\partial\varepsilon}\right){dx}
\end{equation}
Note that we still must take the partial derivative with respect to $\varepsilon$ on the RHS, even though we have plugged in 0 for $\varepsilon$. This is because these are still functions of $\varepsilon$, even after plugging in values of 0.

Next, we can find expressions for $\frac{\partial y}{\partial \varepsilon}$ and $\frac{\partial y'}{\partial \varepsilon}$. This is done with \eqautoref{eq:y epsilon} and \eqautoref{eq:y prime epsilon}. Starting with the first, we differentiate \eqautoref{eq:y epsilon} with respect to $\varepsilon$:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:y diff epsilon}
    \frac{\partial}{\partial \varepsilon}y(\varepsilon,x)=\frac{\partial}{\partial \varepsilon}\left[y(0,x)+\varepsilon\eta(x)\right]
\end{equation}
Here, $y(0,x)$ is independent of $\varepsilon$ since we define it to occur at value $\varepsilon=0$. So this leaves us with
\begin{equation}\label{eq:diff y diff epsilon}
     \frac{\partial y}{\partial \varepsilon}= \eta(x)
\end{equation}
Doing the same for \eqautoref{eq:y prime epsilon}, we arrive at
\begin{equation}\label{diff y' diff epsilon}
     \frac{\partial y'}{\partial \varepsilon}= \frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x}
\end{equation}
Finally, plugging these back into the original expression for \eqautoref{eq:diff functional}, we get the expression
\begin{equation}\label{eq:diff functional subs}
    \frac{\partial J(\varepsilon)}{\partial\varepsilon}= \int_{x_1}^{x_2}\left(\frac{\partial f}{\partial y}\eta(x)+\frac{\partial f}{\partial y'}\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x}\right){dx}
\end{equation}
Finally, performing integration by parts on the RHS to simplify, we arrive at the final integral expression:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:diff functional final}
    \frac{\partial J(\varepsilon)}{\partial\varepsilon}= \int_{x_1}^{x_2}\left(\frac{\partial f}{\partial y}-\frac{d}{dx}\frac{\partial f}{\partial y'}\right)\eta(x){dx}
\end{equation}
Since we are evaluating the extrema, $\frac{\partial J(\varepsilon)}{\partial\varepsilon}$ becomes 0. This leaves us with the final form of the expression, which will give us the useful result we have been looking for:
\begin{equation}
    0= \int_{x_1}^{x_2}\left(\frac{\partial f}{\partial y}-\frac{d}{dx}\frac{\partial f}{\partial y'}\right)\eta(x){dx}
\end{equation}

Since $\eta(x)$ is an arbitrary function, the only way to guarantee equality is to ensure that the integrand part in parentheses is 0. This gives us the fundamental equation of the calculus of variations, called \gls{Euler Equation}:
\begin{gather}\label{eq: Euler's Diff eq}
\color{boxBlue}\boxed{\color{black}\frac{\partial f}{\partial y}-\frac{d}{dx}\frac{\partial f}{\partial y'}=0}\\
\color{boxBlue}\mathrm{Euler's \ Equation}
\end{gather}
Remember that when we defined $f$, we considered it as a function of $y(x)$, $y'(x)$, and $x$. That is important when we solve \gls{Euler Equation}!

\subsection{Using the First Variation}
In \autoref{sec: first variation}, we considered writing the derivative of a functional using the variation, $\delta$. We may also rewrite \eqautoref{eq:diff functional final} using this notation. After some algebraic manipulation, we find this as:
\begin{equation}
    \delta J= \int_{x_1}^{x_2}\left(\frac{\partial f}{\partial y}-\frac{d}{dx}\frac{\partial f}{\partial y'}\right)\delta y{dx}
\end{equation}
And thus, \eqautoref{eq: Euler's Diff eq} can be written concisely as:
\begin{equation}
    {\color{black}\delta J=0}
\end{equation}
This is no surprise, it's the same thing we've been saying all along. When we nudge by some $\varepsilon$ near a stationary point, the function will not change value. The implications of this statement are far reaching, though. For our use cases in dynamics, we have more useful forms of this equation which will be explored in the following sections.

\section{Using Variational Calculus}
A vast majority of the use cases of variational calculus are solving optimization problems. For this purpose, we will use \gls{Euler Equation} extensively. One example where \gls{Euler Equation} is quite useful is in finding the shortest path between two points on a given surface.

\subsection{Geodesics}
We will explore a short example of its usefulness here as it relates to problems in geometry - namely of the \glspl{geodesic} of spherical geometries.

\begin{example}[Geodesics of a Sphere]\label{ex:geodesic}
To show an example of the use of variational calculus, we will consider finding the shortest path of a spherical surface. We know from intuition that this path is a great circle. Let us prove this mathematically with variational calculus:

To start, we need to define what an arc length on a spherical surface looks like. In general, we have the formula for arc length that $ds^2=dx^2+dy^2+dz^2$. We can use the standard spherical coordinates:
    $$\begin{array}{l}
        x=r\sin\theta\cos\theta\\
        y=r\sin\theta\sin\phi\\
        z=r\cos\phi
    \end{array}$$
Some long and arduous mathematics gives the following for the arc length:
$$ds=\sqrt{r^2d\theta^2+r^2\sin^2\theta d\phi^2}$$
At this point, we want to make two assumptions to get this into a form that we can easily evaluate. Since we are only looking for a \gls{geodesic}, we can assume that $r=1$ without losing any information. Next, we want to put our solution in terms of just one variable - either $d\theta$ or $d\phi$. In this case, I choose $d\theta$. This means that we parameterize our path with $\phi=\phi(\theta)$. Taking this derivative, we have:
$$\phi'=\frac{d\phi}{d\theta}$$
This gives ${\phi'}^2=\frac{d^2\phi}{d\theta^2}$. Rewriting with these new terms, we have an expression for $ds$:
$$ds=\sqrt{1+\sin^2\theta{\phi'}^2}d\theta$$
To get the total arc length, we simply integrate this path over the desired path.
$$S=\int_{\theta_1}^{\theta_2}{\sqrt{1+\sin^2\theta{\phi'}^2}d\theta}$$

Up to here, the problem has been mostly set-up. Now, we apply the variational principles to find the minimum. In this case, $S$ is our functional which should be minimized to give the minimum length, that is, to find $\delta S=0$.

We take our function $f$ to be $\sqrt{1+\sin^2\theta{\phi'}^2}$ and apply the Euler equation:
$$\frac{\partial f}{\partial \phi}=\frac{d}{d\theta}\frac{\partial f}{\partial \phi'}$$

Note that $\phi$ has replaced $y$ in the form presented in \eqautoref{eq: Euler's Diff eq}, $\phi'$ has replaced $y'$, and $\theta$ has replaced $x$. The fundamental nature of the equation is identical.

Now, we simply perform the calculus to find our answer:
$$\frac{\partial f}{\partial \phi}=\frac{\partial}{\partial\phi}\left(\sqrt{1+\sin^2\theta{\phi'}^2}\right)=0$$
We find that this first expression is 0 since there are no terms containing $\phi$ explicitly. This means that the derivative of $\frac{\partial f}{\partial \phi'}$, $\frac{d}{d\theta}\frac{\partial f}{\partial \phi'}$, must be constant with respect to $\theta$ so that its derivative is 0. We will use this fact below. We establish the whole term as:

$$\frac{d}{d\theta}\frac{\partial f}{\partial \phi'}=\frac{d}{d\theta}\left[\frac{\partial}{\partial \phi'}\left(\sqrt{1+\sin^2\theta{\phi'}^2}\right)\right]=0$$
Computing the partial derivative, we have:

\begin{equation}
\frac{\partial}{\partial \phi'}\left(\sqrt{1+\sin^2\theta{\phi'}^2}\right)=\frac{\sin^2\theta\phi'}{\sqrt{1+\sin^2\theta\phi'}}=const.
\end{equation}
We can rearrange the statement to solve for the constant (which I will now call $c$) in terms of $\phi'$, which is simply algebraic manipulation.
\begin{equation}
    \begin{split}
      c &=\frac{\sin^2\theta\phi'}{\sqrt{1+\sin^2\theta\phi'}}\\
\sin^2\theta\phi' &= c\sqrt{1+\sin^2\theta\phi'}\\
\sin^4\theta{\phi'}^2 &= c^2\left(1+\sin^2\theta\phi'\right)\\
c^2 &= {\phi'}^2\sin^2\theta\left(\sin^2{\theta}-c^2\right)\\
{\phi'}&=\frac{c}{\sin\theta\left(\sin^2\theta-c^2\right)}
    \end{split}
\end{equation}
We have been using the implicit assumption that $\phi$ is a function of $\theta$, so we finally integrate this with respect to $\theta$ to attain the final \gls{geodesic} curve in terms of $\phi$ and $\theta$.

$$\phi=\int{\phi'}=\int{\frac{c}{\sin\theta\left(\sin^2\theta-c^2\right)}}$$
After looking at the problem for a long time, it becomes apparent that we should perform u-substitution with $u=\cot\theta$. Evaluation of this integral can easily be done with MATLAB or Mathematica (there is no useful information gleaned from doing this integral by hand). Doing so yields: $$\cot{\theta}=a\cos{(\phi-\phi_0)}$$
Where $\phi_0$ comes from the integration constant. This is our final answer. We can solve for $a$ and $\phi_0$ using numerical methods since the equation is transcendental.

A MATLAB script which shows such \glspl{geodesic} is included in the script "SphereGeodesic" in the GitHub (linked in \autoref{app:code}). The output of the script is shown in \autoref{fig:Geodesic}.

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=.75\linewidth]{Scripts/Geodesic.png}
    \caption{Geodesic shown on the Earth}
    \label{fig:Geodesic}
\end{figure}

For a short path length where the distance is much smaller than the sphere radius $r$, we expect that this will return the standard straight line as the shortest distance.

Performing a Taylor expansion and keeping only the first-order terms:
$$\frac{1}{\theta}=a$$
So, it is seen that the value $\theta$, as expected, is constant for a short path length. This formula thus returns the standard equation of a line on short scales!

One important consequence of this is that a spherical geometry, when looked at `closely' (that is, looking at an infinitesimal portion of the surface) is locally Euclidean! This fact that the geometry looks locally Euclidean will be important in our later discussions in \autoref{chap:lie theory}.
\end{example}
 
\subsection{Revisiting the Brachistochrone}

\begin{example}[Brachistochrone]\label{ex:brachistrochrone}
    Let us return to the example of the \gls{brachistochrone} to demonstrate the usefulness of the calculus of variations in solving  this problem. Of course, we have seen John Bernoulli's solution to this problem, but let's explore the more conventional derivation here.

    We start with the same basic idea. We know that the total time for the trajectory is given by $v=\frac{s}{t}$. We can rearrange this equation and use the integral form:
    $$t=\int_1^2\frac{ds}{v}$$
    As in our previous example, we may use energy conservation to see that the velocity is a function of $x$ only (we are using the same coordinate conventions as \autoref{fig:Brachistochrone Bernoulli}) : $v(x)=\sqrt{2gx}$.  We may also parameterize our path $ds$ in a similar way. 
    \begin{equation}
        ds=\sqrt{dx^2+dy^2}=\sqrt{1+y'(x)^2}dx
    \end{equation}
    Combining these two formulas gives us a complete functional for the \gls{brachistochrone} problem:
    $$t=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2g}}\int_1^2\frac{\sqrt{1+y'(x)^2}}{\sqrt{x}}dx$$
    Once again, we have made the equation amenable to expression via the \gls{Euler Equation}. Let's consider the integrand as our function $f$. We can see it follows the standard form\endnote{Notice that $y(x)$ doesn't actually present itself in the equation. That's no problem, but it does imply that the physical location of the entire system has no effect on the outcome -- something we call a symmetry. This is an important idea to highlight and we'll come back to it many times. }:
    $$f=f[y(x),y'(x);x]=\frac{\sqrt{1+y'(x)^2}}{\sqrt{x}}$$
    Now, we can simply apply the \gls{Euler Equation} to find the criteria for which this system has a minimum:
    $$\frac{\partial f}{\partial x}=\frac{d}{dx}\frac{\partial f}{\partial y'}$$
    We may notice immediately that the system has no $y(x)$, so the LHS is 0. Equivalently, the derivative of the RHS must be constant. This leaves us with:$$c=\frac{\partial}{\partial y'}\left(\frac{\sqrt{1+y'(x)^2}}{\sqrt{x}}\right)$$
    After going through the calculations, we find:$$y'(x)=\sqrt{\frac{x}{2c-x}}$$
    We can perform a final integration to get our curve $y(x)$\endnote{
    This integral takes the form $$y(x)=\int\sqrt{\frac{x}{2c-x}}dx$$
    We use a substitution with $x=c(1-\cos\theta)$, which gives us the integral as:
    $$y(x)=c\int(1-\cos\theta)d\theta=c(\theta-\sin\theta)+c_1$$} (see the endnote for details on the integration). This gives us the final parametric curve:
\begin{gather}
        y=c(\theta-\sin\theta)\\
        x=c(1-\cos\theta)
    \end{gather}
\end{example}

From our two examples, we can see some general continuity in our solution methods. We may briefly outline our approach:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Construct an integral which describes the total `cost' or criteria which we want to optimize.
    \item Rearrange our equation to be amenable to \gls{Euler Equation} (write our function in terms of $y(x), y'(x), x$).
    \item Solve the \gls{Euler Equation} for the system to find the stationary points.
\end{enumerate}

With these examples completed, we have enough understanding to embark on our journey with Lagrangian mechanics. There are still a few lingering questions with the calculus of variations, but we may handle those cases directly in the required context, where the physicality of the problems will aid us in our understanding.

\section{Practice Problems}
\begin{exercise}
    Show that the path $y=y(x)$ for which the integral $$\int_{x_1}^{x_2}x\sqrt{1-y'^2}dx$$ is stationary is the $\sinh^{-1}$ function.
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}
    Consider two points in the plane. Find the equation for a general line segment, and show that the shortest path must be a straight line.
\end{exercise}


\printendnotes

\chapter{Lagrangian Mechanics}\label{chap:lagrangian}

\begin{chapquote}{Richard Feynman}
 ``Two formulations of a theory might give exactly the same predictions, and yet, not be psychologically identical when trying to move from that base into the unknown.''
\end{chapquote}

Having studied the origins and mathematics of variational calculus, we are at a good spot to start studying the applications of this theory to physical systems. This approach will allow us to use the energy of a system to determine the \glspl{eom}. Energy, being a scalar quantity, is often easier to handle than the vectors in Newtonian dynamics. Especially as our coordinate systems become more complex, handling things from the standpoint of energy rather than vectors is particularly advantageous. As a brief note, some topics in this chapter were also covered in Volume I. There are many additional topics and they are all covered at a deeper level, but some repetition exists for the sake of continuity.

There are two main types of energy mechanics, referred to as \gls{Hamiltonian} and \gls{Lagrangian} Mechanics. In this chapter, we will focus on the \gls{Lagrangian} approach to mechanics. \gls{Hamiltonian} mechanics will be discussed further in \autoref{chap:hamiltonian}. Both approaches use energy, but have distinct differences in implementation. Generally, the \gls{Lagrangian} approach is more easy to start with.

A careful study of \gls{Lagrangian} dynamics will help us understand the principles behind 6-\gls{dof} modeling more deeply, and also allow us to understand control theory and other principles to be introduced later in this book. For the preview of this theory in fundamental physics, please see \autoref{chap:lagrange continued}.

When we discuss \gls{numerical int}, we can use \glspl{symplectic int} when our problem has a \gls{Hamiltonian} description. This will be further discussed in \autoref{sec: Syplectic Ints}. You may find that certain \gls{eom} derivations are much easier with energy mechanics. I have tried to point out such examples with the practice problems in this chapter. Another reason to motivate these systems is that these energy methods are invariant to transformations such as rotations. Energy methods also employ a generalized coordinate system, which are more powerful and allow us to describe a huge variety of systems.

A brief overview of the differences between the two schemes of \gls{eom} derivation is shown in \autoref{fig:newt v lang}. The underlying governing mechanics are no different, but the way in which we arrive at the \gls{eom} is completely different. Knowing this, we can begin our dive into \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics.

\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering

\end{figure}

\section{The Optical-Mechanical Duality}

We have seen in \autoref{sec: historical background} that there is some connection between optical phenomena and mechanics, which Bernoulli exploited to great success in order to find the brachistochrone solution curve. In the past sections, we have seen that light obeys this `least time' principle, which allows us to understand the optics of systems.

Hamilton\endnote{It is difficult to not be confused about Hamilton's presence in energy mechanics. Hamilton's Principle and the Optical Mechanical Duality are principles that relate to \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics, not the Hamiltonian mechanics. Hamilton, like Euler, was such a prolific mathematician that he has too much named after him.}, the next great mind to enter our story, thought that this connection between the optical and mechanical systems was not a mere coincidence.\endnote{The order of events presented here is not exactly the same as the historical order. The history of physics is muddy and I don't want to confuse the mathematics with intervening history. Hamilton's ideas came after the original discovery of energy mechanics, but it is a nice way to introduce \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics.} Perhaps, if he could create an analogy between the mechanical and optical systems, a more general principle would be revealed.

Hamilton started by considering the optical scenario which we have previously imagined. In the past, we have treated light primarily like particles. Let us start there, and construct a general picture according to Fermat's least time principle. We may imagine a point source of light in a medium of constant index of refraction (like air) or vacuum. At a certain time, we will turn the point light source on. Let's consider a snapshot of time after the light has been turned on. As we can imagine, the light will propagate out from the source and each particle will have the same distance from the light source, each having gone in a straight line:

Let's  now consider the wave description of the light. We have seen before that the descriptions with either particles or waves should produce identical descriptions of the light path.\endnote{To a good approximation, at least. We assume that $\lambda$ is small compared to the path length in this example. Since no diffraction or interference occurs in this example, both descriptions are identical.}

% Text Node

     \caption{Wavefronts of light from a point source}
     \label{fig:wavefront}
 \end{figure}
In this case, we see that the waves emanate outwards radially from the source. We may imagine circles around the light source which describe how far light may travel in a certain amount of time. We call such circles \textit{wavefronts}. Importantly, these wavefronts are always orthogonal to the light rays! This is one reason that the Newtonian description of light meshes well with the descriptions of Huygens in most cases. Fermat's Least time principle describes both.

What is interesting is that knowing the wavefront, we know the trajectory that any particle would take. We simply follow the path that is orthogonal to the wavefront at every point. In this way, the wavefront is a more powerful description, as it describes every path that a particle could conceivably take. If we know where we want our light path to start and end, the wavefront will describe a \textit{unique} trajectory through this space.

Hamilton's key insight is that this same idea can be applied to mechanical systems. Our wavefront is now some physical quantity that we call the \gls{action}, and the ray describes the path through some state space. Suppose we start at some point $A$ in our state space, and end at point $B$. We may use the same analogy as before to understand the path by which this may occur.

\todo{Redo this figure}

One important thing to note is that this mechanical realization is not physical, but rather an abstraction of the optical system. The key insight, however, is that if we can find these surfaces of constant action, we can also find these `rays' through phase space that give us our solution to a problem in mechanics. Such a description is a key insight into the formulation of Hamilton's Principle and elucidates the shift from the Newtonian to the \gls{Lagrangian} mindset.\footnote{ See \cite{abide_by_reason_how_2025} for more information.}

\section{Hamilton's Principle}
With a solid foundation set, we may now proceed in explaining Hamilton's principle, the key insight in using variational calculus to solve problems in mechanics. We recall that similar to Fermat's principle of least time, Hamilton's principle states that the \gls{action} must be minimized. With this in mind, Hamilton formulated the following equation:

\begin{theorem}[Hamilton's Principle]
The physical path that is followed is the path which minimizes the time integral of the difference between the kinetic and potential energies.
$$\delta \int_{t_1}^{t_2}{T-U}{dt}=0$$
\end{theorem}

The quantity $T-U$, the kinetic energy minus the potential energy, is a quantity called the \gls{Lagrangian}, denoted $L$. 
\begin{equation}
    L\equiv T-U
\end{equation}
The \gls{Lagrangian} typically has no physical meaning (although it certainly has units of energy). Instead, we simply choose a \gls{Lagrangian} such that the laws of mechanics work. The reason for the negative sign on the potential, $U$, can be seen in the derivation in \autoref{equiv newt and lang}. In short, the negative must be present so that the \gls{Lagrangian} matches the predictions of the Newtonian formulation.

Before we get too ahead of ourselves, though, we should investigate this integral a bit further. We can notice that the form of the integral is exactly similar to the equations we have seen in the calculus of variations.

In fact, we can notice that the potential energy, generally, is a function of the position, $y$, and the kinetic energy is a function of $y'$ -- the time derivative of the position. Going forward with mechanics and controls, the systems are a function of time instead of a function of $x$. Additionally, it's more common to use $x$ instead of $y$ since our independent variable is now time. Unfortunately, both of these forms are quite common depending on which source you read (mathematicians seem to prefer $y(x)$ and engineers/physicists the $x(t)$ notation). From here on, I will use the more standard $x(t)$ convention used in control theory / physics. Thus, we will instead be integrating over $t$ and our integrand will look like $f[x(t),x'(t);t]$. As a result, we can write our \gls{Lagrangian} quite generally as:
$$\int_{t_1}^{t_2}L[x(t),x(t)';t]dt$$
This, of course, matches the definition for the functional that we described in \autoref{chap:calculus of variations}, so we may use those same principles to solve this equation.

\newglossaryentry{princ of least action}
{
    name=principle of least action,
    first = {\textit{principle of least action}},
    description={A form of Hamilton's Principle that states that the action of a system should be minimized in a physical mechanical system},
    see={action}
}
In the case of \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics, the functional is called the \gls{action}, denoted $S$. This is often why Hamilton's Principle is also called the \gls{princ of least action}.  We can thus equivalently write the integral as:
\begin{equation}
    S=\int_{t_1}^{t_2}{L}{dt}
\end{equation}
So, our \gls{action} takes the form of the \gls{functional} from the calculus of variations. From Hamilton's principle, this \gls{functional}, the \gls{action}, must be minimized. So, we often see the compact form of Hamilton's Principle as:
$$\delta S=0$$
We know from our previous studies that the extremal conditions of a functional can be found using the \gls{Euler Equation}. Substituting in the variables that we use for the \gls{Lagrangian}, we have the \glspl{euler lagrange}, which are used to find the extremal solutions in each component $i$ of the system\endnote{Note that we have only considered the case of a single variable. However, we can easily generalize this to the situation of $i$ variables, resulting in $i$ \glspl{euler lagrange}. By vectorizing the inputs, we can easily show this holds by considering the dot product as the multiplication.}:
\begin{gather}\label{eq: Euler-Lagrange}
    \color{boxBlue}\boxed{\color{black}\frac{\partial L}{\partial x^i}-\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{x}^i}=0,\quad i=1,2,\cdots,n}\\ \mathrm{\color{boxBlue}Euler-Lagrange \ Equations}
\end{gather}
 Hamilton's Principle then states that the solutions to this \gls{euler lagrange} are the \gls{eom} of the system! Remember, in the calculus of variations problems, $x^i$ and $\dot{x}^i$ are treated as different variables.

 \subsubsection{Mathematic Aside: Covariant and Contravariant Vectors}
In the previous section, we saw that the positions and velocity vectors for our system have their index as a superscript $(x^i,\dot{x}^i$). As we will see later, there are also vectors which will have their index as a subscript $(p_i,\lambda_i)$. The difference between these two is important and deserves a short explanation.

Consider the following scenario: An engineer at NASA is working on a satellite. They have a position vector which describes the difference between the center of rotation and the location of a certain sensor. As a proper engineer, they express this distance in the metric system as $\bm{x}=(5,2.5) \ \text{cm}$ in the satellite body frame. However, one of the contractors on the project (the super-conglomerate Northgreed Martin) insists on using inches instead of centimeters. In such units, the new $\bm{x}$ vector is approximately $\bm{x}\approx(2,1) \ \text{in}$. This situation is depicted in \autoref{fig:coordinate update scaling}.
\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering

\tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

\begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-1,xscale=1]
%uncomment if require: \path (0,300); %set diagram left start at 0, and has height of 300

%Straight Lines [id:da15126370416320367] 
\draw    (100,130) -- (117,130) ;
\draw [shift={(120,130)}, rotate = 180] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;
%Straight Lines [id:da9371436517769267] 
\draw    (100,130) -- (117,130) ;
\draw [shift={(120,130)}, rotate = 180] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;
%Straight Lines [id:da9716520349207857] 
\draw    (100,130) -- (100,113) ;
\draw [shift={(100,110)}, rotate = 90] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;
%Straight Lines [id:da6258151349028135] 
\draw    (240,130) -- (337.32,81.34) ;
\draw [shift={(340,80)}, rotate = 153.43] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;
%Straight Lines [id:da319086438444822] 
\draw    (240,130) -- (287,130) ;
\draw [shift={(290,130)}, rotate = 180] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;
%Straight Lines [id:da9840941612188078] 
\draw    (240,130) -- (240,83) ;
\draw [shift={(240,80)}, rotate = 90] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;
%Straight Lines [id:da19407740426602338] 
\draw    (100,130) -- (197.32,81.34) ;
\draw [shift={(200,80)}, rotate = 153.43] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;

% Text Node
\draw (102,142.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$1\ \mathrm{cm}$};
% Text Node
\draw (242,142.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$1\ \mathrm{in}$};


\end{tikzpicture}

    \caption{Change in vector under an axis scaling}
    \label{fig:coordinate update scaling}
\end{figure}
Notice that when we increase the length of the basis vectors, the representation of the vector becomes a smaller number. Of course, the vector is still identical. Only our representation of it has changed under this new coordinate system. Since our vector components change `counter' to the change in the basis vectors, such a vector is called `contravariant'. Position and velocity are examples of such contravariant vectors. We will give these quantities a superscript index.

Covariant vectors (sometimes called covectors or 1-forms) behave in the opposite way. Consider, for example, how the temperature field changes along the position. We can express this using the gradient:
$$h_i=\frac{\partial T(x^i)}{\partial x^i}$$
Here, $h_i$ are the components that describe the gradient of the temperature. If we consider the same type of scaling transformation that we did before, we see that $h_i$ behaves in the opposite way:
$$T(x^i)\rightarrow T(ax^i), a\approx2.5\text{ for cm to in}$$
It is simple to see how this transformation will give the following via chain rule:
$$ah_i=\frac{\partial T(ax^i)}{\partial x^i}$$
As we see, the partial derivatives / gradient of the system transform in the same way as the basis vectors. For this reason, we call them covariant and give them a lower index, like $p_i$.  For engineering purposes, we can typically consider contravariant quantities to be column vectors and covariant ones to be row vectors.\endnote{In non-Euclidean geometry, this will get us in trouble, as covariant and contravariant vectors can see changes in sign or other issues. But we will work with orthogonal `Cartesian-esque' coordinates most of the time. When the distinction is important, we will use the covariant / contravariant notation.} 

When we discuss the whole collection of components, I will sometimes abbreviate $\{q^j\}_{j=1}^n$ as simply $\bm{q}$. The same will be done for covariant quantities ($\{p_j\}_{j=1}^n\rightarrow\bm{p}$. When the distinction between covariant and contravariant quantities becomes important, the indices will be restored. As a final note, the particular letter is not of any significance. $p_j$, $p_i$, $p_q$, and so on are all the same exact thing. The only thing that matters when writing equations is that we use the same index across different variables when they should be related.

\subsubsection{Potential Limitations of the Lagrangian}
There is one small part of Hamilton's principle that we glossed over during the derivation: the fact that the system must be \gls{conservative}. In short, a \gls{conservative} system is one in which the energy of the particle is path-independent. See \cite{goldstein_classical_2008} for more information. This assumption helped when I assumed that the potential, $U$, was a function of position only. If this potential had a dependence on velocity, then the problem would not be easily solvable. Thus, this potential function will only allow us to understand forces deriving from a potential (like gravity, electromagnetism, etc.). Other forces need to be added in `by-hand'.

This does mean that frictional forces and aerodynamics are not easily modeled with the \gls{Lagrangian} we describe here (although they can be with the utilization of Rayleigh's dissipation function and other techniques, such as those discussed in \cite{rabei_hamiltonian_2004}). This is the reason we do not directly use these equations in the derivation of the rocket 6-DoF.\endnote{However, much of the rotational dynamics we describe can be represented equivalently using the \gls{Lagrangian} approach, so we do use these equations in a roundabout manner.} This is also one reason that we need to use numerical integration methods to solve the \gls{eom} for the rocket trajectory when using Newtonian dynamics. In fact, the idea that the system in non-\gls{conservative} and cannot be solved analytically are intrinsically linked. As a result, for systems that are non-conservative, it is best to stick with the Newtonian method of mechanics, as we do for our 6-DoF, since the methods to find approximate solutions for these equations are quite a bit simpler. 

However, for systems that are frictionless, the methods described here will work perfectly. In addition, we can directly model a time dependence into the equations of the \gls{Lagrangian} which helps us solve some problems involving frictions and drags. I should also note that the concept of friction and air drag is only a macroscopic one. All of the interactions that we regard as friction are fundamentally electromagnetic in origin. Therefore, in fundamental physics, the energy methods of mechanics are almost always suitable methods and are far more suitable. Unfortunately, our 6-\gls{dof} model does not simulate atomic interactions, so us engineers are sometimes stuck with the troglodytic methods of Newton.

With that noted, let us use the \gls{Lagrangian} in a few examples to see how it can turn some complex Newtonian systems into fairly trivial problems.

\begin{example}[Mass Spring System]
Consider a standard mass spring system. We will derive its \gls{eom} using the \gls{Lagrangian} method. We consider the length $x$ to be referenced from the rest length of the spring.

\end{tikzpicture}

    \caption{Mass-Spring System for Lagrangian}
    \label{fig:mass spring lagrange}
\end{figure}

We know the analytical form of the \gls{eom} from Newtonian Dynamics (without external force applied):
$$m\ddot{x}+kx=0$$
So, in essence, we are using the \gls{Lagrangian} \textit{a posteriori} to match this solution. First, we will find the \gls{Lagrangian} of the system. The kinetic energy of the particle is given by the linear translation only (there is no rotational motion for a point particle), so we have $T=\frac{1}{2}m\dot{x}^2$. The potential energy is the energy of the spring, given as $U=\frac{1}{2}kx^2$. So, the \gls{Lagrangian} is:
$$L=\frac{1}{2}m\dot{x}^2-\frac{1}{2}kx^2$$
Now, applying \eqautoref{eq: Euler-Lagrange} in the $\hat{x}$-direction, as this is the only degree of freedom:
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial x}-\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{x}}=0$$
Taking each term individually,
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial x}=-kx$$
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{x}}=m\dot{x}$$
$$\frac{d}{dt}\left(m\dot{x}\right)=m\ddot{x}$$
So, the \eqautoref{eq: Euler-Lagrange} equation gives us:
$$-kx-m\ddot{x}=0$$
A simple change of signs gives the exact same result as the Newtonian approach:
$$m\ddot{x}+kx=0$$
In this problem, we used a simple case where the dynamics were constrained along a single axis of motion. Before we move to a more complex problem, we will introduce the method by which \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics deals with these different reference frames.
\end{example}
\todo{Another example here}

In the last example, the use of Cartesian coordinates made our system dynamics a bit more complicated than was necessary. To alleviate this issue, we will introduce generalized coordinates into the \gls{Lagrangian} framework.

\section{Generalized Coordinates}

We recall that in Newtonian mechanics, we define reference frames with which we describe the positions, velocities, and accelerations for an object. We represent the position, velocity, and acceleration of a particle in terms of these coordinate systems as a tridimensional vector. Generally, we may write the $\vec{r}^{\ op}$  vector in a frame $a$ as:
$$x\hat{a}_1+y\hat{a}_2+z\hat{a}_3$$
Because any tridimensional set of coordinates that we choose that are linearly independent of each other will span $\mathbb{R}^3$, we can generalize this to any reference frame, $q$, where the terms of $a$ can be described by the terms of $q$.
$$x\left(q^1,q^2,q^3\right)+y\left(q^1,q^2,q^3\right)+z\left(q^1,q^2,q^3\right)$$
Once again, we can use the contravariant shorthand for a generalized coordinate. We will write this full set of $q$, $\left(q^1,q^2,\cdots q^n\right)$, as $\bm{q}$.

We can also generalize this to the velocity and the acceleration. The components of velocity can be written in terms of $q^j, \dot{q}^j$ and those for acceleration in terms of $q^j, \dot{q}^j, \ddot{q}^j$.  When we do Newtonian dynamics, we need to use the \gls{bke} to transform these quantities if they are not in an \gls{inertial frame}. Not so with the \gls{Lagrangian}! We are free to choose whatever $q$'s we want, inertial or not.\endnote{The coordinates themselves are allowed to be non-inertial. We must be careful, however, that when we write our \gls{Lagrangian} as $L=T-U$, we do so in an inertial frame.}

\subsection{Lagrange's Equation in Generalized Coordinates}

The good thing about the \gls{Lagrangian} method is that it is invariant to changes in our coordinate system. Because the \gls{Lagrangian} is an equation of energy, and energy is independent of coordinate transformations, we can easily express the \gls{euler lagrange} in generalized coordinates with little hassle.\endnote{I note here that this explanation is not strictly correct, since our \gls{Lagrangian} does not have to be coordinate lengths. Instead, the configuration space has special properties that make it invariant to such transformations. Deriving the \gls{Lagrangian} from Hamilton's principle ensures that this is always true, which the intrepid reader may prove to themselves by studying differential geometry and Lagrangians on manifolds. Further information will be provided in \autoref{chap:lie theory} although we will not strictly cover this.} 

Using the generalized coordinates, the kinetic energy is a function of the generalized positions, velocities, and time. The potential is a function of the generalized position and time, so the \gls{Lagrangian} becomes:
$$L\equiv T-U=T(q^j,\dot{q}^j,t)-U(q^j,t)$$
Stated another way, the \gls{Lagrangian} is a function of the generalized positions, velocities, and time, as $L=L(q^j,\dot{q}^j,t)$. Since the \gls{Lagrangian} is a scalar, nothing important changes when using generalized coordinates. We simply replace the appropriate letters. The \glspl{euler lagrange} then become:
\begin{gather}\label{eq:Euler-Lagrange General}
\color{boxBlue}\boxed{\color{black}\frac{\partial L}{\partial q^j}=\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}^j},\quad j=1,2,\cdots M}\\
\mathrm{\color{boxBlue}Generalized \ Euler-Lagrange \ Equations}
\end{gather}
Notice the similarity of this equation to Newton's 2nd law, $F=\frac{dp}{dt}$. These two equations are so similar, in fact, that we can give names to each of the terms. On the left, we have something which looks like the force (specifically, a force deriving from a potential):
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial q^j}=j\text{th }\text{`potential force'}$$
and on the right, we have what looks like the time derivative of the momentum, so:
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial\dot{q}^j}=j\text{th generalized momentum conjugate}$$
We can think of these terms as \textit{analogous} to the ones in Newton's 2nd law, but not always precisely the same. The fact that they are not precisely the same is what gives us an immense advantage in describing a large variety of systems.

\subsubsection{Gauge Invariance}
\todo{Update this section with the appropriate indices}
Now that we have generalized Euler-Lagrange equations, there is one implication that is very important for physics. The fact is that our the result of the Euler-Lagrange equations will be the same for the following two \glspl{Lagrangian}:

$$L(\bm{q},\bm{\dot{q}},t)\rightarrow L(\bm{q},\bm{\dot{q}},t)+\frac{d f(\bm{q},t)}{dt}$$
Here, $f(bm{q},t)$ is an arbitrary function of $\bm{q}$ and $t$. All we have to do to show that this is true is to see that the this term will vanish when we apply the Euler-Lagrange equations. To help us do this, it is useful to rewrite the total derivative explicitly:
$$L(\bm{q},\bm{\dot{q}},t)\rightarrow L(\bm{q},\bm{\dot{q}},t)+\left(\frac{\partial f(\bm{q},t)}{\partial q^j}\frac{dq^j}{dt}+\frac{\partial f(\bm{q},t)}{\partial t}\frac{dt}{dt}\right)$$
Which can be rewritten as:
$$L(\bm{q},\bm{\dot{q}},t)+\left(\frac{\partial f(\bm{q},t)}{\partial q^j}\dot{q}^j+\frac{\partial f(\bm{q},t)}{\partial t}\right)$$
We can now input this \gls{Lagrangian} into the Euler-Lagrange equations and find the following:
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial q^j}+\frac{\partial^2 f(\bm{q},t)}{\partial q^j\partial t}=\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}^j}+\frac{\partial^2 f(\bm{q},t)}{\partial q^j\partial t}$$
Since the term is present on both sides of the equation, we can subtract it from both sides and end up with the original equation! It turns out that the equations or motion are invariant under the addition of such a term. This type of invariance is called a \textit{gauge invariance}. Let's keep this idea in the back of our minds as we move forward with describing constraints in the \gls{Lagrangian} approach.

\subsection{Constraints}\label{sec:constaints}
For a single particle, we can describe its position with the 3 coordinates. When multiple particles are involved, we may need to increase this number of coordinates. As a very general statement, we can say for $N$ particles, we will need $n$ generalized coordinates. In other words, the position of an arbitrary set of particles is given in terms of $n$ generalized coordinates. To find a precise number for a given system, we turn our attention to constraints.

We can imagine constraints as a condition that restricts the motion of a body in some way. A body which has complete freedom to move translationally and rotate in any way is free. When a constraint is applied, this freedom is reduced. Often, we see these constraints denoted with either an equality or an inequality. These constraints, most often, are functions of the position, velocity, and/or time. We see constraints written most often in the form:
$$f(\bm{r},\bm{v},t)=0$$
This form may seem abstract, but an example of a constraint is that a length is fixed. Such a constraint looks like:
$$r-L=0$$
which encodes the fact that the distance $r$ between two particles is a constant length $L$. The powerful thing about constraints in \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics is that we only need to know the way in which a particle is constrained, not the force or analytical description of this constraint. In addition, the imposition of a constraint does not change how we solve a problem! It is a wonderful fact that the \gls{euler lagrange} equations will still provide the physical trajectory even when an external constraint changes the motion of the system! A concise proof of this fact is in \autoref{lagran constraint}.

\subsubsection{Holonomic Constraints}

The first class of constraints that we deal with are those which are only a function of the generalized positions, and not a function of velocity.

To contextualize this, we take the example of a system of $N$ particles. For each particle, we need 3 values to define the position, so we need $3N$ generalized coordinates. However, we often have constraints that reduce this number. Often, we see a problem that is planar or where the particles are connected in a \gls{rigid body}. These constraints will reduce the number of generalized coordinates needed. These examples only constrain the allowable positions. There is no imposition on the velocity of particles in the system. Thus, these constraints on the positions of particles are \glspl{holonomic const}. 

A \gls{holonomic const} relates generalized coordinates to each other. In the commonly used notation, the \gls{holonomic const} is expressed in terms of the $q^j$'s as:
$$\Phi_k\left(q^j,t\right)=0, \ k=1\cdots K$$
or in terms of the position vectors:
$$f_k\left(\bm{r}^{op_1},\bm{r}^{op_2}\cdots \bm{r}^{op_N},t\right)=0$$
The number of \glspl{holonomic const}, $K$, will change the number of generalized coordinates. A holonomic constraint on a system directly removes a degree of freedom from the system. This makes holonomic constraints straightforward to deal with. Mathematically, we can represent the change in the number of degrees of freedom due to \glspl{holonomic const} by:
\begin{gather}\label{eq:dof}
\color{boxBlue}\boxed{\color{black}\mathcal{M}=3N-K}\color{black}
\end{gather}

The quantity $\mathcal{M}$ is the number of \gls{dof}. This is the number of \gls{dof} for the whole system, which is why it can exceed 6 in many cases!

\subsubsection{Particle on a Path}
Suppose we have a single particle that is constrained to motion along a path in the $xy$-plane. This path is given by $y=g(x)$.

    \centering

\draw [shift={(340,140)}, rotate = 135] [color={rgb, 255:red, 208; green, 2; blue, 27 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][line width=0.75]    (10.93,-3.29) .. controls (6.95,-1.4) and (3.31,-0.3) .. (0,0) .. controls (3.31,0.3) and (6.95,1.4) .. (10.93,3.29)   ;
\draw [shift={(280,200)}, rotate = 315] [color={rgb, 255:red, 208; green, 2; blue, 27 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 208; green, 2; blue, 27 }  ,fill opacity=1 ][line width=0.75]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 3.35, y radius= 3.35]   ;
%Shape: Circle [id:dp7406066990608903] 
\draw  [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ,fill opacity=1 ] (338.33,140) .. controls (338.33,139.08) and (339.08,138.33) .. (340,138.33) .. controls (340.92,138.33) and (341.67,139.08) .. (341.67,140) .. controls (341.67,140.92) and (340.92,141.67) .. (340,141.67) .. controls (339.08,141.67) and (338.33,140.92) .. (338.33,140) -- cycle ;

% Text Node
\draw (433,190.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$x$};
% Text Node
\draw (277,22.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$y$};
% Text Node
\draw (209,256.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$z$};
% Text Node
\draw (282,203.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$O$};
% Text Node
\draw (337,112.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$P$};
% Text Node
\draw (420,72.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,opacity=1 ]  {$y=g( x)$};
% Text Node
\draw (321,162.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [color={rgb, 255:red, 208; green, 2; blue, 27 }  ,opacity=1 ]  {$\vec{r}^{\ op}$};

\end{tikzpicture}

    \caption{Particle Moving on Constrained Path}
    \label{fig:constrainted path}
\end{figure}
We can select our generalized coordinates to be the same as the cartesian coordinates, so that:
\begin{gather}
    q^1=x\\q^2=y\\q^3=z
\end{gather}
With no other information, this is likely the best choice of generalized coordinates. However, we can start applying the constraints to simplify the equation. The first constraint is that the motion is confined to the $xy$-plane. Thus, 
$$q^3=z=0$$
Next, we have the path constraint which is given by $y=g(x)$. We can write this as a \gls{holonomic const}:
\begin{gather}
    q^2-g(x)=0
\end{gather}
With these two constraints, we have reduced the number of degrees of freedom, $\mathcal{M}$, to 1. This makes sense intuitively, as the particle can only move along the path specified by the equation $g(x)$, therefore only having one degree of freedom.

In this example, we could describe that single degree of freedom with our coordinate, $q^1=x$. The power of using generalized coordinates is that we could choose a coordinate that more easily defines this system. Knowing that we only have one degree of freedom, our one coordinate could be the arc length along the path, $s$, defined from some starting point. Such a parameterization would make the velocity of the bead along the path trivially easy to calculate.

In general, these generalized coordinates do not even need to have units of length! We can select any parameters that are most convenient for defining the state, even those that are energies or Fourier coefficients, so long as we can define a continuous derivative of that state and use the \gls{euler lagrange}. 

\subsubsection{Rigid Body Constraints}\label{sec: rigid body constraints}
For most of the systems that we will deal with, including our rocket, we make the assumption that particles are rigidly attached to each other, meaning that there is no change in distance between particles. Using the notation of constraints described above, we will show how this is done for a two particle system, which can then naturally be extended with more particles.

Given two particles that are connected together by a massless rigid rod of length L, we have a constraint on the particles' distance:
$$\left\Vert \bm{r}^{op_1}-\bm{r}^{op_2}\right\Vert=L$$
Written in the more standard notation:
$$f\left(\bm{r}^{op_1},\bm{r}^{ op_2}\right)=\left\|\bm{r}^{op_1}-\bm{r}^{ op_2}\right\|-L=0$$
So this constraint takes the 6-\gls{dof} for the two particles and reduces it to 5-\gls{dof}. Intuitively, because the system is symmetric about the line between the two particles, there is one less degree of freedom. An example of the generalized coordinates describing this system would be the position of the first particle in Cartesian coordinates (giving 3 of the generalized coordinates, $x,y,z$), and the azimuthal and elevation angle, $\phi$ and $\theta$. The roll angle does not change the system in any measurable way, hence we only need 5 parameters to define a two particle system.

In the general case of $N$ particles composing a \gls{rigid body}, we have $3N$-\gls{dof} in a completely unconstrained system. However, we must constrain each pair of particles. An example of this for the 3 particle system in shown in \autoref{fig:rigid body 3 particle}.

\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering
 

\tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

\begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-1,xscale=1]
%uncomment if require: \path (0,300); %set diagram left start at 0, and has height of 300

%Shape: Circle [id:dp11919774335444377] 
\draw  [fill={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,fill opacity=0.59 ] (120,135) .. controls (120,121.19) and (131.19,110) .. (145,110) .. controls (158.81,110) and (170,121.19) .. (170,135) .. controls (170,148.81) and (158.81,160) .. (145,160) .. controls (131.19,160) and (120,148.81) .. (120,135) -- cycle ;
%Shape: Circle [id:dp1512759538865308] 
\draw  [fill={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,fill opacity=0.59 ] (360,235) .. controls (360,221.19) and (371.19,210) .. (385,210) .. controls (398.81,210) and (410,221.19) .. (410,235) .. controls (410,248.81) and (398.81,260) .. (385,260) .. controls (371.19,260) and (360,248.81) .. (360,235) -- cycle ;
%Shape: Circle [id:dp4443678325062712] 
\draw  [fill={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,fill opacity=0.59 ] (380,65) .. controls (380,51.19) and (391.19,40) .. (405,40) .. controls (418.81,40) and (430,51.19) .. (430,65) .. controls (430,78.81) and (418.81,90) .. (405,90) .. controls (391.19,90) and (380,78.81) .. (380,65) -- cycle ;
%Straight Lines [id:da963502184755707] 
\draw    (170,130) -- (380,70) ;
%Straight Lines [id:da5114822387415651] 
\draw    (167.27,146.68) -- (361.67,226.68) ;
%Straight Lines [id:da3674207943033927] 
\draw    (405,90) -- (389.67,209.88) ;

% Text Node
\draw (145,135) node    {$m_{1}$};
% Text Node
\draw (385,235) node    {$m_{3}$};
% Text Node
\draw (405,65) node    {$m_{2}$};
% Text Node
\draw (91,125.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$\vec{r}_{1}$};
% Text Node
\draw (421,242.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$\vec{r}_{3}$};
% Text Node
\draw (351,25.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$\vec{r}_{2}$};
% Text Node
\draw (229,65.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$|\vec{r}_{1} -\vec{r}_{2} |$};
% Text Node
\draw (215.4,202.2) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$|\vec{r}_{1} -\vec{r}_{3} |$};
% Text Node
\draw (402.6,136.6) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$|\vec{r}_{2} -\vec{r}_{3} |$};

\end{tikzpicture}

    \caption{3 Particle Rigid Body}
    \label{fig:rigid body 3 particle}
\end{figure}

So, we have 3 constraint equations in a system with 9 particles. This gives a total of $3N-3=6$-\gls{dof}. When any additional particles are added, they must be fully constrained in all 3 translational degrees of freedom by constraints between $r^1$,$r^2$, and $r^3$. So, we always have constraints of the form $3N-3(N-2)$ for a \gls{rigid body}. This is why we always see a \gls{rigid body} displaying 6-\gls{dof}, independent of the number of particles which comprise it. We can then, of course, parametrize this in general coordinates with the cartesian coordinates and 3 angles (or another parameterization of the attitude).

\subsubsection{Minimal Set of Constraints}

\newglossaryentry{holonomic sys}
{
    name=holonomic system,
    first = {\textit{holonomic system}},
    description={A system that is entirely composed of holonomic constraints},
    see={holonomic const}
}
So far, we have only discussed holonomic constraints, those that are related to position or on the generalized parameters, and possibly have some relation in time. When a system is a \gls{holonomic sys}, that is, purely defined by \glspl{holonomic const}, it is always possible to find a set of $\mathcal{M}$ generalized parameters to describe the particle motion. In other words, the minimum number of parameters is equivalent to the number of degrees of freedom in the system.

That said, we often choose to use more parameters, particularly in the case of rotations. As we see in Volume II, we often find it more useful to use a non-minimal set of constraints to encode rotations to avoid singularities in the solution.

It is also common to see a non-minimal set of constraints used when the minimal set is cumbersome and does not give an intuitive feel to the problem. There is no strict requirement to use the minimal set of constraints for a problem, but you should be aware of the number of parameters required to describe the minimal set.
\subsubsection{Non-Holonomic Constraints}

\newglossaryentry{non holo const}
{
    name=non-holonomic constraint,
    first = {\textit{non-holonomic constraint}},
    description={A constraint that depends on more than the position of the particles in the system},
    see={holonomic const}
}
The other class of constraints are those constraints that are not merely a function of the generalized coordinates. These constraints, called \glspl{non holo const}, define the set of all constraints that depend on more than just the position of particles. This is a huge class of constraints, so we only cover specific cases here.

\newglossaryentry{gen vel}
{
    name=generalized velocity,
    first = {\textit{generalized velocity}},
    plural = {generalized velocities},
    firstplural = {\textit{generalized velocities}},
    description={A free direction of movement for a vehicle. These can be translational or rotational directions}
}

There is one type of constraint which we see very commonly and is also somewhat simple to deal with. These are constraints on the \glspl{gen vel}. Such an example of a constraint on \glspl{gen vel} is the `ice skater' or `rover' model. In such a system, the velocity is restricted to be parallel to the current heading. The direction may only change through an acceleration in an orthogonal direction. 
\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering



\tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

\begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-1,xscale=1]
%uncomment if require: \path (0,300); %set diagram left start at 0, and has height of 300

%Curve Lines [id:da6205809353166649] 
\draw [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][line width=1.5]    (73,152.7) .. controls (215,64.7) and (236,202.7) .. (380,154.7) ;
%Straight Lines [id:da22679868240630552] 
\draw    (161,122.7) -- (222,123.65) ;
\draw [shift={(225,123.7)}, rotate = 180.9] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;
%Straight Lines [id:da24653510278861845] 
\draw    (229,139.7) -- (293.28,169.44) ;
\draw [shift={(296,170.7)}, rotate = 204.83] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;
%Straight Lines [id:da3083687218610517] 
\draw    (380,154.7) -- (434.15,136.65) ;
\draw [shift={(437,135.7)}, rotate = 161.57] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;

% Text Node
\draw (236,112.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$\bm{v}$};


\end{tikzpicture}


    \caption{An example of a system with a constraint on the velocity}
    \label{fig:placeholder}
\end{figure}
We could describe the equations of motion for such a system:
\begin{align}
    \dot{x}&=v\cos\theta\\
    \dot{y}&=v\sin\theta\\
    \dot{\theta}&=\omega
\end{align}
The non-holonomic constraint is implicit in the fact that $\dot{x}$ and $\dot{y}$ will always be in the directions associated with $\theta$, the current heading. Since constraints on the generalized velocity are typically easy to deal with, these are sometimes called semi-holonomic\index{semi-holonomic} constraints. However, they still classify as non-holonomic.

One important thing about \glspl{non holo const} is that they do not reduce the number of parameters needed to define the system. Additionally, there is no general way to solve systems which have non-holonomic constraints. Semi-holonomic constraints sometimes have a special geometry which lets us solve for them, but they are still non-trivial to deal with. Later, we will look at a few such cases which can be solved. For now, we will concern ourselves mostly with systems consisting of holonomic constraints. These are far easier to deal with and will be the main point of discussion for the sake of brevity.

It may seem that this limits us greatly, but in fact, this is not the case for most physics problems! When we are considering large systems, this may be quite limiting, but when considering small scale physics, such as atomic problems, we often find that \glspl{non holo const} are the exception, not the rule. Most systems are conservative and have constraints only on positions. As such, most of our modern physics theories can use \glspl{Lagrangian}.

In the context of engineering problems, we can sometimes make derivations under the assumption of holonomic constraints and then use empirical data and correlations where needed to find suitable solutions (when friction or other factors are present). In other cases, we simply stick to the Newtonian regime and employ the approaches detailed in Volume I.


\section{Pendulum EOM Example}
\subsubsection{Problem Set-Up}
Suppose we have a pendulum of length L. The mass is rigidly attached to the pendulum. A reference frame $a$ that moves with the pendulum is shown in \autoref{fig:Pendulum}. The \gls{inertial frame} is also shown. The pendulum moves in only the $xy$ plane.
\begin{figure}[ht]

\centering
