\draw (481.25,191.58) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [color={rgb, 255:red, 208; green, 2; blue, 27 }  ,opacity=1 ,rotate=-330]  {$\hat{a}_{\theta }$};
% Text Node
\draw (467.54,261.84) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [color={rgb, 255:red, 208; green, 2; blue, 27 }  ,opacity=1 ,rotate=-330]  {$\hat{a}_{r}$};

\end{tikzpicture}

    \caption{Pendulum Set-Up}
    \label{fig:Pendulum}
\end{figure}
Our goal here is to approach the problem in several different ways to gain insight into the benefits and drawbacks of each method in a practical setting.

\subsubsection{Newtonian Approach}
You have likely seen the Newtonian approach many times, but we show it here for completeness and to contrast the approach with the \gls{Lagrangian} more directly. 

In the Newtonian Approach to deriving EOM's, we always start with the forces governing the system. In this case, we have two forces. The gravitational force, $mg$, acts in the $-\hat{y}$-direction and the tension force acts in the $-\hat{a}_r$-direction. From the constraint on length, the acceleration in the $\hat{a}_r$ must be zero. Thus, the component of gravity in the $\hat{a}_r$ direction must equal the tension.

We express the gravity in the $a$-frame to find this:
$$\hat{y}=-\cos\theta\hat{a}_r+\sin\theta \hat{a}_\theta$$
So, by balancing the forces in the $\hat{a}_r$-direction:
$$m{\ddot{a}}_r=mg\cos\theta \hat{a}_r-\vec{F}_T$$
In the $\hat{a}_\theta$-direction, we have unconstrained motion, so $\sum\vec{F}=m\vec{a}$. Our force equation is thus: 
$$F=-mg\sin\theta\hat{a}_{\theta}$$
Next, we need to find our acceleration in the \gls{inertial frame} using the \gls{bke} to solve the RHS of the force balance. Starting with the position vector:
$$\bm{r}^{op}=L\hat{a}_r$$
We can apply the \gls{bke} to find our inertial velocity:
$${}^i\bm{v}^p={}^a\frac{d}{dt}\bm{r}^{op}+{}^i\bm{\omega}^a\times\bm{r}^{op}$$
Plugging in appropriate quantities:
$${}^i\bm{v}^p={}^a\frac{d}{dt}(L\hat{a}_r)+\dot{\theta}\hat{a}_3\times(L\hat{a}_r)$$
$${}^i\bm{v}^p=\dot{\theta}L\hat{a}_\theta$$
We do this again to find the inertial acceleration:
$${}^i\bm{a}^p={}^a\frac{d}{dt}{}^i\bm{v}^p+{}^i\bm{\omega}^a\times{}^i\bm{v}^p$$
$${}^i\vec{a}^p={}^a\frac{d}{dt}{}\dot{\theta}L\hat{a}_\theta+\dot{\theta}\hat{a}_3\times\dot{\theta}L\hat{a}_\theta$$
$${}^i\vec{a}^p=\ddot{\theta}L\hat{a}_\theta-\dot{\theta}^2L\hat{a}_r$$
Now, we equate the forces in each direction:
$$-mg\sin\theta\hat{a}_\theta=\ddot{\theta}L\hat{a}_\theta$$
Rearranging, we arrive at our \gls{eom}:
$$\ddot{\theta}+\frac{g}{L}\sin\theta=0$$
\subsubsection{Lagrangian Approach}
In the \gls{Lagrangian} approach, we will start by defining our constraints and coordinates. Some preliminary analysis shows that using polar coordinates for this problem will be useful.\endnote{One way in which we can determine this is that the system has no explicit dependence on time under the choice of polar coordinates. We would call such a selection \textit{natural} or \textit{scleronomous} as a result.} If we do not recognize this, we can always convert later (a distinct advantage of the \gls{Lagrangian} approach, since it is coordinate frame invariant). Here we will choose a cylindrical coordinate system:
\begin{gather}
    q_1=r\\q_2=\theta\\q_3=z
\end{gather}
Next, we define constraints. Since there is no motion in the z-axis:
$$z=0$$
And the constraint on the length gives us:
$$q^1-L=0$$
So, we have 1 \gls{dof} from \eqautoref{eq:dof}. Since both $z$ and $r$ are constrained, we can express our \gls{eom} in terms of one generalized coordinate, $\theta$.

Next, we define the \gls{Lagrangian}. Knowing that the linear velocity of the particle is $\dot{\theta}L$, we get:
$$T=\frac{1}{2}m\dot{\theta}^2L^2$$
For the potential, we choose the origin as our reference, giving us:
$$U=-mgy$$
And a coordinate transformation into polar using $y=r\cos\theta$ yields:
$$U=-mgL\cos\theta$$
This gives the \gls{Lagrangian}:
$$L=\frac{1}{2}m\dot{\theta}^2L^2+mgL\cos\theta$$
We can then apply the Euler-Lagrange equations on the single generalized coordinate:
\begin{gather}
    \frac{\partial L}{\partial \theta}=-mgL\sin\theta\\
    \frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{\theta}}=mL^2\dot{\theta}\\
    \frac{d}{dt}\left(mL^2\dot{\theta}\right)=mL^2\ddot{\theta}
\end{gather}
So, the final system is:
$$-mgL\cos\theta -mL^2\ddot{\theta}=0$$
With some rearrangement:
$$\ddot{\theta}+\frac{g}{L}\sin\theta=0$$
We notice that with the \gls{Lagrangian} approach, we did not need to have an understanding of the vector directions, frame translations, or \gls{bke} to find our \gls{eom}. This, in addition to the flexibility of the generalized coordinates, is the real power of this approach.

\begin{exercise}[Lagrangian in Code]\label{ex:lagrangiancode}
Sometimes computing the derivatives in the Euler-Lagrange equation can be a bit tedious. Using MATLAB or Python, create a function which takes in symbolic expressions for $L$ and the $q_j$ terms and outputs the \glspl{eom} of the system. Test your code on the mass-spring system and the pendulum shown above.
\end{exercise}


\section{Lagrange Multipliers}
In our discussion of constraints, there was a discussion between holonomic and non-holonomic constraints. As we further progress our knowledge, it should be stated that the key difference between these two in actually solving problems is that holonomic constraints are \textit{integrable}, while non-holonomic constraints generally are not.

Sometimes, this integrability can be tricky, though. This is the case when two or more parameters must abide by some special relationship or constraint. When this is the case, we deliberately maintain a full set of coordinates instead of eliminating them (using generalized coordinates). This procedure gives us additional information in the force which is causing the constrained motion. In the example of the bead on the wire, we may employ the method of Lagrange multipliers to find the force acting on the wire. Therefore, we could decide the necessary thickness of wire for a given trajectory (or whatever else we would like to do with the forces).

A classic example of such a constraint is the standard pendulum system. Typically, we would use polar coordinates for the system so that the constraint is:
$$r-L=0$$
However, if we use the standard Cartesian basis, we instead have a constraint which depends on both $x$ and $y$:
$$\sqrt{x^2+y^2}-L=0$$
Using the method of Lagrange multipliers, we may find the suitable relationship between $x$ and $y$ and also determine the tension in the pendulum. Using the standard \gls{Lagrangian}, we have two Euler-Lagrange equations. We can write them both at the same time:\endnote{We could also write both in the same integral, it is mathematically equivalent.}
$$\delta S=\int\left({\frac{\partial L}{\partial x}-\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{x}}}\right)\delta xdt+\int\left({\frac{\partial L}{\partial y}-\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{y}}}\right)\delta ydt$$
This is the same as our standard result. The trick is that we must include our constraint equation in the mix.  Specifically, we want the variation of the function $f(x,y)=\sqrt{x^2+y^2}-l=0$. We have seen that the \textit{Gateaux} derivative will look nearly identical to the standard derivative for the smooth functions we are concerned with. Therefore, we find the first variation $\delta f$ to be:
$$\delta f=\frac{\partial f}{\partial x}\delta x+\frac{\partial f}{\partial y}\delta y=0$$
There is an important point to highlight here, and it is the crux of this methodology. Because the first variation is equal to zero, we are free to multiply it by any function and the equality will hold. This condition holds for any $f(x,y)=\text{const.}$ Therefore, we will add an additional term $\lambda(t)$ -- the Lagrange multiplier. We can then add this term into the integrand for the first variation of the action:
$$\delta S=\int\left({\frac{\partial L}{\partial x}+\lambda(t)\frac{\partial f}{\partial x}-\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{x}}}\right)\delta xdt+\int\left({\frac{\partial L}{\partial y}+\lambda(t)\frac{\partial f}{\partial y}-\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{y}}}\right)\delta ydt$$
The key point here is that we now have a system with three unknown functions -- $x,y$ and $\lambda$ -- but also three equations when we include $f(x,y)=\text{const}$. We first choose a $\lambda(t)$ such that the first part of the LHS abides by the following:
$${\frac{\partial L}{\partial x}+\lambda(t)\frac{\partial f}{\partial x}=\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{x}}}$$
In this circumstance, since the integrand is always zero, the relationship between $\delta x$ and $\delta y$, however complicated, will not matter. We guarantee that the first part of the integrand will be zero nonetheless. As a result, the second half can be independently solved:
$${\frac{\partial L}{\partial y}+\lambda(t)\frac{\partial f}{\partial y}=\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{y}}}$$
And our last equation is the constraint. In the case of our pendulum:
$$f(x,y)=\sqrt{x^2+y^2}=l$$

Let's remember how we related this equation to Newton's 2nd law:
$$\underbrace{\frac{\partial L}{\partial y}}_\text{pot. force}+\lambda(t)\frac{\partial f}{\partial y}=\underbrace{\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{y}}}_{\frac{d}{dt}\text{momentum}}$$
In our standard case, the time derivative of the momentum is the force derived from a potential. Here, we have a new term, which must be the force derived from a constraint. This tells us that:
$$F^\text{cstr}_j=\lambda(t)\frac{\partial f}{\partial q^j}$$
This constraint force is a covariant quantity. Since $\lambda$ describes a geometric invariance in the system (by our construction), it is natural that the resulting constraint force will change in a covariant manner.

\subsubsection{Constraint force for a Pendulum}
Let's consider our pendulum example with the constraint given by
$$f(x,y)={x^2+y^2}-l^2=0$$
This is the square of what we have used previously, but since the equation is equal to 0 we are free to manipulate it in any way. We can write the modified \gls{Lagrangian} with the addition of the constraint equation:
$$L=\frac{1}{2}m(\dot{x}^2+\dot{y}^2)+\lambda(x^2+y^2-l^2)-mgy$$
We know that the force of constraint is given by:
$$F^\text{cstr}_j=\lambda\frac{\partial f}{\partial q^j}$$
As a result, we can find the constraint forces in the $x$ and $y$ directions as:
\begin{gather}
    F^\text{cstr}_x=\lambda\frac{\partial f}{\partial x}=2\lambda x\\
    F^\text{cstr}_y=\lambda\frac{\partial f}{\partial y}=2\lambda y
\end{gather}
To solve the system, we use the full Euler-Lagrange equations. For this system, we find that the result is:
\begin{align}
    m\ddot{x} &= 2\lambda x \\
    m\ddot{y} &= 2\lambda y-mg
\end{align}
From here, we simply solve this set of equations in any way we like. This one is simple enough to easily solve by hand. First we compute the second derivative of our constraint:
$$2\dot{x}^2+2x\ddot{x}+2\dot{y}^2+2y\ddot{y}=0$$
Now, we plug in the results of the Euler-Lagrange equation to find the solutions:
$$2\dot{x}^2+2x\frac{2\lambda x}{m}+2\dot{y}^2+2y\left(\frac{2\lambda y}{m}-g\right)=0$$
We can divide out the factor of two and expand the equation to get:
$$\dot{x}^2+\dot{y}^2+\frac{2\lambda}{m}\left(x^2+y^2\right)-gy=0$$
We can substitute $l^2$ and $v^2$ for $(x^2+y^2)$ and $(\dot{x}^2+\dot{y}^2)$, respectively. Then, we find that:
$$\lambda=\frac{mgy-mv^2}{2l^2}$$
From which it is easy to find the tension in the rod by plugging in appropriate values.

\subsection{Kinetic Energy Generalization}
In the previous two examples that we explored, the functions for $T$ and $U$ were quite simple because the transformations from the generalized coordinates were quite simple and could be understood from intuition. In the case that they are not as simple, we would previously have to use \gls{bke} to find transformations or employ some other method. It would be useful if we had a general formula to express $T$ and $U$ in the generalized coordinates. We know that the kinetic energy is simply the following:
$$T=\frac{1}{2}\sum_im_i({v^i})^2$$
We can more explicitly define $v^i$ to be $\dot{r}_i$, with $r$ being the vector in Cartesian coordinates. The kinetic energy in terms of $\dot{r}$ is thus:
$$T=\frac{1}{2}\sum_im_i(\dot{r}^i\cdot\dot{r}^i)$$
As we have shown before, $r^i$ can generally be represented in terms of the generalized positions, $q^j$:
$$r^i=r^i(q^j)$$
As a result, $\dot{r}$ can be expressed in terms of the generalized positions and velocities using the chain rule (it is worth doing this derivation yourself!):
$$\dot{r}=\frac{dr}{dt}=\sum_k{\frac{\partial r^i}{\partial q^k}\dot{q}^k+\frac{\partial r^i}{\partial t}}$$
Combining these two formulas, it is quite easy to see that we have the following for a general formula of the kinetic energy which converts between $r$ and the generalized coordinates:\footnote{See \cite{goldstein_classical_2008} Chapter 1.6 for more information on this derivation.}
$$T=\frac{1}{2}\sum_im_i\left(\sum_j\frac{\partial\vec{r}^i}{\partial q^j}\dot{q}^j+\frac{\partial \vec{r}^i}{\partial t}\right)^2$$
This is a `snake-proof' way in which we can derive the kinetic energy of the system, and it will account for any issues that may arise from differing coordinate systems or conventions.

\subsubsection{Free Particle in Polar Coordinates}
We can show this equation in work with a simple example of a single free particle. We express the motion in polar coordinates in order to showcase the use of the formula. We need only know the transformations between the coordinate systems.

We define the two cartesian coordinates of interest $x$ and $y$ such that $r=\sqrt{x^2+y^2}$. The two \gls{Lagrangian} coordinates are $r$ and $\theta$ since we have assumed polar coordinates. We are free, of course, to choose anything. The one fact we need to complete the derivation is that 
\begin{gather}
    x=r\cos\theta\\
    y=r\sin\theta
\end{gather}
Of course, for a more general problem, there are methods to derive these relations if they are more complex, such as the Jacobian matrix.

Since we are only considering a single particle in this example, the sum over $i$ for different masses need not be considered. We thus have:
$$T=\frac{1}{2}m\left[\left(\frac{\partial r^1}{\partial r}\dot{r}+\frac{\partial r^1}{\partial t}\right)^2+\left(\frac{\partial r^1}{\partial \theta}\dot{\theta}+\frac{\partial r^1}{\partial t}\right)^2\right]$$
We can quite easily eliminate the term $\frac{\partial r}{\partial t}$, since nothing has explicit dependence on time in our problem. From here, the problem is simply `bookkeeping' to ensure that all of the partial derivatives are performed properly. Expressing $r_1 = (r\cos\theta,r\sin\theta)$:
$$\begin{array}{l}
\dfrac{\partial}{\partial r}r^1=(\cos\theta,\sin\theta)\\
\\
    \dfrac{\partial}{\partial \theta}r^1=(-r\sin\theta,r\cos\theta)
\end{array}$$
Plugging back in, we have:
$$T=\frac{1}{2}m\left[\left(\dot{r}\cos\theta-r\sin\theta\dot{\theta}\right)^2+\left(\dot{r}\sin\theta-r\cos\theta\dot{\theta}\right)^2\right]$$
Upon computation of the cross terms, we find that they cancel and are left with:
$$T=\frac{1}{2}m\left[\dot{r}^2+r^2\dot{\theta}^2\right]$$
Which matches the Newtonian result and is generally applicable to many cases. More of this approach will be seen later in the case of the inverted pendulum.


\section{Cyclic Coordinates and Conservation}
The \gls{Lagrangian} formulation of mechanics can give us some special insight into the quantities of a problem that are conserved. This is, once again, one of the distinct epistemic advantages over the Newtonian approach. In the Newtonian scheme, we can learn to recognize when systems will have conserved quantities, but there is no satisfactory analysis we can perform beforehand to ensure this. On a deeper level, Newton's laws do not predict conservation laws, but the \gls{Lagrangian} formalism includes them explicitly. Foundationally, we know that any equations or physics derived from a \gls{Lagrangian} will contain certain conservation laws. These ideas about conservation ultimately stem from what we call \textit{Noether's Theorem}.\footnote{Emmy Noether, 20th century mathematician who studied abstract algebra and symmetries.}

\begin{theorem}[Noether's Theorem]
In a system that can be modeled purely by a Lagrangian (that is, a conservative system), every continuous symmetry has a corresponding conservation law.
\end{theorem}
The implications of this statement are quite great. Noether's Theorem gives us a pathway to understand where the conservation laws actually come from. Of course, we can also reverse the statement and understand the symmetries of our system based on the conserved quantities! If you are curious what exactly is meant by a \textit{continuous symmetry}, more information is to come in \autoref{sec:groups and syms}.

Before we start applying this theorem, we should concretely define the terminology of the theorem. What exactly do we mean by a conserved quantity?

A conserved quantity is simply one that does not change over time. When we say that energy is conserved, we mean that the energy of the system will be exactly the same if observed at $t=0\ \mathrm{s}$ or $t=100\ \mathrm{s}$. And an important point is that it must also stay constant for any time \textit{between} 0 and 100. If we call our quantity of interest $X$, we can express this as:
$$\frac{dX}{dt}=0$$
Another way of saying the same thing is that we can shift the system by any $dt$ and the quantity $X$ is \textit{invariant} to such a change.

\subsection{Symmetries in Physics}

Symmetries in physics can be a bit different from the symmetries you might typically imagine -- such as the reflectional symmetry of a triangle. There are, of course, physical symmetries of a system that we leverage when solving a problem, such as spherical symmetry or axial symmetry. Our discussion here is somewhat distinct and more generic than those types of symmetries.

When we discuss a symmetry in physics, we mean to say that the physics that act upon that system are in no way altered by whatever transformation we have performed (that is to say, the \gls{eom} is identical). For example, if we decide to move our rocket launch 20 ft to the north, the aerodynamics, gravity, and forces of the rocket are still exactly the same.\endnote{To good approximation, at least. Ignore different winds and gravity and such.} We have performed a transformation -- in the form of moving the launch location -- but there are no observable differences to the dynamics of the system!

Of course, these transformations are not merely limited to physical movements of the system, there are other types of transformations that we can do that do not impact the physics of the system. We will be best informed by an example:

\begin{example}[Rolling Wheel]
Let us consider a system consisting of a wheel which is rolling without slipping along a level surface, as shown in \autoref{fig:roll wheel}:

(381.22,119.98) -- (330,160) -- cycle ; \draw    (323.21,95.35) .. controls (325.44,95.12) and (327.71,95) .. (330,95) .. controls (349.76,95) and (367.47,103.82) .. (379.39,117.74) ; \draw [shift={(381.22,119.98)}, rotate = 226.89] [fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.08]  [draw opacity=0] (8.93,-4.29) -- (0,0) -- (8.93,4.29) -- cycle    ;

% Text Node

% Text Node

\end{tikzpicture}
    \caption{Rolling Wheel}
    \label{fig:roll wheel}
\end{figure}
The \gls{Lagrangian} for such a system is quite simple. Since the center of mass is aligned with the zero potential height, we can disregard $V$ and only consider the kinetic energy $T$. The kinetic energy is given as the combination of the rotational and translational motions:
$$L=T=\frac{1}{2}\left(m\dot{x}^2+I\dot{\theta}^2\right)$$
The value of $I$ will change depending on the mass distribution of the rolling object, but the exact value of $I$ is unimportant here. We can now consider the Euler-Lagrange equation for each coordinate, taking $x$ first:
$$\frac{d}{dt}\left(\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{x}}\right)=\frac{\partial L}{\partial x}$$
The partial with respect to $x$ yields $0$ since the coordinate $x$ appears nowhere in the \gls{Lagrangian}. When this is the case -- that a variable does not appear directly in the \gls{Lagrangian} -- it is \textit{cyclic}. Whenever a coordinate is cyclic, its partial derivative is 0.

Thus, we can see that $\frac{d}{dt}\left(\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{x}}\right)=0$ and thus $\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{x}}=\mathrm{const.}$ The result of this is that the linear momentum of the system is conserved, since it is constant! This is one possible result of Noether's Theorem -- that linear momentum is conserved when the \gls{Lagrangian} is independent of position. \endnote{More precisely, the theorem states that the \gls{Lagrangian} must be invariant under a continuous transformation.} Using our generalized language, the generalized momentum is invariant when the generalized coordinate does not appear in the \gls{Lagrangian}. We can apply the same reasoning to the $\theta$ component, and just reword it:
$$I\dot{\theta}=\mathrm{const.}$$
Which is the statement that the angular momentum of the system is conserved.
\end{example}

Before we move on the mathematical formulation and proof of Noether's Theorem, it would be prudent to consider a few more key points. Firstly, we should clarify a key point: A certain system may or may not conserve a quantity, but the laws of physics as a whole will conserve that quantity. That is to say, the conservation of energy, momentum, charge, etc. is a fact of the universe\endnote{For the pedants, in an expanding universe the total energy is indeed increasing. Aerospace engineers (and most physicists barring cosmologists) need not worry about this.}; for any given system these quantities are not guaranteed to be conserved due to specific constraints on the system.

We should also discuss the types of conserved quantities we expect to appear in a system. The following relations are typical:
\begin{align}
       \text{time invariant system}&\Leftrightarrow\text{Conservation of Energy}\\
    \text{spatially invariant system}&\Leftrightarrow  \text{Conservation of Momentum}\\
    \text{rotationally invariant system}&\Leftrightarrow \text{Conservation of Angular Momentum}
\end{align}
In classical mechanics, these invariances are the most common. However, there are more symmetries that occur, and they are very fundamental in our understanding of the universe and how forces arise. To understand these symmetries requires some physics and group theory beyond the scope of this volume.\footnote{See \cite{zee_quantum_2025} for a good introductory text explaining some of these symmetries in quantum mechanics, special relativity, and other contexts.}

\subsection{The Mathematical Formulation of Symmetry}
We know from Hamilton's Principle that physical trajectories have an action that is an extremal value. We denote this action as $S$, and the path it follows as $\gamma$, so we may write $S[\gamma]=0$. It may be possible to find another path $S[\gamma_2]=0$ somewhere else along the state space. This is not particularly noteworthy. We can take the analogy of the derivative from calculus as an example in \autoref{fig:calc sym}. Physically, this just means that we could have two paths which both result in an extremal action:
\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering

\tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

\begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-0.7,xscale=0.7]

\draw (354,242.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [color={rgb, 255:red, 218; green, 44; blue, 0 }  ,opacity=1 ]  {$f'=0$};
% Text Node
\draw (249,47.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [color={rgb, 255:red, 218; green, 44; blue, 0 }  ,opacity=1 ]  {$f( x)$};


\end{tikzpicture}

    
    \caption{Calculus Analogy of Discrete Symmetry}
    \label{fig:calc sym}
\end{figure}
Given a general function, it is expected that there may be multiple minima, separated by a large spatial distance. What Noether's Theorem is concerned with is infinitesimal and continuous transformations that do not change the action at all. These are more rare and special. To denote such paths, let's consider a small (infinitesimal) transformation $T$, which maps path $\gamma$ to $\gamma'$:
$$\gamma\xrightarrow{T}\gamma'$$
When we have a continuous symmetry between these two, the final result must be the same for a symmetry to exist.\endnote{Physicists often consider more complex cases involving fields or other generalizations, in which something called the \textit{Noether current} is a conserved quantity. We will only deal with the simple case in classical mechanics here.} We can understand conservation of linear momentum in a system quite easily in the case of the \gls{Lagrangian}. In fact, we have already seen it in some of our examples. We'll solidify the idea here. Consider a continuous transformation of the coordinates. For simplicity, we will consider only one coordinate:
$$q\rightarrow q+\varepsilon\psi$$
This $\varepsilon\psi$ is a small transformation, which is generic. It could be a transformation of the coordinate, or something else. We will keep it general so that we can derive a general result. We require that the change in the \gls{Lagrangian} under such a change is zero. Applying basic calculus on $L$, we find that the small perturbation is given by:

$$\delta L=\frac{\partial L}{\partial q}\varepsilon\psi+\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}}\varepsilon\dot{\psi}=0$$
This comes from the fact that $L$ is a function of $q$ and $\dot{q}$, and the above result shows this. We also remember the Euler-Lagrange equation, which the system must satisfy:
$$\frac{d}{dt}\left(\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}}\right)=\frac{\partial L}{\partial q}$$
Notice that the term $\frac{\partial L}{\partial q}$ is present in the perturbation, so we may plug in the result of the Euler-Lagrange equation to simplify:
$$\delta L=\frac{d}{dt}\left(\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}}\right)\varepsilon\psi+\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}}\varepsilon\dot{\psi}=0$$
As a last trick, we will rewrite the last term explicitly:
$$\delta L=\frac{d}{dt}\left(\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}}\right)\varepsilon\psi+\varepsilon\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}}\frac{d}{dt}\psi=0$$
Notice that this is the product rule, so we may write it more compactly. We can also remove $\varepsilon$ by dividing it out:
$$Q=\frac{d}{dt}\left(\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}}\psi\right)=0$$
Therefore, we have shown that the quantity $Q=\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}}\psi$ is a constant. This is a quantity which is called the \textit{Noether charge}. It is a general result that the Noether charge is conserved. From this, we can see multiple results. If we consider $\psi$ as a simple translation of coordinates, we have $\psi=1$, giving us:
$$ q\rightarrow q+\varepsilon$$
In that case, we simply have:
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}}=\text{const.}$$
Which is the statement that the generalized momentum is conserved when a change in the position results in the same \gls{Lagrangian} (our cyclic / ignorable coordinate). You can try plugging in a few other things for $\psi$ and seeing what kind of symmetries you can dream up, and what those might imply.

It is hard to overstate how important Noether's theorem is for physics. While the final result is not difficult, the implications for physics are quite large. In the wake of this idea, symmetry became a leading principle in physics. In engineering, such ideas are also becoming increasingly important, especially in control system analysis and non-linear system design. We will explore this more deeply in \autoref{part: advanced topics}.

\section{Generalized Forces}
The last major point that we need to consider in our journey through \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics are \textit{generalized  forces}. Not all forces that we encounter derive from a potential, especially large scale emergent forces like friction and drag. Therefore, we would like to have a way to include these forces in \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics. The inclusion of these will allow us to model almost anything we would desire from a \gls{Lagrangian}. We can include our forces that derive from a potential in the standard way, and the non-conservative ones through an explicit generalized force. This will prove useful for a variety of systems, including when we want to apply control with an explicit force.

The addition of generalized forces into the \gls{Lagrangian} is surprisingly simple. We will move all of the standard \gls{Lagrangian} terms to the left hand side:
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial q_j}-\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}_j}=0$$
Then, we will include our generalized force on the right hand side of the equation. It is typical to denote this generalized force as $Q_j$. This is distinct from the Noether charge, which also uses the symbol $Q$. The distinction should be clear in application. The updated equation is simply:
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial q^j}-\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}^j}=Q_j$$
This $Q_j$ term will describe any forces that do not derive from a potential. We find that a generalized force has the following form:
$$Q_j=\sum_i F_i\cdot\frac{\partial r^i}{\partial q^j}$$
From D'Alembert's principle of virtual work (which we will not derive here), we know that as long as the above equation has units of energy / work, we may select a generalized force which has any units. D'Alembert's principle does become important if we want to vary the \gls{Lagrangian} directly to find our equations of motion. In that case, we define the virtual work:
$$\delta W=Q_j\delta q^j$$
Here, the sum over index $j$ is implicit. For a virtual work, the sum over all the indices is always zero:
$$\delta W=Q_j\delta q^j=0$$
As a result, the time integral of the virtual work will also be zero:
$$\int_{t_1}^{t_2}\delta W=0$$
Therefore, we can insert the virtual work into the \gls{Lagrangian} for our system and the optimality condition remains the same.
$$\delta\int_{t_1}^{t_2}Ldt+\int_{t_1}^{t_2}\delta Wdt=\int_{t_1}^{t_2}
(\delta L+Q_j\delta q^j)dt=0$$

\begin{example}[Forced Mass Spring Damper]
We can consider a mass spring damper system subject to an external force. The dynamics will obey the Euler-Lagrange equation with a generalized force:
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial q^j}-\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}^j}=Q_j$$
Here, we only have one degree of freedom, and we may choose the variable to interest to be $x$. Our \gls{Lagrangian} for the system is:
$$L=T-U=\frac{1}{2}m\dot{x}^2-\frac{1}{2}kx^2$$
And our generalized force contains the two non-conservative forces. This includes the forcing function, $u$, as well as the non-conservative damper. We also have $r=x\hat{e}_1$. Therefore:
$$\frac{\partial r^i}{\partial q^j}=\frac{\partial x \hat{e}_1}{\partial x}=\hat{e}_1$$
And we can ignore the summation over $i$ since there is only a single direction of interest. Therefore, we have:
$$Q=(c\dot{x}+u)\hat{e}_1\cdot\hat{e}_1$$
The dot product simplifies trivially in the one dimensional case, leaving us with the following equation:
$$-kx-m\ddot{x}=c\dot{x}+u$$
A small rearrangement of the equation shows that we arrive at the standard form:
$$m\ddot{x}+c\dot{x}+kx=u$$
\end{example}


\section{Lagrangian Rigid Body Dynamics}
Rigid body dynamics are of paramount importance to aerospace engineering applications. Here, we will explore rigid body dynamics in the context of the \gls{Lagrangian}. In Volume I, we explored both the Newtonian method of deriving the Euler rotation equations using the \gls{bke} as well as a simple derivation of the rigid body equations using Euler angles. With our more advanced knowledge, we can derive the general form for any inertia tensor. This will also introduce us to the generalized form of tensor equations, some basic properties of three dimensional rotation spaces, and another approach to solving problems using the direct variation of the \gls{Lagrangian}.

\subsection{Torque Free Equations}
To begin our derivation, we need to consider a new type of quantity in the form of a tensor. In Volume I, we introduced the Inertia tensor as a $3\times3$ matrix, but make no specific mention of it's particular properties. We can construct the inertia tensor by noting that the kinetic energy must be a scalar quantity. We already know the standard form of the rotational kinetic energy:
$$T=\frac{1}{2}I\omega^2$$
Angular velocities exist in $\mathbb{R}^3$ space and behave similar to translational velocity. As a result, $\omega$ is a contravariant quantity. Note that whenever a covariant and contravariant quantity with the same index combine, we get a scalar.\endnote{Or as physicists sometimes like to say, a covector is an object which `eats' a vector and returns a scalar.} Consider the simple case in Cartesian space where the covariant vector is simply a row vector and the contravariant vector is a column:
$$u_iv^i=\begin{bmatrix}
    u_1&u_2&u_3
\end{bmatrix}\begin{bmatrix}
    v^1\\v^2\\v^3
\end{bmatrix}=u_1v^1+u_2v^2+u_3v^3$$
In this notation, the sum over the index $i$ is implied. If the indices were different $(u_iv^j)$, we do \textit{not} get a sum. This implicit sum is known as the Einstein summation notation. In the case of the rotational kinetic energy, it will allow us to construct the inertia tensor. To get a scalar, we require that $I$ has two covariant indices. This new object is a general form for the kinetic energy of a rotating body. Notice that we have made no assumptions about the nature of the $I$ in doing this:
$$T=\frac{1}{2}I_{ij}\omega^i\omega^j$$
We should take a moment to understand what $I_{ij}$ is. As we have heard before, it is a \textit{tensor}. A tensor is a generalization of the covariant and contravariant vectors. A tensor can have any number of indices. A 2-tensor (one with two indices) has four varieties:
$$I_{ij}\quad {I_i}^j\quad {I^i}_j\quad I^{ij}$$
As before, the index location describes whether the quantity associated with that index is covariant or contravariant. The inertia tensor has two covariant components. Tensor equations are preferable to work with because their interpretation is completely independent on our selection of coordinates. If we write a valid tensor equation in one frame, it will work in all reference frames! That seems like a very attractive property for aerospace applications, where we very often have to deal with many different frames and conventions. By deriving equations using the \gls{Lagrangian} and tensors, we can create equations which are completely valid in any frame of reference we might imagine. Imagine doing such a thing with the Newtonian approach! 

With all that said, let's proceed with our derivation. Our \gls{Lagrangian} is exactly the kinetic energy:
$$L=\frac{1}{2}I_{ij}\omega^i\omega^j$$
To find the equations of motion, we vary the \gls{Lagrangian} and set it integral equal to zero. Here, we take our generalized coordinate to be $\bm{\theta}$ and the generalized velocity to be $\bm{\omega}$. Now, we can vary the \gls{Lagrangian}:
$$\delta L=\delta(\frac{1}{2}I_{ij}\omega^i\omega^j)$$
We assume $I_{ij}$ to be a constant such that it's variation vanishes. Then, we can apply the standard rules for the Gateaux derivative:
$$\delta L = \frac{1}{2}I_{ij}\left[(\delta \omega^i)\omega^j+\omega^i(\delta\omega^j)\right]$$
Remember that the inertia tensor is a symmetric tensor, so $I_{ij}=I_{ji}$. Therefore, the variation in the $i$ and variation in $j$ are really the same thing. Therefore, our varied \gls{Lagrangian} becomes:
$$\delta L = I_{ij}\omega^i(\delta\omega^j)$$
Understanding how to expand the variation in $\delta\omega^j$ is slightly tricky because the space of rotations is nonlinear. This space is called $SO(3)$ and we will explore it's properties more deeply in \autoref{chap:rot with lie}. To put it briefly, we can use the \gls{bke} in order to expand this term:
$$\delta\bm{\omega}=\frac{d}{dt}(\delta\bm{\theta})+\bm{\omega}\times\delta\bm{\theta}$$
We want to write this out component wise, which will require that we can express the cross product terms. We have a single symbol which captures all of the sign conventions of the cross product. We call it the Levi-Civita permutation tensor. It is a 3-tensor and is denoted by the $\epsilon$ symbol. Here, we will require the version with one contravariant index and two covariant indices. This gives us the following for the $\delta\omega^i$ terms:
$$\delta\omega^i=\frac{d}{dt}(\delta\theta^i)+{\epsilon^i}_{jk}\omega^j\delta\theta^k$$
\begin{exercise}
Tensor equations require that the free indices (those not being summed) on both sides of the equation match. Confirm for the equation above that:
\begin{itemize}
    \item $i$ is a free index on both sides.
    \item All other indices vanish from the equation.
    \item The index $i$ is contravariant on both sides of the equation.
\end{itemize}
\end{exercise}

Putting this term back into the full \gls{Lagrangian} will give us the following:
$$\delta L = I_{ij}\omega^i\left(\frac{d}{dt}(\delta\theta^j)+{\epsilon^j}_{kl}\omega^k\delta\theta^l\right)$$
The time integral of the varied \gls{Lagrangian} must be zero from Hamilton's principle. We can simply insert this integrand into the integral to find the appropriate condition. Note that we are doing this in lieu of passing this through the Euler-Lagrange equations.
$$\int_{t_1}^{t_2}I_{ij}\omega^i\left(\frac{d}{dt}(\delta\theta^j)+{\epsilon^j}_{kl}\omega^k\delta\theta^l\right)dt=0$$
Our final step is to integrate the term $I_{ij}\omega^i\frac{d}{dt}(\delta\theta^j)$ by parts. Doing so, we find that the integral is now:
$$\int_{t_1}^{t_2}\left(-\frac{d}{dt}(I_{il}\omega^i)+I_{ij}\omega^i{\epsilon^j}_{kl}\omega^k\right)\delta\theta^ldt=0$$
Since the variation in $\delta\theta^l$ is arbitrary, we find that the term in the middle must be zero. Equivalently:
$$\frac{d}{dt}(I_{il}\omega^i)=I_{ij}\omega^i{\epsilon^j}_{kl}\omega^k$$
This is the most general expression of the Euler rigid body equation. Because it derives from a tensor equation, it will hold in any reference frame. Often, we find it useful to write this in vector notation so we may input the equations into simulation software. Remember that the Levi-Civita symbol encodes a cross product. Therefore, we can write the equation in terms of the vectors (assuming $I$ constant):
$$I\bm{\dot{\omega}}=I\bm{\omega}\times\bm{\omega}$$
\begin{exercise}
Show that when the tensor $I_{ij}$ is expressed in a frame where it is a diagonal matrix, $I\bm{\dot{\omega}}=I\bm{\omega}\times\bm{\omega}$ will recover the standard equations of motion:
\begin{gather}
\left(I_{22}-I_{33}\right)\omega^2\omega^3=I_{11}\dot{\omega}^1\\
     \color{black}\left(I_{33}-I_{11}\right)\omega^1\omega^3=I_{22}\dot{\omega}^2\\
     \color{black}\left(I_{11}-I_{22}\right)\omega^1\omega^2=I^3_3\dot{\omega}^3
\end{gather}
\end{exercise}

\subsection{Forced Euler Rigid Body Dynamics}
Forced motion requires the additional of a generalized force. Since such a force does not have to arise from a potential, we will consider it as an external input into the \gls{Lagrangian}. Since we had solved for our equations of motion directly using the variation of the \gls{Lagrangian}, adding an external force requires that we use D'Alembert's principle of virtual work in order to describe our system. In this case, our generalized force is a moment and the generalized coordinate is an angle. Therefore, we have:
$$\delta W=Q_j\delta\theta^j=0$$
Inserting this into our general equation for the D'Alembert-Lagrange action, we have:
$$\int_{t_1}^{t_2}\left(-\frac{d}{dt}(I_{il}\omega^i)+I_{ij}\omega^i{\epsilon^j}_{kl}\omega^k+Q_l\right)\delta\theta^ldt=0$$
As before, the variation in $\delta\theta^l$ is arbitrary and the optimality condition requires that the part of the integrand in paranthesis is neccesarily zero. Therefore, we have a small modification of the equations of motion. We will call the generalized force components an external torque, $\tau_l$.
$$\frac{d}{dt}(I_{il}\omega^i)=I_{ij}\omega^i{\epsilon^j}_{kl}\omega^k+\tau_l$$
Or in the vector form of the equations:
$$I\bm{\dot{\omega}}=I\bm{\omega}\times\bm{\omega}+\bm{\tau}$$
It is quite amazing to see that these equations of motion, which we had taken as a fact in Volume I, can be derived fully from variational principles.

\subsubsection{Final Thoughts}
With the inclusion of generalized forces in the \gls{Lagrangian} framework, we have made a very robust framework for mechanics. In the following chapter, we will build upon our foundation here in more advanced mechanics. Those ideas will propel us forward into advanced physics and control theory, but the \gls{Lagrangian} never loses it's paramount importance. It remains an incredibly robust tool for understanding the dynamics of a huge variety of systems.

\section{Key Ideas}
\begin{enumerate}
    \item We can find the \gls{eom} of a system using either the Newtonian approach or the \gls{Lagrangian} approach, and it is often easier to find an \gls{eom} using the \gls{Lagrangian} for a \gls{conservative} system.
    \item The \gls{Lagrangian} is scalar, so it is invariant to coordinate transforms. This means we can choose any variables we want for our generalized coordinates when performing derivations.
    \item The \gls{Lagrangian} guarantees notions of conservation, which the Newtonian paradigm cannot. It is for this reason that we find it so fundamental in physics.
    \item When our forces do not derive from a potential, we must include generalized forces to understand the equations of motion for the system.
    \item The methodology of Newtonian mechanics and \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics are fundamentally different. Newtonian mechanics deals with the influence of external forces, while \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics emphasizes the internal state of the system.
%add Hamiltonian v. Lagrangian
\end{enumerate}
\section{Further Reading}
The first section borrows from the derivations of Chapter 6 and 7 of  \cite{thornton_classical_nodate} and Chapter 5 of  \cite{cline_variational_2017}. These sources are a good first introduction to the subject with simple mathematics.

For further study on using the \gls{Lagrangian} in physics applications, I would recommend first reading \autoref{chap:lagrange continued}. This section is inspired greatly by the wonderful books by Susskind, \cite{susskind_general_2025,susskind_special_2019,susskind_quantum_2014,susskind_theoretical_2014}.

A great book for further study (and one I have read with much scrutiny in constructing these chapters), is \cite{goldstein_classical_2008}. This book is most definitely a graduate level treatment of the subject, but is extremely thorough and goes deep into many topics which have not been discussed here for the sake of brevity. Much knowledge is expected of the reader, so I find this book best for further study only after consulting some other sources first.

There are an abundance of video sources for classical mechanics. A good conceptual video overview of this topic can be found at \cite{veritasium_closest_2024}. This video contains little math, but is quite good for understanding the reasoning behind the formulation. I can personally recommend the video series at \cite{ross_lagranges_nodate}, which covers this material in a more suitable manner to engineering contexts and is quite easy to follow along with.

\section{Practice Problems}
\begin{exercise}[Generalized Coordinates]
Arthur keeps finding bugs on the apples that he is feeding his horse. Arthur heard that you have been learning about \glspl{generalized coordinate}. He wants a way to describe the position of the bugs on his apples using a minimal set of coordinates.

Find a suitable set of \glspl{generalized coordinate} to describe this system. Write out any equations of constraints of the system. \textit{Hint: An apple is topologically similar to a sphere!}

\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}[Brachistochrone]
You are a prominent mathematician in the year 1696. Bernoulli proposes a problem to you to find a curve from a point $(x_0,y_0)$ to another point $\left(x_f,y_f\right)$. When a particle is placed on this path, it should be a faster path than any other path the particle could take down the slope under the influence of only gravity.
%    \item brachistochrone
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Find the velocity as a function of the distance from the initial height, $x$, for the particle using energy conservation.
    \item Write a general expression for the amount of time it takes a particle to descend the path using an integral. \textit{Hint: you can write $t=\int\frac{ds}{v}$}.
    \item Use the Euler-Lagrange equation to solve for the form of this curve (You may find the integral form useful!).
    \end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}
\newpage
\begin{exercise}[Inverted Pendulum]\label{ex:inverted_pend}
Jonah wakes up one day and he is running late to his dynamics final! After brushing his teeth and grabbing all the essentials for his exam (including his copy of the \textit{Flight Dynamics Bible}), he heads to his exam.

In a rush, he hops on his Onewheel and starts riding to class. You can model Jonah as a mass $m$ on the end of a massless rod and his Onewheel (mass $M$) as a cart constrained to motion along the $\hat{x}$-axis. The angle of Jonah from the vertical is denoted as $\theta$. Derive the \gls{eom} of the system using the Lagrangian. The set-up is shown in \autoref{fig:Inverted Pendulum}.

Hint: You'll want to write the position vector first to make sure you don't miss any of the terms in the kinetic energy. Take the derivative of the position vector and square it to get the kinetic energy.
$$T=\frac{1}{2}m|\dot{r}|^2$$

\newpage
\begin{exercise}[Double Pendulum]\label{exer_doublepend}
Luckily,  Jonah was an avid reader of the Flight Dynamics Bible and aced his dynamics exam! On his way back home, he needs to grab a small model rocket for some aerodynamics testing. On the way, the parachute pops out of the nose and Jonah grabs it right before it hits the ground. Jonah is holding the rocket by the parachute and the rocket and parachute are swinging around.

The dynamics of the system can be modeled as a double pendulum with two rigid arms, both of length $L$ with masses $m$ at their ends, which are free to rotate in the $xy$-plane. 
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Find the \gls{eom} for the system as shown in \autoref{fig:Double Pendulum}.
    \item Simulate the system for the initial condition $\theta=\pi/2 \text{ rad}$ and $\phi=\pi-0.1 \text{ rad}$. Set $\dot{\theta}=0$ but $\dot{\phi}=0 \text{ rad/s}$. Create an animation of the output.
    \item If you are adventurous, create some small variation in the initial conditions (a normal distribution with $\sigma\approx0.01  \text{ rad}$ is a good starting point) and perform many simulations with the outputs plotted together.
\end{enumerate}

(360,215) -- (360,150) -- cycle ; \draw [color={rgb, 255:red, 208; green, 2; blue, 27 }  ,draw opacity=1 ][line width=0.75]    (406.85,195.05) .. controls (395.03,207.35) and (378.41,215) ..
