    \caption{Double Pendulum Set-Up}
    \label{fig:Double Pendulum}
\end{figure}
\end{exercise}

\newpage
\printendnotes

\chapter{Hamiltonian Mechanics}\label{chap:hamiltonian}

As we have seen, the \gls{Lagrangian} formulation was a large jump from Newton's laws. However, it still left a few conceptual questions. In more advanced physics and engineering applications, another approach will prove to have more use. This approach is called the \gls{Hamiltonian} approach. The \gls{Hamiltonian} formulation, like the \gls{Lagrangian}, does not introduce any `new' concepts, because it is simply an alternate approach to dynamics. In fact, most of the time we have to find the \gls{Lagrangian} to construct our \gls{Hamiltonian}!\endnote{Of course, many times we also just guess what the \gls{Hamiltonian} should look like, bypassing this step entirely.} So, the \gls{Hamiltonian} does not even make most problems simpler to solve. Why is it important, then?

The first useful feature of the \gls{Hamiltonian} lies in the fact that it is easier to interpret. The \gls{Lagrangian} has no physical meaning associated with it. $T-V$ doesn't describe any of attributes of the system we can point to and see. It is purely analytical. We \textit{need} the \gls{Lagrangian} to be $T-V$ for the formalism to work; there is no fundamental reason.

The \gls{Hamiltonian}, on the other hand, is constructed as $H=T+V$. This is something we can recognize! It's the total energy of the system. We take the conservation of total energy to be a fact of physics, so it's a good definition to have in our formulation. This has one unique advantage: there is no such thing as the conservation of the \gls{Lagrangian}, but the conservation of the \gls{Hamiltonian} is a fundamental law of physics.\endnote{However, this does not mean the \gls{Hamiltonian} is always conserved.} Energy is simply changing form and that attribute allows us to understand the trajectory of the system. Therefore, we often think about this description as more `fundamental' than the \gls{Lagrangian}. 

More important for our applications is that the \gls{Hamiltonian} finds extensive use in the domain of optimal control! Here, we will outline the basics of \gls{Hamiltonian} mechanics and the relationship to optimal control will be explored in \autoref{part:modern control}. As a note, the \gls{Hamiltonian} approach is also used deeply in quantum mechanics and special relativity.\footnote{We will see more information on this in \autoref{chap:lagrange continued}.} We owe much of modern physics to the \gls{Hamiltonian}, perhaps even more so than the \gls{Lagrangian}.

Finally, there is a nice geometrical interpretation that the \gls{Hamiltonian} can give us. We will dive into the geometric interpretations later, and dive into them especially deep when we discuss symplectic manifolds and structure later. Such a geometry will allow us to develop efficient algorithms for the numerical integration of such systems. A good preview of the content to come in this chapter can be found in the wonderful article \cite{hirvonen_hamiltonian_nodate}.

\section{Derivation of the Hamiltonian}

After doing a few problems with the \gls{Lagrangian} approach to mechanics, we notice that the $\frac{d}{dt}\left(\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}^i}\right)$ term always results in a second order derivative. If we want to solve this differential equation, then we need to describe both the $q  ^i$'s and the $\dot{q}^i$'s. The physical manifestation is that we need to know the initial position and velocity in order to integrate our acceleration \gls{eom} and get a particular solution to the ODE. While we treat $q$ and $\dot{q}$ as independent in the Euler-Lagrange equations, they are obviously related. As a result, such a system will have $n$ independent variables. In the \gls{Hamiltonian}, we will completely decouple them and arrive at a state which has $2n$ independent variables that describe the full system motion.

The \gls{Lagrangian} formulation is conceptually unsatisfying in some ways. Consider that we may only observe the state of a system at one point in time. We may know all of the values of $q^i$ by looking at the system (known as the \textit{configuration space}). To determine its derivative, however, we would need to look at some $dt$ in the future to determine the evolution of those $q^i$'s -- and thus the $\dot{q}^i$'s. This time difference is infinitesimal, but it still means that we cannot determine the state of the system \textit{precisely} from an observation at a single time. To solve this, we want to introduce something into our formulation that is more fundamental than $\dot{q}^i$. Instead of a generalized velocity, we would like something like a generalized momentum. If you wonder why the momentum would be more general, consider that $F=ma$ may be written more precisely as $F=\frac{d\vec{p}}{dt}$. Knowing velocity alone does not give us direct information about the forces, but knowing the momentum as a whole \textit{does}.

There is yet another way to think about this. If we think about Newton's 2nd law mathematically, knowing the position and velocity does not give us a unique solution to the equation! We still require the mass to be defined for a particular solution to the equation. Given the momentum, we have all required quantities to understand the trajectory of a particle through space. For that reason, physicists generally consider momentum to be a more fundamental quantity than velocity.

With all that being said, we would like to develop a mathematical strategy which can alleviate these issues. What if we could transform one of these coordinates $\dot{q}^i$ so that it does not require a derivative? Doing so, we would resolve all of the issues demonstrated above. Luckily, we can do this! We'll use something called the \textit{Legendre Transform} to do so.\endnote{Interestingly, the Legendre Transform also finds use in thermodynamics, where it can be used to convert an internal energy into an enthalpy.}

\subsection{The Legendre Transform}
The Legendre transform is the key mathematical tool that we will need in order to derive the \gls{Hamiltonian}. The Legendre transform is conceptually very simple -- it changes the variables we are using in our equation while still maintaining equivalence.

The way the Legendre transform accomplishes this is as follows -- it takes information about the derivative and turns that into the value of a function. This property is precisely what allows us to go from a generalized velocity into a momentum.

It turns out that the derivative of a function encodes a lot of information about the function. In fact, given just the derivative of a function, we can fully reconstruct the original function through integration up to a constant offset. This is exactly what we do with numerical integration schemes, which you have read about in Volume I. The only thing we are missing is some initial condition.

This is the key understanding that we need for the Legendre transform. We have some original function $f$ -- which in our case will be the \gls{Lagrangian} -- which we can transform by considering its derivative as the new input:
$$f\Rightarrow f^*\left(\frac{df}{dx}\right)$$
Of course, we also need to describe the initial condition. The Legendre transform does this for every point, by considering where the tangent line, with slope $\frac{df}{dx}$, intersects the origin. This guarantees that every single point has both a defined slope, as well as a defined starting point. To encode the origin intersection point, we take this to be the output of function $f^*$, so that 
$$f^*\left(\frac{df}{dx}\right)=-b$$
Where $b$ is the $y$-intercept we are intimately familiar with from algebra. Of course, we want to express $-b$ in terms of $\frac{df}{dx}$, that's what makes a functional expression useful! Here is where a geometric construction may help us. This is shown in \autoref{fig:Legendre}.

\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering


\tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

\begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-1,xscale=1]
%uncomment if require: \path (0,300); %set diagram left start at 0, and has height of 300

%Straight Lines [id:da01484601527940943] 
\draw [color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,draw opacity=1 ] [dash pattern={on 4.5pt off 4.5pt}]  (302,90) -- (302,130) ;
%Shape: Axis 2D [id:dp9936162466939741] 
\draw  (170,130.59) -- (350,130.59)(259.99,40) -- (259.99,220) (343,125.59) -- (350,130.59) -- (343,135.59) (254.99,47) -- (259.99,40) -- (264.99,47)  ;
%Shape: Polynomial [id:dp0478973045554304] 
\draw  [color={rgb, 255:red, 218; green, 44; blue, 0 }  ,draw opacity=1 ] (170.81,180) .. controls (220.54,-4.8) and (270.27,224.8) .. (320,40) ;
%Straight Lines [id:da9433398230086687] 
\draw    (260,184) -- (302,90) ;
\draw [shift={(302,90)}, rotate = 294.08] [color={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.75]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 2.01, y radius= 2.01]   ;
\draw [shift={(260,184)}, rotate = 294.08] [color={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][fill={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ][line width=0.75]      (0, 0) circle [x radius= 2.01, y radius= 2.01]   ;

% Text Node
\draw (241,173.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]    {$b$};
% Text Node
\draw (311,104.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [font=\small,color={rgb, 255:red, 74; green, 144; blue, 226 }  ,opacity=1 ]  {$f( x)$};
% Text Node
\draw (279,144.4) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]  [font=\small,color={rgb, 255:red, 0; green, 0; blue, 0 }  ,opacity=1 ]  {$\frac{df}{dx}$};


\end{tikzpicture}
    \caption{Geometric construction of Legendre Transform}
    \label{fig:Legendre}
\end{figure}
We can see that taking $\frac{df}{dx}$ and multiplying by the distance $x$, we get from point $b$ to $f(x)$. We can represent this as $\frac{df}{dx}x=f(x)-b$.

We know that $f^*\left(\frac{df}{dx}\right)=-b$, so we can rearrange to solve for this in the equation above to get:
\begin{gather}\label{eq:legendre}
    \color{boxBlue}\boxed{\color{black}f^*\left(\frac{df}{dx}\right)=\frac{df}{dx}x-f(x)}\\
    \color{boxBlue}\text{Legendre Transform}
\end{gather}
The application of the Legendre transform to mechanics is simple. We simply exchange $f(x)$ for $L$ and take our variable $x$ to be $\dot{q}$. Before we perform this translation, let's spend a bit more time discussing the implications of momentum.

\section{What is Momentum?}
Now that we are equipped with the Legendre transform, we are ready to tackle the \gls{Hamiltonian}. To do so, we will need to investigate the \textit{generalized momentum} more deeply. We investigated it in terms of the \gls{Lagrangian}, but its presence in \gls{Hamiltonian} mechanics is more significant.

Of course, many of us are familiar with the concept of momentum from introductory physics classes, or even higher level classes. Typically, we define the momentum to be $mv$. However, this concept only entails linear momentum! We also have angular momentum, $r\times mv$.

\newglossaryentry{gen momentum}
{
    name=generalized momentum,
    first = {\textit{generalized momentum}},
    plural = {generalized momenta},
    description={A generalized coordinate that is defined as $\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}_i}.$ Generalized momentum is mostly used in Hamiltonian mechanics}
}

Furthermore, if you are familiar with quantum mechanics, you likely know that the momentum operator can also be represented as $-i\hbar\frac{\partial}{\partial x}$. We know that the momentum of a relativistic particle is also different, given by $\gamma m_0v$. You may have also heard that photons have momentum. It seems that our notion of momentum of $mv$ is in dire need of a new, more general formulation. It is okay if you've never seen these representations of momentum before. They are here simply to show that momentum is a concept that is very broad.\endnote{In fact, our generalized momentum does not even need the same units as a typical momentum. Consider, for example, angular momentum, which has units of $\mathrm{kg\cdot m^2\ s^{-1}}$ while typical momentum is $\mathrm{kg\cdot m\ s^{-1}}$}

What we hope to accomplish is to capture all of these in a single formula for momentum. As a result, we should define a \textit{generalized momentum}, analogous to what we have done for generalized positions and velocities. Of course, this notion of the \gls{gen momentum} is more flexible than the ordinary momentum. It's best to not think of the generalized momentum as something that \textit{must} equal $mv$ or $r\times mv$ or whatever else. Instead, the generalized momentum is some general attribute of our system that just so happens to have these properties when we are on a physical trajectory. This is a bit of an abstract concept, but important to understand to see why Hamiltonian mechanics is more `intellectually complete' than the \gls{Lagrangian} formulation.\footnote{See chapter 4 of \cite{carroll_biggest_2022} for an in depth discussion of this idea}. But briefly, the \gls{gen momentum} was an emergent quantity in the \gls{Lagrangian}. In the \gls{Hamiltonian}, it is a fundamental coordinate.

To begin our mathematical discussion of the \gls{gen momentum}, we can consider the example of a free particle -- that is, a particle acted upon by no potentials or forces. This will allow us to quickly remember how we were able to understand momentum in terms of the \gls{Lagrangian}:

\begin{example}[The Free Particle]
    The free particle has one of the simplest possible \glspl{Lagrangian}. Since there are no potentials involved, we can fully describe the \gls{Lagrangian} of the free particle with $L=T$. Since the object is a point mass particle, the \gls{Lagrangian} is thus $$L=\frac{1}{2}m\dot{x}^2$$
We can use the Euler-Lagrange equation here to find the equation of motion quite easily. The first term, $\frac{\partial L}{\partial x}$ goes to zero since there is no dependence on $x$. This leaves only the $\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{x}}=0$ term, which must be a constant. Upon evaluation, we have $m\dot{x}=C$, which means that the linear momentum, $m\dot{x}$, is conserved because it is constant.
\end{example}
As we recall, $\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{x}}$ is what we define to be the \gls{gen momentum}, as we had defined in the \gls{Lagrangian}. We can formalize this for any generalized velocity, as:
\begin{gather}
    \color{boxBlue}\boxed{\color{black}p_j=\frac{\partial L}{\partial\dot{q}^j}}\\
    \color{boxBlue}\text{Generalized Momentum Conjugate}
\end{gather}
The key distinction here from when we defined the generalized momentum for the \gls{Lagrangian} is that we think about this as a coordinate on a certain space, not as something that is a result of the \gls{Lagrangian}. We can think of a particle which possesses a position (a location in configuration space) and a momentum. Together, the particle lives in a \textit{phase space} consisting of the position and momentum. This phase space has nicer geometric properties than the \gls{Lagrangian}. We also notice that the generalized momentum is a covariant vector. Because it is `row vector-like', we often say that is it \textit{conjugate} to the position in the system.\endnote{This becomes especially important in quantum mechanics. Because position and momentum are conjugate to each other, they are related by the Fourier transform in quantum mechanics. This is the exact relation that leads to the Heisenberg uncertainty principle. The mathematical relation of conjugate quantities first appears in classical settings!}

This definition of \gls{gen momentum} is much more powerful than the standard definition that we use. This definition, in fact, provides an answer for all of the problems that we encountered before. By defining the momentum as something inherent to the configuration space, we no longer need some $dt$ to discover the velocity. If we understand this idea deeply, we are able to solve problems in quantum mechanics, special relativity, and of course, classical mechanics! 

As a final note, we can see the presence of a derivative in this definition goes hand in hand with the previously discussed Legendre transform. This property is what will allow us to transform from the \gls{Lagrangian} perspective into the \gls{Hamiltonian} one.

\subsection{From the Lagrangian to the Hamiltonian}
With the \gls{gen momentum} defined, we are now ready to describe the \gls{Hamiltonian} in full. We will start with the \gls{Lagrangian}, which we can describe very broadly as a function of $q$ and $\dot{q}$:
$$L(\bm{q},\bm{\dot{q}})$$
Using the Legendre transform, we can transform $\bm{\dot{q}}$ into a new variable. We replace $f$ with $L$ and $x$ with $\bm{\dot{q}}$ in \eqautoref{eq:legendre} to get the following:
$$L^*\left(\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}^j}\right)=\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}^j}\dot{q}^j-L(\bm{q},\bm{\dot{q}})$$
We can recognize that the term $\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}}$ is the \gls{gen momentum} that we have just described. We can simplify the equation by calling this term $p_j$. We will also denote $L^*$ as $\mathcal{H}$:
$$\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p})=p_j\dot{q}^j-L(\bm{q},\bm{\dot{q}})$$
As we have done in the previous chapter, the combination of the covariant and contravariant indices implies a summation between the generalized momentum and the generalized velocity. In the standard notation, we would include the sum over $j$ in the \gls{Hamiltonian}:\endnote{From this point on, I will assume the reader is familiar with the Einstein summation notation. The summation will be implied by the presence of covariant and contravariant quantities.}
\begin{gather}
\color{boxBlue}\boxed{\color{black}\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p})=\sum_j p_j\dot{q}_j-L}\\
\color{boxBlue}\text{Hamiltonian}
\end{gather}
We can notice that the expression for the \gls{Hamiltonian} still has $\dot{q}_j$ in it, so we still need an expression for the generalized velocities. However, we no longer need to take the derivative with respect to this term, so we have reduced the order of the differential equation. By the guarantees of the Legendre transformation, we have also made sure that all of the relevant information in the system is still present!

\section{The Hamiltonian as Total Energy}
In the introduction to this section, I mentioned that the \gls{Hamiltonian} has an interpretation as the total energy of the system. Remember that we define the \gls{Hamiltonian} as:
$$\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p})=p_j\dot{q}^j-L(\bm{q},\bm{\dot{q}})$$
And the generalized momentum is the partial derivative of the \gls{Lagrangian} with respect to the generalized velocity. Since our potential only depends on the generalized positions (and not their velocities), we could rewrite the equation above as:
$$\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p})=\frac{\partial T}{\partial \dot{q}^j}\dot{q}^j-(T-V)$$
From here, we simply need to reduce the summation term.
\begin{exercise}
Show that the term $\frac{\partial T}{\partial \dot{q}^j}\dot{q}^j$ reduces to $2T$. Start with the cartesian system, and then prove the result in general (Euler’s Homogeneous Function Theorem will help).
\end{exercise}
After proving that result, we find that our \gls{Hamiltonian} will be:
$$\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p})=2T-(T-V)$$
Which is in fact $\mathcal{H}=T+V$. The result is this: \textbf{when the \gls{Lagrangian} has no explicit time dependence, the system conserves energy.}\endnote{So long as the coordinates themselves are not time-dependent.}

This leads us to an interesting question. Since we know that conservation of energy is a fundamental law, where does energy go when our \gls{Lagrangian} is time varying? The answer is simple: we need to consider a larger system in order to see the conservation of energy. If the \gls{Lagrangian} is time varying, energy is being dissipated into some other form. Examples are common in electrodynamics. Particles often change direction or slow down in a way that seems to violate conservation of energy for the particles. In such cases, the energy is going into the electric or magnetic field itself. 

This comparison is also apt when we consider an aircraft or rocket. These systems have very obvious time dependence and apparent losses of energy. The mass of the rocket changes over time, and air drag slows the rocket down. Ultimately, we can reconcile these losses if we are to consider a system which includes all of the surrounding air and exhaust gasses. Such an analysis doesn't make practical sense, so we live with time varying \glspl{Lagrangian} and accept the fact that the apparent energy in our systems change. 

\section{Hamilton's Equations of Motion}
When we create a \gls{Lagrangian}, we can apply the Euler-Lagrange equations to derive \glspl{eom}. In the case of \gls{Hamiltonian} mechanics, we can derive a similar relationship for the equations of motion. The same machinery of the calculus of variations can be used to derive these relationships.\endnote{See \autoref{proof:hamilton} in the Appendix for this derivation.} Since we have already derived the \gls{Lagrangian} from a variational standpoint, it will prove a bit easier to derive Hamilton's equations of motion directly from the Legendre transformation relating the two. However, a variational argument is also valid, and is shown in \autoref{proof:hamilton} in the Appendix. We start with the definition of the \gls{Hamiltonian} as given by the Legendre transform:
$$\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p},t)=p_j\dot{q}^j-L(\bm{q},\bm{\dot{q}},t)$$
For this derivation, I will consider each dimension one at a time (each $j$ individually). The final result will be the same, but will allow us to drop the indices and boldface. Let's now take the total derivative of both sides of this equation:
$$\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial q}dq+\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial p}dp+\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial t}dt=p d\dot{q}+\dot{q}dp-\frac{\partial L}{\partial q}dq-\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{q}}d\dot{q}-\frac{\partial L}{\partial t}dt$$

Now, we substitute the Euler-Lagrange equation for $\frac{\partial L}{\partial q}$ and also substitute in the generalized momentum. This will simplify to a term in $\dot{p}$:
$$\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial q}dq+\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial p}dp+\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial t}dt=p d\dot{q}+\dot{q}dp-\dot{p}dq-pd\dot{q}-\frac{\partial L}{\partial t}dt$$
At this point, we simply associate each component, $dq$,$dp$, etc., with each other. This gives us the following relationships:
\begin{gather}
\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial q}=-\dot{p},\quad \frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial p}=\dot{q}\\
\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial t}=-\frac{\partial L}{\partial t},\quad\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial \dot{q}}=0
\end{gather}
The last two equations have already been discussed. They say that the \gls{Hamiltonian} is constant when the \gls{Lagrangian} has no explicit time variance and that the \gls{Hamiltonian} gets rid of the notion of the generalized velocity in favor of the generalized momentum. The first two equations are the important ones. Reestablishing that these can have $j$ different terms, we arrive at two first order differential equations for the \gls{eom} with the \gls{Hamiltonian}.

\begin{gather}
\color{boxBlue}\boxed{
\begin{array}{c}\color{black}
     \dot{q}^j=\dfrac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial p_j}\\\\
     \color{black}
  \dot{p}_j=-\dfrac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial q_j}
\end{array}
}\\
\color{boxBlue}\mathrm{Hamiltonian \ EOM's}
\end{gather}

\section{Why the Hamiltonian?}
So far, the \gls{Hamiltonian} is certainly \textit{different} from the \gls{Lagrangian}, but doesn't seem to be better in any way. In the following section, I will demonstrate that it does indeed have some advantages over the \gls{Lagrangian} that might make its use more important outside of the aforementioned use in optimal control.
\begin{enumerate}
    \item The differential equations are autonomous.
    \item The total derivative of the \gls{Hamiltonian} with respect to time is the same as the partial with respect to time.
    \item Ignorable/cyclic coordinates have a cleaner interpretation.
\end{enumerate}

Here, we will touch on the first and the third point. The second is easily derived and is left as an exercise to the reader.

\subsubsection{Autonomous Form of Hamilton's Equations of Motion}
As we have seen in Volume I, the reduction of a second order differential equation to a first order one is not an impressive feat. We could do that for any $n^\text{th}$ order differential equation. The big advantage of Hamilton's equations is not just that they are first order, but that the first order equations are autonomous differential equations.\endnote{A differential equation that is only a function of the dependent variable, not the independent variable.} 

We can perform this reduction by being smart about how we group Hamilton's equations of motion. First we will notice that the \gls{Hamiltonian} is a function of $p$ and $q$. We can then write:
$$\bm{\dot{q}}=\bm{f}(\bm{q},\bm{p})$$
Here a boldface is used to indicate that the quantities are vectors. The function $\bm{f}$ written out explicitly is $\frac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial p_j}$. We can do the same for the other equation:
$$-\bm{\dot{p}}=\bm{g}(\bm{q},\bm{p})$$
It is evident that if we introduce a state variable which contains both $\bm{q}$ and $\bm{p}$, which we call $\bm{x}$, we can capture the whole system in a single equation:
$$\bm{x}=(\bm{q},\bm{p}),\quad \bm{\dot{x}}=\bm{h}(\bm{x})$$
The final equation is an autonomous equation. The advantage of an autonomous equation is that there are many mathematical results surrounding such equations. They are well studied and generally have nicer solutions than the second order equations of the \gls{Lagrangian}.

\subsubsection{Ignorable Coordinates with the Hamiltonian}
Whenever we find that a coordinate is ignorable using the \gls{Lagrangian}, we will also find that it is ignorable using the \gls{Hamiltonian}. That in itself is no different and we should not expect it to be. 

The simplification comes from the fact that the position and momentum are more natural than the position and velocity of the system. Consider that we have a system of two particles:
$$\mathcal{H}(q^1,q^2,p_1,p_2)$$
Let's suppose that $q^2$ is a cyclic coordinate. This means that $p_2$ is automatically going to be a constant:
$$-\dot{p}_2=\dfrac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial q^2}=\text{const.}$$
The other equation of motion is obvious since $\dot{q_2}$ does not appear. The \gls{Hamiltonian} is then very simple:
$$\mathcal{H}(q^1,p_1,c)$$
Here $c$ is some constant value. We have effectively reduced the computation to a single degree of freedom problem with a constant condition $c$. In the \gls{Lagrangian} approach, this cannot be done:
$$L(q^1,q^2,\dot{q}^1,\dot{q}^2)$$
Even if we identify $q_2$ as a constant, it is not necessarily true that $\dot{q}_2$ is a constant -- only that the generalized momentum is a constant. As a result, the \gls{Lagrangian} cannot be reduced in the same way. We must still compute:
$$L(q^1,\dot{q}^1,\dot{q}^2)$$
This is one very obvious advantage of the \gls{Hamiltonian}. It seems that the relation between position and momenta is stronger than the relation between position and velocity. This fundemental relationship is very real, and it is one that we will seek to quantify in the following sections.

\section{Hamiltonian Flow: An Analogy with Fluid Dynamics}
Many aerospace and mechanical engineers have seen fluid dynamics and studied it fairly extensively. As it turns out, there is a beautiful relationship between \gls{Hamiltonian} systems and a fluid dynamics analogy. This relationship is unique to the phase space representation, and not something we find with the \gls{Lagrangian}.

Let's remember the idea of an incompressible flow which we mentioned in Volume I. In such a flow, the divergence at every point in the velocity field is zero:
$$\nabla\cdot\bm{v}=0$$
Remember that such an expression really means that:
$$\frac{\partial v^x}{\partial x}+\frac{\partial v^y}{\partial y}+\frac{\partial v^z}{\partial z}=0$$
Very surprisingly, a collection of particles in phase space will also be an incompressible flow! Because phase space is not physical, it is somewhat difficult to get an intuitive feel for this amazing fact.\endnote{Unless we were to consider the implications in quantum mechanics, in which Liouville's theorem can prove the conservation of quantum states. But it is best to not convolute our discussion here with this fact. For further information, \textit{quantum incompressibility} is a good search term.} The result is very simple to show mathematically, though. Remember that we were able to cast Hamilton's equations into an autonomous differential equation:
$$\bm{\dot{x}}=\bm{h}(\bm{x})=\left(\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial p},-\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial q}\right)$$
Since the phase space is $2n$-dimensional, $\bm{x}$ can be viewed as the velocity field of a two dimensional fluid. We can easily check that it is incompressible:
$$\nabla\cdot\bm{\dot{x}}=\frac{\partial \dot{q}}{\partial q}+\frac{\partial \dot{p}}{\partial p}=\frac{\partial}{\partial q}\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial p}-\frac{\partial}{\partial p}\frac{\partial\mathcal{H}}{\partial q}$$
As we know, the order in which we do a partial derivative does not matter. Thus, this expression is equivalently:
$$\nabla\cdot\bm{\dot{x}}=\frac{\partial^2\mathcal{H}}{\partial p\partial q}-\frac{\partial^2\mathcal{H}}{\partial p\partial q}=0$$
This result is known as \textit{Liouville's Theorem}\footnote{Joseph Liouville, 19th century French mathematician and physicist.}. The fact that the phase space flow is incompressible is a very interesting fact. The statement of Liouville's theorem is also very general. It holds true even when the Hamiltonian is time varying and forces are non-conservative. It only relies on the fact that Hamilton's equations are true. 

\subsubsection{Implications of Liouville's Thoerem}

The generality of Liouville's theorem leads us to some interesting ideas. One of them is that if our system is chaotic, like the double pendulum explored in \autoref{exer_doublepend}, the phase space evolution is very complex. It will fold on itself and split into many different branches, but the total volume will still remain constant and the density at each point will remain the same. A visualization of this may be seen in \cite{maciej_matyka_phase_2021}.

Another consequence of Louiville's theorem has to do with the study of statistical mechanics (roughly akin to thermodynamics). When studying a large collection of particles which evolve under the same dynamics, the density at each point must remain the same and the total volume must remain constant. This is equivalent to saying that the information about the state of the system is never lost. This \textit{conservation of information} is another fundamental conserved quantity in physics that is very important in the study of statistical and quantum mechanics. \endnote{I will leave it to the reader to consider the implications of this idea in the context of Noether's theorem. It is highly related to ideas about unitarity in quantum physics.} 
The idea that the dynamics of such a thermodynamic system conserve information and volume seems to go against the notion of entropy. However, the notion of entropy and the incompressibility of phase space actually play together quite nicely. We can imagine a system where all elements of the phase space start in a compact area of the phase space with some initial position and velocity. Over time, the natural dynamics of the system will cause the phase space to scatter in many different directions and cause various filaments to emerge. After some time, the total phase space volume remains the same, but particles exist in many portions of the phase space rather than being closely bunched near the initial point. Entropy is a natural consequence of Liouville's theorem in macroscopic chaotic systems.

The implications for 6-\gls{dof} modeling are not quite as profound, but they are important. The fact that Hamilton's equations form an incompressible flow tells us something important: that the equations of motion are deterministic in nature. The implication is that for whatever dynamics we encounter with a rocket, the underlying physics are truly deterministic and evolve in a known manner. While we may not always be able to compute exact solutions to these physics, it is a fact that the equations of motion are deterministic (up to the level of precision we are worried about in classical dynamics).\endnote{Even in quantum mechanics, the governing laws are indeed deterministic. It is only in observing these systems that counter-intuitive and seemingly non-deterministic behaviors emerge.}

One of our final steps in building our foundation of classical mechanics will allow us to tie together our many ideas. The fact that the \gls{Hamiltonian} evolves as an incompressible fluid signals that there is some underlying geometric structure to phase space. To begin the discussion of that structure, we will need to study the \textit{symplectic geometry} of \gls{Hamiltonian} systems.

 \section{Symplectic Geometry}

Symplectic geometry is the natural geometry of the equations of motion in \gls{Hamiltonian} mechanics. To see how this structure emerges, let's once again consider the autonomous form of Hamilton's equations:
$$\bm{\dot{x}}=\bm{h}(\bm{x}),\quad\bm{x}=(\bm{q,p})$$
 We have already identified each individual component of this equation:
\begin{gather}
\bm{\dot{q}}=\bm{f}(\bm{q},\bm{p})=\dfrac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial \bm{p}}\\\\
-\bm{\dot{p}}=\bm{g}(\bm{q},\bm{p})=\dfrac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial \bm{q}}
\end{gather}
We can then write the equation as a vector equation in $\bm{p}$ and $\bm{q}$:
$$\begin{bmatrix}
\bm{\dot{q}}\\\bm{\dot{p}}
\end{bmatrix}=\begin{bmatrix}
\frac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial \bm{p}}\\[3pt]
-\frac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial \bm{q}}
\end{bmatrix}$$
From here, we can pull out a matrix term to make both of the partial derivatives positive. Doing so will let us write out the RHS term as a gradient term multiplied by some matrix. Because the gradient also has flipped the arrangement of the partial derivatives, the resulting matrix will be off-diagonal (skew):
$$\underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}
\bm{\dot{q}}\\\bm{\dot{p}}
\end{bmatrix}}_{\bm{\dot{x}}}=\underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}
    0&I\\-I&0
\end{bmatrix}}_J\underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}
\frac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial \bm{q}}\\[3pt]
\frac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial \bm{p}}
\end{bmatrix}}_{\nabla_{\bm{x}}\mathcal{H}}$$
Here, the matrix $J$ is of size $2n\times2n$, where $\bm{p}$ and $\bm{q}$ are both of size $n$. We may write this equation more simply as:
$$\bm{\dot{x}}=J\nabla_{\bm{x}}\mathcal{H}$$
The matrix $J$ is called a \textit{symplectic} matrix. This matrix structure encodes the geometry of the equations of motion. All of the nice features of the \gls{Hamiltonian} seen so far (and to be seen in the future) are neatly expressed in this equation. The symplectic matrices have interesting properties which are explored in the next exercise.
\begin{exercise}
Prove that the matrix $J$ has the following properties:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item $J^2=-I$
    \item $\det(J)=1$
    \item $JJ^T=I$
\end{enumerate}
\end{exercise}

The last definition that we need is that a matrix $P$ will be symplectic if it satisfies the following relationship:
$$P^TJP=J$$
\subsubsection{Louiville's Theorem Revisited}
The symplectic matrix really describes the evolution of phase space quantities. It tells us that the flow of these differential equations is generated by a sort of `rotation' of the Hamiltonian. This structure is very apt for exploring geometric properties. As one example, we can easily understand Louiville's theorem in this framework. One clue is that the determinant of $J$ is 1. This means that the transformation $J$ is indeed volume preserving at each point in space. We can check the divergence of $\bm{\dot{x}}$ as well:
$$\nabla_{\bm{x}}\cdot\bm{\dot{x}}=J\nabla_{\bm{x}}\mathcal{H}$$
\todo{finish dis shit}

\subsubsection{Quadratic Hamiltonians}
Let's imagine a system which is quadratic in the position and momenta. Such a system can be represented as:
$$\mathcal{H}=\frac{1}{2}\bm{x}^TM\bm{x}$$
Here, $M$ is a weighting matrix on the terms of $\bm{x}$. It allows us to construct a generic quadratic \gls{Hamiltonian}. Let's compute the gradient for this expression:
$$\nabla_{\bm{x}}\mathcal{H}=\frac{1}{2}\frac{\partial}{\partial \bm{x}}\left(\bm{x}^TM\bm{x}\right)$$
This gives us the following:
$$\nabla_{\bm{x}}\mathcal{H}=M\bm{x}$$
If we remember the relation that $\bm{\dot{x}}=J\nabla_{\bm{x}}\mathcal{H}$, we call multiply both sides of the equation by $J$ on the left and arrive at the following:
$$\bm{\dot{x}}=JM\bm{x}$$
This matrix equation is interesting. We will see this linear structure very often when we study control systems. The solution of such a matrix equation is very simple (it is analogous to $\dot{x}=ax$ in the scalar case):
$$\bm{x}(t)=e^{JMt}\bm{x}_0$$
In the case of the quadratic \gls{Hamiltonian}, the solution is simple and we may often have closed forms for the solution. For this reason, we will often desire that the \gls{Hamiltonian} for our system is quadratic. Under this assumption, we can find closed form solutions. This will be of surmount importance in our study of optimal control and estimation, where we see quadratic systems very often.

\begin{exercise}
Show that the matrix $e^{JMt}$ is symplectic.
\end{exercise}


Often, we care about tracking how certain \textit{functions} change in a symplectic geometry. Just like the transformation of the coordinates themselves, there is a nice structural tool which will allow us to do this. To begin the discussion of that structure, it will be wise to reformulate \gls{Hamiltonian} dynamics in terms of \textit{Poisson brackets}.

\section{Poisson Brackets}
Poisson\footnote{Sim\'eon Denis Poisson, 19th century mathematician and physicist.} brackets are not only a nice way of simplifying the notation of Hamiltonian dynamics, but will have an explicit relationship to the geometry of dynamical systems. Here, we will just provide an overview of how the Poisson bracket works, and how we may rewrite some of our equations using it. Later, we will explore its geometric meaning more deeply.

The basic idea is to consider quantities which are functions of some variables in the state space. For example, the kinetic energy of the system is a function of the momentum, typically given as:
$$T(\bm{p})=\frac{\bm{p}^T\bm{p}}{2m}$$
As we often see, potential functions depend on the locations $q$. We can also imagine general functions of both position and momentum. These general functions will be called $F(q,p)$. We can compute the time derivative of an arbitrary function $F$. The following expression can be generalized to our standard $j$ number of positions and momenta with a sum over $j$ on the RHS of the equation.
$$\dot{F}=\frac{\partial F}{\partial {q}}\dot{q}+\frac{\partial F}{\partial p}\dot{p}$$
It should be fairly obvious that we can use Hamilton's equations of motion for $\dot{q}$ and $\dot{p}$ to make some further progress here:
$$\dot{F}=\frac{\partial F}{\partial {q}}\frac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial {p}}-\frac{\partial F}{\partial p}\frac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial {q}}$$
This type of structure where we do a subtraction like $AB-BA$ are special. We call them \textit{commutators} and they will become important later. Poisson noticed this type of structure occurred very often. He gave it a special notation called a Poisson bracket:
$$\{F,G\}=\frac{\partial F}{\partial {q}}\frac{\partial G}{\partial {p}}-\frac{\partial F}{\partial p}\frac{\partial G}{\partial {q}}$$
We will later see that the Poisson bracket has the same properties as a Lie bracket. For our purposes now, it will suffice to say that the Poisson bracket has the following properties:
\begin{itemize}
    \item It is antisymmetric: $\{F,G\}=-\{G,F\}$
    \item It is a linear operator: $\{cF,G\}=c\{F,G\}$
    \item It obeys the Leibniz rule: $\{AB,C\}=B\{A,C\}+A\{B,C\}$
\end{itemize}

We can apply this notation in practice to see its immediate use. Consider the following statement:
$$\dot{q}=\{q,\mathcal{H}\}$$
This is really an expression of one of Hamilton's equations of motion. Let's expand it out to see that this is in fact true:
$$\dot{q}=\frac{\partial q}{\partial {q}}\frac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial {p}}-\frac{\partial q}{\partial p}\frac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial {q}}$$
As we are now physicists in training, we can consider derivatives as no different than fractions (much to the chagrin of mathematicians). This produces an immediate reduction of the first term. The second term will reduce to zero because $q$ is completely independent of $p$ in phase space:
$$\dot{q}=\frac{\partial \mathcal{H}}{\partial {p}}$$
\begin{exercise}[Hamilton's Equation]
Show that $\dot{p}=\{p,\mathcal{H}\}$ produces the correct equation of motion.
\end{exercise}
\begin{exercise}[Poisson Bracket Derivatives]
Show that the following is true$$\{F(q,p),p_i\}=\frac{\partial F(q,p)}{\partial q^i}$$
It will also be useful to try and show what the converse of this statement results in:
$$\{F(q,p),q^i\}=\ ?$$
\end{exercise}

\begin{exercise}[Poisson Bracket Properties]
Now, consider a system with $j$ terms, $q^j$ and $p_j$. Show the following are true:
\begin{itemize}
    \item $\{q^i,q^j\}=0\quad i\ne j$
    \item $\{p_i,p_j\}=0\quad i\ne j$
    \item $\{q^i,p_j\}=\delta_{ij}$
\end{itemize}
\end{exercise}

The Poisson bracket proves useful for a few other things. For a function $F$ that has no explicit time dependence, $F$ will be a constant of motion when the Poisson bracket between $F$ and $\mathcal{H}$ vanishes:
$$\{F,\mathcal{H}\}=0\Leftrightarrow F\text{ is a conserved quantity}$$
\subsubsection{Rigid Body Dynamics with Poisson Brackets}
The Poisson bracket is also very useful for rigid body dynamics and understanding rotations. A brief study of rotational motion using Poisson brackets will serve as a nice introduction to rotational dynamics in general.

\todo{Rigid Body. Goldstein Ch. 9.6 and 9.7}

\section{Canonical Transformations}
Canonical transformations have an importance that is two-fold for our purposes. First, they are one of the important tools for understanding the application of \gls{Hamiltonian} mechanics to physical systems, such as orbits. Second, they will set us up to move towards the ideas of optimal control. The idea of a canonical transformation is to perform a `change of variables' which makes our problem simpler to solve, then convert back if we desire.
\begin{figure}[ht]
    \centering

\tikzset{every picture/.style={line width=0.75pt}} %set default line width to 0.75pt        

\begin{tikzpicture}[x=0.75pt,y=0.75pt,yscale=-1,xscale=1]
%uncomment if require: \path (0,300); %set diagram left start at 0, and has height of 300

% Text Node
\draw (194,72) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]   [align=left] {hard!};
% Text Node
\draw (192,162) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]   [align=left] {easy!};
% Text Node
\draw (264,172) node [anchor=north west][inner sep=0.75pt]   [align=left] {EOM};

\end{tikzpicture}

    \caption{Canonical Transformation}
    \label{fig:canonical}
\end{figure}
A canonical transformation is more powerful than a transformation of coordinates in the \gls{Lagrangian}. In \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics, we often transformed or chose variables to make a problem simpler. Those types of transformations are called \textit{point transformations}. While these types of transformations are useful, the \gls{Hamiltonian} formulation sees further benefits from transformations. Part of the reason for this is that cyclic coordinates are more natural in the \gls{Hamiltonian} formulation. As we saw previously, when a coordinate is cyclic, the generalized velocity is not necessarily a constant of motion. The associated momentum, however, is. Because of this, it is simple to find solutions to Hamilton's equations when the coordinates are cyclic. Supposing that some $q^i$ is cyclic, the associated momentum is a constant $\alpha_i$. Therefore, $\dot{q}^i$ is another constant $\beta_i$ and the solution of the system is trivial:
$$q_i=\beta_it+C$$
Here, $C$ is some constant dictated by the initial configuration of the system. The goal of canonical transformations is often to simplify our system so the dynamics can be expressed in this trivial form. These canonical transformations also preserve phase space geometry, so important properties like incompressible phase space flow are preserved. Additionally, we require that the Poisson bracket remains the same under such a transformation. For this reason, we have to simultaneously change both the position and momentum coordinates in order to perform a canonical transformation.

\subsubsection{The Transformation Equations}
In order to perform a canonical transformation of our \gls{Hamiltonian}: $\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p})\rightarrow\mathcal{H}'(\bm{Q},\bm{P})$, we need a mathematical description that ensures we maintain the necessary properties of the system.\endnote{Some authors call the transformed \gls{Hamiltonian} $K$. Goldstein jokingly refers to this as the `Kamiltonian'. As long as we are clear that $\mathcal{H}'$ is not a derivative of $\mathcal{H}$, this new notation is certainly better.} 

We remember from \gls{Lagrangian} mechanics that two systems will have identical equations of motion if the \gls{Lagrangian} is the same up to a gradient term. Thus, we can perform the inverse Legendre transform to get an equivalence between our original coordinates and new coordinates. Remember that this transformation looks like:
$$L=p_i\dot{q}^i-\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p},t)$$
Now we can set the two \glspl{Lagrangian} equal to each other, remembering that we can add the additional gradient:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:canonical transform}
p_i\dot{q}^i-\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p},t)=P_i\dot{Q}^i-\mathcal{H}'(\bm{Q},\bm{P},t)+\frac{dF}{dt}
\end{equation}

Technically, we can also have a simple scaling factor on the coordinates too. A constant scaling between the coordinates will still preserve the required properties. In that case, we have
$$\lambda \left(p_i\dot{q}^i-\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p},t)\right)=P_i\dot{Q}^i-\mathcal{H}'(\bm{Q},\bm{P},t)+\frac{dF}{dt}$$
Most of the time, we consider the case where $\lambda=1$ since the scaling transformation is fairly trivial. For the rest of the section, I will consider this case where $\lambda=1$. Notice that in our expression that the term $F$ is responsible for whatever changes must occur in the system. If $\frac{dF}{dt}=0$, the two systems must be identical. Because $F$ `generates' the canonical transformation, we call it the \textit{generating function} of the transformation.

In most texts, there are four main types of generating functions that are cited. Here, we will cover how these generating functions work, but will not spend too much time on actually using them. We are about to discover a tool which is very powerful for performing these canonical transformations which allows us to be less studious here. That being said, it's good to see how they work. The first type of generating function that we consider is one which is a function of the generalized positions and time. We call these types of transformations $F_1$ by convention:
$$F_1=F_1(\bm{q},\bm{Q},t)$$
To see what the transformation of our variables will look like under this generating function, we simply plug in to \eqautoref{eq:canonical transform}.
$$p_i\dot{q}^i-\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p},t)=P_i\dot{Q}^i-\mathcal{H}'(\bm{Q},\bm{P},t)+\frac{\partial F_1}{\partial q}\dot{q}+\frac{\partial F_1}{\partial Q}\dot{Q}+\frac{\partial F_1}{\partial t}$$
Matching terms in $p$ and $P$, we find the following:
\begin{gather}
p_j=\frac{\partial F_1}{\partial q^j}\\
P_j=-\frac{\partial F_1}{\partial Q^j}\\
\mathcal{H}'=\mathcal{H}+\frac{\partial F_1}{\partial t}
\end{gather}
 \begin{exercise}[Generating Function]
Do a similar transformation for the generating function $F=F_2(\bm{q},\bm{P},t)-Q^iP_i$ (the Legendre transform of $F_1$). Show that this generating function gives:
\begin{gather}
p_j=\frac{\partial F_2}{\partial q^j},\quad Q^j=\frac{\partial F_2}{\partial P_j}, \quad \mathcal{H}'=\mathcal{H}+\frac{\partial F_2}{\partial t}
\end{gather}
 \end{exercise}

There exist other generating functions, the solutions to which can either be derived or looked up. Typically we want to use the generating functions in order to make the variables of our system simpler. If we were able to come up with a methodology to transform the \gls{Hamiltonian} of the system into whatever we want, there is one obvious candidate: to make the new \gls{Hamiltonian} equal to zero. In such a case, our equations of motion are extraordinarily simple. The methodology that we use to do this is the \textit{Hamilton-Jacobi} equation.

\section{Hamilton-Jacobi Equations}
The Hamilton-Jacobi equations allow us to transform some system via a canonical transformation into one where the Hamiltonian is zero. As we have seen, these types of systems are useful because our system will have constants of motion and the evolution in phase space is trivial. Mathematically, the criteria is:
$$\mathcal{H}(\bm{q},\bm{p},t)+\frac{\partial F}{\partial t}=0$$
It will turn out that the most useful form of $F$ is to consider the old generalized positions $(\bm{q})$ and the new momenta $(\bm{P})$ as the inputs to the function $F$. These are exactly the inputs that we had for the function $F_2$. Remember that this canonical transformation looked like:
\begin{gather}
p_j=\frac{\partial F_2}{\partial q^j},\quad Q^j=\frac{\partial F_2}{\partial P_j}, \quad \mathcal{H}'=\mathcal{H}+\frac{\partial F_2}{\partial t}
\end{gather}
With a transformation for the momentum defined, we can now state the \gls{Hamiltonian} in terms of this transformed coordinate:
$$\mathcal{H}\left(\bm{q},\frac{\partial F_2}{\partial \bm{q}}\right)+\frac{\partial F_2}{\partial t}=0$$
This equation that we have just written is the Hamilton-Jacobi equation. The standard notation is to relabel $F_2\equiv S$ when we use it in this context ($S$ here is the action). Also, remember that our transformed momenta are going to be constants of integration when this equation is satisfied. As a result, we can write $S$ as a function of the positions and the constants of integration, which we will call $\alpha$:\endnote{In general, the solutions of such an equation may have time varying coefficients. But there are classes of solutions which are constant. Classification of partial differential equation solutions is outside the scope of this book.}
$$S=S(\bm{q},\bm{\alpha},t)$$
Thus, the final form of the Hamilton-Jacobi equations is:
\begin{gather}
\color{boxBlue}\boxed{\color{black}\mathcal{H}\left(\bm{q},\frac{\partial S(\bm{q},\bm{\alpha},t)}{\partial \bm{q}}\right)+\frac{\partial S(\bm{q},\bm{\alpha},t)}{\partial t}=0}\\
\color{boxBlue}\text{Hamilton-Jacobi Equations}
\end{gather}
Before we move on, let's highlight one important thing in our canonical transformation. The momenta are easily expressed in terms of the action, $S$. They are simply:
$$p_j=\frac{\partial S(\bm{q},\bm{\alpha},t)}{\partial q^j}$$
Under this interpretation, the generalized momenta can be thought of as a measure of how sensitive the action is to changes in the generalized coordinates. Intuitively, the way in which the phase space resists a change in dynamical flow is exactly what the momentum describes. 

The Hamilton-Jacobi equations are not always analytically solvable, but when they are, they yield a closed form solution for the system. When the system is quadratic, we have exact solutions to these partial differential equations. For that reason, we'll often require that our functions are quadratic in form. We'll see this very often in control theory.

\subsubsection{Astrodynamics and Delaunay variables}
 A practical application of the canonical transformation is to describe the motion of orbits in terms of Delaunay variables. These are a type of \textit{action-angle} variable for the system in which 5 of the 6 elements are constants. Clearly this is an improvement compared to the standard equations for an orbit where none of the elements are guaranteed to be constant!

 \todo{finish up}

 
\section{Looking Ahead}
This chapter and the previous ones have introduced lots of content. It is no small feat to reformulate all of classical mechanics (twice)! As we proceed to control theory, there will be a few important points that we should remember and will use to great effect in the future. Most of these theoretical ideas came from our work with the \gls{Hamiltonian}. Even if you are unconvinced by the practical use of the \gls{Hamiltonian}, the list below should be enough to convince us that our study here was useful in setting the field for our future work.
\begin{itemize}
    \item The Poisson bracket describes the geometry and evolution of classical systems. It also tells us about how large scale motions of our system are generated.
    \item The Hamilton-Jacobi equations give us a new grounding for exploring system dynamics from the point of view of fields. This perspective will be the paradigmatic shift required for optimal control.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{Final Summary}
With that, we have finished our brief survey of classical mechanics. Classical physics is a huge realm, and further areas such as chaos theory, astrodynamics, scattering, and geometric mechanics exist (just to name a few). Hopefully these few chapters serve as a good jumping off point for study into such topics. Indeed, our work here will suffice in setting us up nicely for the ideas present in control theory.

A table with a brief summary of the ideas throughout these chapters is included below. Knowing when to apply each tool in mechanics is a very important skill, and the table can help make the choice more clear. Newtonian dynamics are indeed very useful for simple simulation frameworks and for including complex dynamical effects like air resistance. As we have seen, a huge variety of problems in control theory and robotics lend themselves to representations with the \gls{Lagrangian}. With the inclusion of generalized forces, we can reproduce almost any Newtonian system. However, we do lose some of the theoretical advantages in these cases. Finally, \gls{Hamiltonian} mechanics presents the most foundational structure and becomes very useful in extending our theories into more complex areas. It gives us very nice theoretical knowledge of how general systems evolve, but is somewhat less practical for flight dynamics applications. However, its theoretical use will pay dividends in control theory.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Comparison of Mechanics Frameworks}
\label{tab:mechanics_comparison}
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{X|X|X}
\toprule
\textbf{Newtonian} & \textbf{Lagrangian} & \textbf{Hamiltonian} \\ \midrule

\multicolumn{3}{l}{\textbf{Variables}} \\ \addlinespace
($\bm{r}, \bm{v}$)&($\bm{q}, \bm{\dot{q}}$)&($\bm{q}, \bm{p}$) \\ \midrule

\multicolumn{3}{l}{\textbf{Space}} \\ \addlinespace
Physical 3D Space & $n$-dimensional Configuration Space & $2n$-dimension Phase Space \\ \midrule

\multicolumn{3}{l}{\textbf{Structure}} \\ \addlinespace
Vector, 2nd-order ($\bm{F}=m\bm{a}$) & Scalar, $n$ 2nd-order ODEs & Scalar, $2n$ 1st-order ODEs \\ \midrule

\multicolumn{3}{l}{\textbf{Geometry}} \\ \addlinespace
Non-preservative & Variational & Symplectic \\ \midrule

\multicolumn{3}{l}{\textbf{Use Case}} \\ \addlinespace
Simple dynamics \& simulation & Complex dynamics and constraints & Optimization and theoretical ideas \\ \bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{table}

\subsubsection{Further Reading}
Understanding the principles of Hamiltonian mechanics deeply requires significant development that requires further study. There are a few sources which I highly recommend for further study of these ideas. In my opinion, the best source is \cite{arnold_mathematical_1989}. This text does a deep dive on the mathematical foundations of both \gls{Lagrangian} and \gls{Hamiltonian} mechanics while also remaining surprisingly accessible. Do not be dissuaded by the fact that this book is a `graduate text in mathematics', I have found it surprisingly readable for someone in the target audience of this book.

% \chapter{Introductory Astrodynamics}
% If we loosen our definition of rocket modeling \textit{slightly}, orbital mechanics becomes a very important part of our discussion. The \gls{Lagrangian} and \gls{Hamiltonian} description of mechanics are instrumental to orbital applications. Because many of the applications of our previously mentioned dynamics are in the realm of spacecraft dynamics, I feel it is a necessity to include information on orbits. 

% Additionally, we will find that energy mechanics has extensive uses here, as space is the perfect playground for our 'no frictional forces or drag' that we often use for energy mechanics. This chapter will essentially be an extension of the discussion of spacecraft in Volume II, with a specific focus placed on spacecraft applications. Of course, to anyone who has played Kerbal Space Program, some parts of this section may feel quite familiar. We will solidify that intuition with the mathematical tools needed in our engineering systems.

% \section{Orbital Mechanics Introduction}
% There is no better place to start with orbital mechanics than the humble 2-D conic orbit. These orbits are simple to describe and derive from elementary physics, but are extraordinarily powerful. We will keep utilizing these orbits far into our study of orbital mechanics!

% \section{2-D Conic Orbits}
% 2-D Conic orbits are the simplest orbits that we can describe which model the physical behaviors of a gravitational system. In a conic orbit, we consider only a single force acting on the body, gravity. We express this force with Newton's famous law of Universal Gravitation:
% $$\ddot{r}=-\frac{GM}{r^2}\hat{e}_r$$
% There are many sources which proceed to compute many equations of orbital mechanics using Newtonian mechanics and other laws, such as the fact that angular momentum must be conserved. Most of these approaches end up using energy relations at some point during their derivation, so we may as well start there!

% \subsection{The Lagrangian Approach}

% Because these Newtonian derivations are abundant, I will instead proceed from the \gls{Lagrangian} point of view, which will give us many insights, and all of the results will come directly from the mathematical architecture.

% We know that gravitational forces are a conservative force. That is -- the change in energy is only dependent on the distance from the gravitational field. This makes the \gls{Lagrangian} description very convenient. As is typical, we define the \gls{Lagrangian} of the system to be $L=T-V$.

% In a two dimensional orbit, there are two ways we can move: tangentially or radially. In the tangential direction, this velocity is simply $\dot{r}\hat{e}_r$. In the radial direction, this velocity is $r\dot{\theta
% }\hat{e}_{\theta}$. Thus, the total velocity is simply the addition of these two orthogonal components:
% $\vec{v}=\dot{r}\hat{e}_r+r\dot{\theta}\hat{e}_\theta$ (you can easily show this with use of the \gls{bke}). From here it is trivial to find the specific kinetic energy, $\frac{1}{2}\vec{v}^2$.

% We can describe the potential as a function of the radius only. Since the potential is conservative, it is known that $F=-\nabla U$ (The force derives from a potential, as a physicist may say). We can of course reverse this statement to find an expression for $U$.

% This equation reduces quite readily in this scenario. Since the force is only a function of the radial component, we have reduced the equation to $$F(r)=-\frac{\partial U}{\partial r}$$
% This can easily be integrated to attain an expression for $U$. We first fully expand $F(r)$ and isolate $\partial U$:
% $$-\frac{GM}{r^2}\partial r=-\partial U$$
% Since both sides are single-variate, the partial derivatives reduce to ordinary total derivative. We then integrate to attain the following:
% $$\int\frac{GM}{r^2} dr=\int dU$$
% Upon evaluation, we have the following:
% $$\frac{-GM}{r}=U+C$$
% It is customary to assume that the potential goes to zero at an infinite distance. We can apply this condition to find that $C=0$, which leaves us with the gravitational potential:
% $$U(r)=\frac{-GM}{r}$$
% We now have all of the components necessary to construct our \gls{Lagrangian}, which gives us
% $$L=\frac{1}{2}\left[\dot{r}^2+\left(r\dot{\theta}\right)^2\right]+\frac{GM}{r}$$
% Now we can start evaluating the \gls{Lagrangian} to find the equations of motion!

% We start with the equation for $\theta$, which gives us:
% $$\frac{\partial L}{\partial \theta}-\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{\theta}}=0$$
% We notice that no terms contain $\theta$, so our \gls{eom} for this direction reduces to
% $$\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{\theta}}=0$$
% We can remove all the $\dot{r}^2$ and $\frac{GM}{r}$ terms via the linearity of the derivative since these have no dependence on $\dot{\theta}$. This leaves us with the following upon evaluation of the partial derivative:
% $$\frac{d}{dt}\left(r^2\dot{\theta}\right)=0$$
% Since this derivative is zero, $r^2\dot{\theta}$ must be a constant. This $r^2\dot{\theta}$ is in fact our (specific) angular momentum, $r\times v$! So we have recovered fully mathematically that the angular momentum of the orbit must be conserved. We will define this specific angular momentum $h$.

% Let us then move on to the second equation, which is 
% $$\frac{\partial L}{\partial r}-\frac{d}{dt}\frac{\partial L}{\partial \dot{r}}=0$$
% This requires a bit more computation, but is still straightforward. We can proceed with the computation to get
% $$\ddot{r}=r\dot{\theta}^2-\frac{GM}{r^2}$$
% This is the equation of motion for the orbit! From here, we can numerically integrate this equation to get any orbit we want. However, this is a case where we can find an exact analytic solution.

% \subsection{The Analytical Solution of the Orbit}
% Central force problem Goldstein

% \subsection{The Euler Angles}

% \section{Orbit Transfers}

% \section{Numerical Simulation of Orbits}
% \subsection{Basic N-Body simulations}
% \subsection{CR3BP}
% \subsection{Lambert Equation}
% \subsection{Trajectory Optimization}
% \subsection{Gravity Assists and Multiple Transfers}
